Third Proof: God's Names Are Not Merely Human Conventions
Baqillani argues from the pre-existence of God's names and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consensus of the ummah proves that God's names and attributes are real, existing prior to creation, and are not merely human descriptions.

Core Idea
God's names and attributes exist before any human speaks them.

This lesson presents Baqillani's third proof, which draws on the community's consensus to establish that divine names and attributes are not mere human inventions. Instead, they are eternal realities inherent to God, independent of creation. The argument uses a conditional to expose the absurdity of the opposite view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Baqillani begins with the consensus and then presents a conditional argument.
The passage is concise; every word is important.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Find the 'if... then...' structure. Understand what would follow if God's names were merely human speech.
The conditional reveals the logical consequence of the opposing view.
3
Reflect on the consensus as evidence
Consider why the ummah's rejection of that consequence serves as proof. How does consensus function as a source of evidence in kalam?
Consensus (ijma) is a primary source of Islamic law and theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على ذلك أيضاً إجماع الأمة على أن الله تعالى أسماء وصفاتٍ قبل أن يخلق خلقه. ولو كانت أسماء الله وصفاته هي أقوال عباده وتسميتهم وأوصافهم له، لكان الله تعالى قبل خلقه لعباده وخلق غير مُسمّى ولا موصوف ولا ذي اسم. وفي منع الأمة لذلك دليل على أن الوصف والتسمية غير الاسم والصفة التي يكون الموصوف المسمَّى بهما مسمّى موصوفاً.
And among the evidence for that also is the consensus of the community that God Most High has names and attributes before He created His creation. And if the names of God and His attributes were the utterances of His servants and their naming and their descriptions of Him, then God Most High before His creation of His servants and creation would be unnamed, undescribed, and without a name. And in the community's rejection of that is evidence that the description and naming are other than the name and the attribute by which the described and named is named and described.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This third proof uses the consensus that God had names and attributes before creation. If names and attributes were merely human speech, God would have been nameless and attributeless before humans spoke. The ummah rejects this, proving that divine names and attributes are ontologically prior to human speech. Therefore, human speech (al-qawl) is not identical to the attribute (al-ṣifa); the description (al-waṣf) and naming (al-tasmiya) are separate from the actual name and attribute.

Key Terms
أسماء الله
صفات الله
قبل خلق الخلق
التسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's names and attributes are merely human conventions or descriptions.
Correction: The passage argues that divine names and attributes are real and exist prior to any human speech, based on consensus.

Checkpoint
According to this proof, if God's names were only human utterances, what would follow?
A) God would be unnamed before creation
B) God would have no attributes
C) Both A and B
D) Nothing, as it is not relevant

Key Takeaway
The consensus of the ummah proves that God's names and attributes are eternal realities, not mere human inventions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Ar-Rahman', do you recognize that this attribute of mercy exists in God eternally, even if you had never spoken it? How does that awareness change the way you call upon Him in your du'a?
?
If God's names were merely human inventions, your worship would be directed at something defined by you. But they are real, objective realities. Does your prayer reflect that you are approaching the Eternal, or are you just repeating words? What would change if you truly internalized this truth today?
?
The ummah rejects that God could ever be nameless. Yet do you sometimes act as if God is defined only by your limited understanding? How can you make the belief in His eternal names transform your awe and humility before Him in your next prostration?

Argument from Consensus: Names Are Not Descriptions
Baqillani argues from ijmāʿ that God had names and attributes before creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the consensus of the Ummah to prove that Allah's names and attributes are distinct from the human act of describing Him.

Core Idea
Names and attributes are real, not just human descriptions.

This passage presents a concise proof from consensus (ijmāʿ). Baqillani shows that if names were merely descriptions, then before creation Allah would have no names—contradicting the Ummah's consensus. Thus, the act of describing is different from the attribute itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then its English translation. Let the reasoning unfold naturally.
Notice that the argument is built around a conditional: if...then.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Find the phrase 'if the names...were the sayings of His servants...then...' and trace the consequence. Recognize that this is a reductio ad absurdum.
The Ummah's consensus prevents the absurd conclusion.
3
Grasp the core distinction
Understand that the act of describing (waṣf/tasmiya) is not the same as the attribute/name (ṣifa/ism) itself. The consensus proves that attributes are real and pre-exist creation.
This distinction is crucial in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على ذلك أيضاً إجماع الأمة على أن الله تعالى أسماء وصفاتٍ قبل أن يخلق خلقه. ولو كانت أسماء الله وصفاته هي أقوال عباده وتسميتهم وأوصافهم له، لكان الله تعالى قبل خلقه لعباده وخلق غير مُسمّى ولا موصوف ولا ذي اسم. وفي منع الأمة لذلك دليل على أن الوصف والتسمية غير الاسم والصفة التي يكون الموصوف المسمَّى بهما مسمّى موصوفاً.
And among what also indicates that is the consensus of the Ummah that Allah, exalted, has names and attributes before He created His creation. And if the names of Allah and His attributes were the sayings of His servants and their naming and their descriptions of Him, then Allah, exalted, before the creation of His servants [by Him] and creation would be unnamed and undescribed and without name. And in the Ummah's prohibition of that is evidence that description and naming are other than the name and the attribute by which the described and named one is named and described.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from consensus (ijmāʿ). The author begins by stating that the Ummah agrees that Allah had names and attributes before creating any creatures. Then he argues by conditional: if names and attributes were merely the sayings and descriptions of creatures, then before creatures existed, Allah would have had no name and no attribute—which is impossible. Since the Ummah rejects that (i.e., they affirm that Allah was always described by His names and attributes prior to creation), it proves that the act of naming and describing (al-waṣf wa al-tasmiyya) is different from the name and attribute (al-ism wa al-ṣifa) themselves. The name/attribute is a real entity in the described, not just the utterance of a describer.

Key Terms
إجماع
اسم
صفة
وصف
تسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that Allah's attributes are merely human descriptions or labels applied to Him after creation.
Correction: Baqillani shows that consensus affirms attributes pre-exist creation, and the act of describing is distinct from the attribute itself.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's argument from consensus, what would have been the case if names and attributes were merely the descriptions of creatures?
A) Allah would have names and attributes even without creatures.
B) Allah would have no names or attributes before creatures existed.
C) The Ummah would still affirm Allah's names before creation.
D) Descriptions would be identical to the names.

Key Takeaway
Allah's names and attributes are real and pre-exist creation; they are not merely human descriptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His name 'Ar-Rahman', do you truly believe that this attribute of mercy is real and eternally present in Him, even if you feel undeserving? How does this certainty affect your hope in moments of despair?
?
If you viewed Allah's attributes as merely human descriptions, how would that change your reliance on His forgiveness or power in your daily struggles? What does this passage demand of you in your most vulnerable moments?
?
Consider a time when you doubted that Allah's knowledge of your actions is real and independent of your own awareness. How does the argument from this passage challenge you to align your heart with the truth that His attributes are eternal and not dependent on your perception?

Refuting That Attributes Are Merely Descriptions
Baqillani refutes that attributes are descriptions by reductio ad absurdum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that attributes cannot be mere descriptions but must be real properties inherent in the essence.

Core Idea
If attributes were just words, contradictory statements would force impossible realities.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani presents a logical proof from reason to refute the view that divine attributes are nothing more than human descriptions. He shows that if attributes were merely statements, then conflicting statements would require an entity to possess contradictory qualities simultaneously—an absurdity. This establishes that attributes are objective realities inhering in the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: hypothesis, consequence, contradiction, conclusion.
2
Identify the hypothesis and its consequence
Baqillani starts by supposing that an attribute is just a description. Under that assumption, different people could give contradictory descriptions. What would follow?
He uses the example of Zayd being called alive by one and dead by another.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Since contradictory descriptions would force contradictory attributes to coexist—which is impossible—the initial assumption is false. What then is the true nature of an attribute?
He concludes that attributes exist in the essence itself, like movement in a moving body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على ذلك أيضاً من جهة المعنى أنه لو كانت الصفة في الحقيقة ليست بمعنى أكثر من وصف الواصف وقوله: «إن زيداً عالم قادر حي»، لوجب أن يكون لزيد في الحقيقة في حالة واحدة صفتان متضادّتان. ولوجب أن يكون حيّاً ميتاً وقادراً عاجزاً وعالماً جاهلاً، إذا قال بعض الواصفين له إنه حي، وقال آخر إنه ميت، وقال قائل إنه قادر عالم، وقال آخر إنه عاجز جاهل. فيجب أن لا يكون العلم من صفته أولى من الجهل لأنه قد وجدت له الصفتان؛ فيجب أن يكون زيد عالماً جاهلاً وحيّاً ميتاً لوجود صفتيه اللتين هما القول. فلما لم يجز ذلك، وكان العالم في الحقيقة هو من وجد به العلم ولوجوده به صار عالماً، بطل أن تكون الصفة هي القول، وثبت أنها ما يوجد بذات الموصوف، كما أن الحركة واللون هما ما وجدا بذات المتحرك والمتلوّن دون القول «إنه متحرك متلوّن».
And among what also indicates that from the perspective of meaning is that if the attribute in reality meant nothing more than the description of the describer and his saying 'Indeed Zayd is knowing, powerful, alive', then it would be necessary that Zayd in reality in one state have two contradictory attributes. And it would be necessary that he be alive and dead, powerful and powerless, knowing and ignorant, if some describers say he is alive, another says he is dead, another says he is powerful [and] knowing, and another says he is powerless [and] ignorant. Then it would be necessary that knowledge not be more entitled to be his attribute than ignorance, because both attributes have come to exist for him; thus Zayd would necessarily be both knowing and ignorant, alive and dead due to the existence of his two attributes which are the utterance. But since that is not permissible, and the knower in reality is the one with whom knowledge exists and by its existence he becomes knowing, it is invalid that the attribute be the utterance, and it is established that it is what exists in the essence of the described, just as movement and color are what exist in the essence of the moving and colored [thing], not the utterance 'he is moving, colored'.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage presents a reductio ad absurdum argument from reason (min jihat al-maʿnā). Baqillani supposes, for the sake of argument, that an attribute (ṣifa) is nothing more than a description (waṣf) — i.e., the speaker's statement. If that were the case, then if different speakers describe the same person Zayd with contradictory descriptions (e.g., one says 'alive', another says 'dead'), Zayd would actually have both attributes simultaneously in one state, which is impossible. Since knowledge and ignorance, life and death cannot coexist, the premise that attribute equals statement must be false. Instead, the true attribute is a real property inhering in the essence (dhāt) of the described, like movement in a moving body, independent of anyone's statement. The conclusion: the attribute is not the utterance but rather something that exists in the entity itself.

Key Terms
صفة
وصف
ذات
قول
تضاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Attributes are merely subjective descriptions that people make about a thing.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if that were true, contradictory descriptions would force impossible coexisting properties, so attributes must be objective realities inherent in the thing.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does Baqillani derive from the claim that an attribute is just a description?
A) A person could have contradictory attributes like being alive and dead at the same time.
B) Attributes cannot exist without a describer.
C) Zayd must be either alive or dead, not both.
D) Descriptions are always true.

Key Takeaway
Attributes are real properties that exist in the essence of the described, not mere statements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God’s attributes are real and exist in His essence, does your daily trust in His mercy and power reflect that reality, or do you treat them as mere labels? What would change if you prayed with the conviction that His knowledge is as real as your own existence?
?
Baqillani shows that contradictory descriptions cannot both be true. Are there areas in your life where you claim to believe one thing about God but your actions assume the opposite? How might this argument call you to align your beliefs and deeds without contradiction?
?
The proof forces you to consider: is your faith rooted in objective truth about God or in what people say about Him? If everything you believed about God were suddenly contradicted by everyone around you, would your faith stand on the reality of His attributes or collapse with their words?

Refuting the Consensus Argument: Derivation of 'Wasif'
Baqillani refutes the Mu'tazilite claim based on consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's linguistic rebuttal to the Mu'tazilite claim that the term 'Wasif' must be derived from creating an attribute.

Core Idea
Wasif is derived from describing (wasf), not from creating an attribute (sifa).

In this passage, Baqillani tackles a Mu'tazilite argument that uses consensus to claim that true attributes are only those by which a describer describes, not the created qualities themselves. He responds by questioning the linguistic derivation of the word 'Wasif', showing that it comes from the act of describing rather than from the act of creating an attribute. This refutation hinges on Arabic verbal patterns.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and first response
Read the complete passage including the opponents' claim and Baqillani's initial rebuttal. Notice how he first questions the derivation before providing the linguistic rule.
The last sentence is incomplete due to page cut-off.
2
Identify the key linguistic principle
Baqillani argues that 'Wasif' is derived from the verb 'wasafa' (to describe), not from the noun 'sifa' (attribute). He supports this with the rule that from 'fa'ala' we get 'fa'il', not from 'fa'ala al-sifata'.
This is a standard principle in Arabic morphology.
3
Understand how this refutes the consensus argument
The opponents claimed that since God is not called 'Wasif' when creating attributes, therefore attributes are not true descriptions. Baqillani shows that the term 'Wasif' is irrelevant to the act of creating attributes; it only applies to the act of describing.
4
Consider the incomplete ending
The passage cuts off mid-sentence. Reflect on what additional argument Baqillani might have made to further refute the opponents' position.
This is marked as needing review for completeness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: الدليل على أن المعاني الموجودة بالذات - العلوم والقدر والحركات - ليست بصفات في الحقيقة، وأن الصفة هي الواصف - إجماع الأمة على أن الله تعالى إذا قال: «إن الجسم عالم أسود متحرك» - فقد وصفه بهذا القول؛ وإذا خلق فيه العلم والقدرة والسواد والحركة، لم يكن واصفاً له عند أحد من الأمة. فيجب أن تكون الصفة ما يكون الواصف بها واصفاً دون ما لا يكون به كذلك.
يقال لهم: لم قلتم إن الاشتقاق الواجب من خلق الصفة «واصف»، وما دليلكم على ذلك؟ وما أنكرتم من أن لا يصح من فعل الصفة اشتقاق على وجه - لا «واصف» ولا «وصف» ولا «موصوف» ولا غير ذلك - وأن يكون قولنا «واصف» مشتقاً من الوصف دون الصفة؟ لأنهم يقولون: «وصف فهو واصف»، ولا يقولون: «فعل الصفة فهو واصف» - و«فَعَلَ» لا يجيء منه أكثر من «فاعل». وهذا يبطل ما قالوه.
ثم يقال لهم: ما أنكرتم من أن لا يجب ما قلتموه من وجه أنه قد صح وثبت من قولنا وقولكم أنه ليس الواجب أن يشتق الله تعالى [؟] (ناقص)
For if they say: The evidence that the meanings existing in the essences — knowledge, power, and movements — are not truly attributes (sifat), and that the attribute is the describer (al-wasif), is the consensus of the community that when God, exalted, says: 'The body is knowing, black, moving' — He has described it by this statement; but when He creates in it knowledge, power, blackness, and movement, He is not a describer (wasif) of it according to anyone of the community. Therefore it is necessary that the attribute be that by which the describer is a describer, not that which is not such.
It is said to them: Why do you say that the necessary derivation from creating the attribute (sifa) is 'wasif', and what is your proof for that? What prevents you from [accepting] that no derivation is valid from the act of creating the attribute in any way — neither 'wasif', nor 'wasf', nor 'mawsuf', nor anything else — and that our saying 'wasif' is derived from 'wasf' (description) rather than from 'sifa'? For they say: 'wasafa fahuwa wasif', and they do not say: 'fa'ala al-sifata fahuwa wasif' — and from 'fa'ala' no more than 'fa'il' comes. And this invalidates what they said.
Then it is said to them: What prevents you from [accepting] that what you have said is not necessary, from the perspective that it has been established from our statement and yours that it is not necessary that God, exalted, be derived [from creating attributes?] (incomplete).
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the opponents (likely the Mu'tazila) and Baqillani's rebuttal. The opponents argue based on consensus: when God says 'the body is black' He is a describer (wasif), but when He creates attributes like knowledge or blackness in the body, no one calls Him a describer. Therefore, the attribute (sifa) is only that by which the describer describes, not the created attribute itself. Baqillani responds by questioning the derivation: why must the word 'wasif' be derived from creating a sifa? It is possible that no derivation exists from that act, and 'wasif' comes from the verbal noun 'wasf' (description). He supports this with a linguistic rule: from the verb 'wasafa' we get 'wasif', but from 'fa'ala al-sifata' we get no such form. Thus their argument collapses. He adds a second rebuttal (incomplete due to page cut-off) that it is not necessary to derive a name for God from creating attributes.

Key Terms
صفة
واصف
اشتقاق
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'Wasif' (describer) must be derived from the act of creating an attribute (sifa).
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'Wasif' is derived from the act of describing (wasf), not from creating an attribute, as proven by Arabic verb patterns.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Baqillani's first rebuttal to the opponents' argument?
A) The consensus of the community is not reliable.
B) The word 'Wasif' is derived from 'wasf' (description), not from 'sifa' (attribute).
C) God is always a describer regardless of the situation.
D) The created attributes are indeed true descriptions of the object.

Key Takeaway
The term 'Wasif' is derived from the verbal noun 'wasf' (description), not from 'sifa' (attribute), undermining the Mu'tazilite argument.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been certain about a belief, only to realize later that your certainty was built on a flawed linguistic or logical foundation? How deeply are you willing to question the words you use to describe your faith?
?
The Mu'tazilites used consensus to support their view, yet they missed a simple linguistic point. Are there areas in your own understanding of God where you rely on 'what everyone says' instead of examining the precise language of revelation and reason?
?
This lesson shows how a single word derivation can unravel an entire theological argument. If the very words we use about God—'attribute', 'describer'—carry such weight, are you careful and intentional with how you speak of Him, knowing that words can mislead if not properly understood?

The Opponent's Derivation Argument and Its Refutation
Baqillani refutes an opponent's linguistic derivation argument against the reality of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the opponent's claim that attributes are not real by analyzing the linguistic derivation of the term 'wāṣif'.

Core Idea
The term 'wāṣif' comes from describing, not from creating attributes.

This lesson presents an opponent's argument that attributes (ṣifāt) are not real because the word for 'describer' (wāṣif) is derived from the act of describing, not from creating attributes. Baqillani responds by examining the Arabic derivation to show that the opponent's inference is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the opponent's argument
Read the opponent's argument carefully: they claim that the consensus of the ummah shows that describing by speech is what makes something an attribute, not creating inherent qualities.
Note the key claim: 'الصفة هي الواصف'.
2
Understand the derivation claim
The opponent argues that 'wāṣif' is derived from creating the attribute. Baqillani questions this: what is the evidence that the derivation goes this way?
Arabic derivation: 'waṣafa' gives 'wāṣif', not 'faʿala al-ṣifa'.
3
See Baqillani's refutation
Baqillani shows that 'wāṣif' comes from 'al-waṣf' (describing), not from 'al-ṣifa' (attribute). The verb 'faʿala al-ṣifa' does not exist. Thus the opponent's argument collapses.
The refutation is linguistic but has theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل لهم آخر من قول
فإن قالوا : الدليل على أن المعاني الموجودة بالذات العلوم والقدر والحركات ليست بصفات في الحقيقة، وأن الصفة هي الواصف إجماع الأمة على أن الله تعالى إذا قال : «إن الجسم عالم أسود متحرك» فقد وصفه بهذا القول؛ وإذا خلق فيه العلم والقدرة والسواد والحركة، لم يكن واصفاً له عند أحد من الأمة. فيجب أن تكون الصفة ما يكون الواصف بها واصفاً دون ما لا يكون به كذلك.
يقال لهم : لم قلتم إن الاشتقاق الواجب من خلق الصفة «واصف»، وما دليلكم على ذلك؟ وما أنكرتم من أن لا يصح من فعل الصفة اشتقاق على وجه - لا «واصف» ولا «وصف» ولا «موصوف» ولا غير ذلك - وأن يكون قولنا «واصف» مشتقاً من الوصف دون الصفة؟ لأنهم يقولون: «وصف فهو واصف»، ولا يقولون: «فَعَل الصفة فهو واصف». و«فَعَل» لا يجيء منه أكثر من «فاعل». وهذا يُبطل ما قالوه.
ثم يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم من أنه لا يجب ما قلتموه من وجه أنه قد صح وثبت من قولنا وقولكم أنه ليس الواجب أن يُشتق لله تعالى من كل ما خلقه في غيره اسم؛ لأن ذلك يوجب أن يُشتق له من فعل الإرادة في غيره «مريداً»، ومن فعل الأذى بدم الحيض وفساد الزرع «مؤذياً»، ومن فعل الهوس والجنون والطيش «مهوساً ومُجَنَّناً ومُطَيِّشاً ومفسداً»، وليس ذلك بقول لأحد. ولا يجب أن لا يُشتق له من جميع، لأن ذلك يوجب أن لا يُشتق له «العادل» من الإحسان والتفضل «محسناً متفضلاً» - وذلك خلاف الإجماع. فوجب أن يُشتق له من بعض ما خلق ولا يُشتق له من بعض. فلا يُحتَّم إذا لم يُشتق له من فعل العلم والسواد في غيره «واصفاً» أن لا يكون العلم صفة؛ كما لا يُحتَّم إذا لم يُشتق له من فعل الإرادة والأذى وفساد الزرع «مريداً ومؤذياً ومفسداً» أن لا يكون ما خلقه في غيره إرادةً وأذىً وفساداً. ولا جواب عن هذا. وبالله التوفيق.
A proof for them from another statement: For they said: The evidence that the meanings existing in the essence – knowledge, power, and movements – are not attributes in reality, and that the attribute is the descriptor, is the consensus of the ummah that God Almighty, when He says: 'The body is knowing, black, moving' – He has described it with this statement; and when He creates in it knowledge, power, blackness, and movement, He is not a descriptor of it according to anyone of the ummah. So it must be that the attribute is that by which the descriptor becomes a descriptor, not that by which he does not become such. It is said to them: Why did you claim that the necessary derivation from creating the attribute is 'wāṣif', and what is your evidence for that? And why do you deny that from the act of the attribute no derivation is valid at all – neither 'wāṣif' nor 'waṣf' nor 'mawṣūf' nor anything else – and that our saying 'wāṣif' is derived from 'al-waṣf' not from 'al-ṣifa'? Because they say: 'He described, so he is a describer (waṣafa fahuwa wāṣif)', and they do not say: 'He performed the attribute, so he is a describer (faʿala al-ṣifata fahuwa wāṣif)'. And the verb form 'faʿala' does not yield more than 'fāʿil'. This invalidates what they said. Then it is said to them: Why do you deny that what you claimed is not necessary from the aspect that it has been established and proven from our statement and your statement that it is not obligatory to derive for God Almighty a name from everything He creates in another; because that would necessitate that He be derived from the act of will in another as 'murīd', from the act of harm by menstrual blood and spoiling crops as 'mu'dhī', from the act of madness, insanity, and foolishness as 'muhawwis', 'mujannan', and 'muṭayyish' and 'mufsid', and that is not said by anyone. And it is not necessary that He not be derived from all, because that would necessitate that He not be derived as 'al-ʿādil' from beneficence and favor as 'muḥsin' and 'mutafaḍḍil' – and that is contrary to consensus. So it becomes obligatory that He be derived from some of what He creates and not derived from some. So it is not necessitated, if He is not derived from the act of knowledge and blackness in another as 'wāṣif', that knowledge is not an attribute; just as it is not necessitated, if He is not derived from the act of will, harm, and spoiling of crops as 'murīd', 'mu'dhī', and 'mufsid', that what He created in another is not will, harm, and corruption. And there is no answer to this. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a refutation (radd) of an objection raised by opponents who argue that attributes (ṣifāt) are not real in the sense of being independent entities inherent in a subject. Their evidence is based on linguistic derivation: they claim that the term 'wāṣif' (describer) is derived from the act of describing a subject with words, not from the act of creating attributes in that subject. For example, if God says 'the body is knowing, black, moving', He is describing it; but if He simply creates knowledge, power, etc. in the body, no one would say He is 'describing' it. Therefore, they conclude, the true attribute (ṣifa) is the act of describing, not the inherent quality. Baqillani responds with two arguments. First, he examines the derivation of 'wāṣif': the verb 'waṣafa' (to describe) yields 'wāṣif', but there is no verb 'faʿala al-ṣifa' (to perform the attribute) from which 'wāṣif' could be derived. Thus 'wāṣif' comes from 'al-waṣf' (the act of describing), not from 'al-ṣifa' (the attribute). Hence the opponents' inference that attributes must be the same as describing is invalid. Second, Baqillani argues by reductio ad absurdum: if we were required to derive names for God from every act He performs in others, we would have to call Him 'mu'dhī' (harmful) for causing menstrual blood and crop damage, and even 'muhawwis' (maddening) etc. — which no one accepts. Conversely, if we were forbidden to derive names from any act, then we could not call Him 'ʿādil' (just) from His justice or 'muḥsin' (beneficent) from His beneficence, which contradicts consensus. Therefore, the principle is selective: God may be named from some acts and not from others. Thus, the fact that He is not called 'wāṣif' from creating knowledge does not negate that knowledge is truly an attribute, just as not calling Him 'mu'dhī' from creating harm does not negate that harm is truly harm. The opponents have no answer to this. The passage concludes with 'And with God is success.'

Key Terms
صفة
وصف
واصف
اشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent believes that if 'wāṣif' is derived from describing, then attributes must be the act of describing, not real qualities.
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'wāṣif' is derived from 'waṣf' (describing) and not from 'ṣifa' (attribute), so the opponent's argument does not prove that attributes are unreal.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, from which word is 'wāṣif' derived?
A) From 'ṣifa' (attribute)
B) From 'waṣf' (describing)
C) From 'ism' (name)
D) From 'sima' (mark)

Key Takeaway
Linguistic derivation does not determine the ontological status of attributes; the term 'wāṣif' arises from describing, not from creating attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'All-Knowing' or 'All-Powerful', do you pause to consider that these names refer to real, inherent attributes in His essence, or do you treat them as mere descriptions? Will you commit to contemplating the reality behind each name you utter?
?
Think of a time you dismissed a truth because the language used to describe it seemed insufficient or contradictory. Are you willing to examine the actual meaning of words like 'attribute' and 'description' in your faith, rather than being misled by linguistic shortcuts?
?
If the linguistic derivation of a term does not determine reality, then what does? How can you ensure that your understanding of Allah's attributes is based on revelation and reason, not on human language limitations? Will you search for deeper knowledge beyond surface terms?

The Principle of Selective Naming for Divine Attributes
Baqillani uses reductio ad absurdum to prove that divine naming is selective, preserving the reality of attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that God's names are derived selectively from His actions, and that not deriving a name does not negate the reality of the underlying attribute.

Core Idea
God is not named from every act, but that does not deny the reality of His actions.

This second part of Baqillani's refutation strengthens his case by showing the absurd consequences of both extremes—deriving names from every act or none at all. By demonstrating that divine naming is selective, he proves that the absence of a specific name like 'wāṣif' does not negate the reality of the attribute of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the reductio argument
Baqillani presents a reductio ad absurdum: if we had to derive a name for God from every act He creates in others, we would have to call Him 'harmful', 'maddening', etc., which is absurd.
Notice the examples: menstrual blood, crop damage, madness.
2
Understand the alternative extreme
On the other hand, if we never derived names from His acts, we could not call Him 'Just' or 'Beneficent', which contradicts consensus.
This shows that the correct path is selective derivation.
3
Apply the principle to the attribute dispute
Since derivation is selective, the fact that God is not called 'wāṣif' from creating knowledge does not prove that knowledge is not an attribute, just as not calling Him 'harmful' does not negate the reality of harm He creates.
This completes the refutation.

Selective Derivation of Divine Names
Baqillani refutes an objection about deriving names for God from all actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that God is named from some of His actions but not all, and why failing to derive a name does not invalidate the attribute.

Core Idea
Not every divine action needs a name—only those consistent with His perfection.

Some theologians argued that since God creates all actions, He must be named from each one. But this leads to absurdities like calling God 'harmful' or 'manic'. Baqillani refutes this, showing that selective naming is the sound approach, and that not naming from an action does not negate the action's reality. Let's follow his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the flow of Baqillani's argument sink in.
2
Find the objector's stance
Identify what the objector assumes: that God must be named from every single action He creates in others. Notice the absurd consequences Baqillani lists.
Look for the hypothetical names like 'harmful', 'manic', etc.
3
Contrast with the opposite extreme
Baqillani also considers the opposite: never deriving names from any action. That would prevent calling God 'Just' from justice, which contradicts consensus.
Understand that both extremes are rejected.
4
Grasp the analogical conclusion
Baqillani argues that not deriving the name 'Describer' from creating knowledge does not mean knowledge is not an attribute. Similarly, not deriving 'willing' from creating will does not negate will as a created act.
This is the key rebuttal to the objection.
5
Reflect on the lesson
Ask yourself: Why is selective naming necessary? How does this protect God's perfection? The commentary will help clarify.
This connects to the core idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من كل ما خلقه في غيره اسماً. لأن ذلك يوجب أن يُشتق له مِن فِعْل الإرادة في غيره «مريداً»، ومن فعل الأذى بدم الحيض وفساد الزرع «مؤذياً»، ومن فعل الهوس والجنون والطيش مهوساً ومُجَيْناً ومُطَيّشاً ومفسداً، وليس ذلك بقول لأحد ولا يجب أن لا يُشتق له من جميع لأن ذلك يوجب أن لا يُشتق له العدل «عادل» من الإحسان والتفضّل «محسناً متفضّلا» - وذلك خلاف الإجماع. فوجب أن يُشتق له من بعض ما خلق ولا يُشتق له من بعض. فلا يجب، إذا لم يُشتق له من فعل العلم والسواد في غيره واصف، أن لا يكون العلم صفة؛ كما لا إذا لم يُشتق له من فعل الإرادة والأذى وفساد الزرع «مريد ومؤذ ومفسد»، أن لا يكون ما خلقه في غيره إرادةً وأذى وفساداً. ولا جواب عن ذلك وبالله التوفيق.
From everything He creates in others as a name. Because that necessitates that He be derived from the act of will in others as 'willing', and from the act of harm through menstrual blood and crop corruption as 'harmful', and from the act of mania, madness, and frivolity as 'manic', 'mad', and 'frivolous' and 'corrupting'—and that is not the saying of anyone, nor must He not be derived from all, because that would necessitate that He not be derived for justice as 'just' from beneficence and favor as 'beneficent, graciously favoring'—and that is contrary to consensus. So it is necessary that He be derived from some of what He creates and not derived from some. Thus it is not necessary, if He is not derived from the act of knowledge and blackness in others as 'describing', that knowledge not be an attribute; just as it is not necessary, if He is not derived from the act of will, harm, and crop corruption as 'willing', 'harmful', and 'corrupting', that what He creates in others not be will, harm, and corruption. There is no answer to that, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The objector apparently argued that if God is not named from every one of His actions in creation, then those actions do not truly belong to Him as attributes. Baqillani begins by listing hypothetical derivations: from will He would be called 'willing', from harm 'harmful', from mental disturbances 'manic', etc. He then states that no one actually claims God must be named from all such actions, and insisting on it would lead to absurd consequences like not calling Him 'Just' from justice or 'Beneficent' from beneficence—contrary to consensus. Therefore, God is named from some actions and not others. The key point: if we do not derive a name like 'Describer' from creating knowledge and blackness in others, that does not negate that knowledge is an attribute of God. Similarly, not deriving 'willing' from creating will in others does not negate that what He creates is will. The argument is analogical: non-naming does not imply non-existence. The conclusion is that there is no valid objection, and success is from Allah.

مشتق (derived)
فعل الإرادة (act of will)
أذى (harm)
عدل (justice)
إحسان (beneficence)
صفة (attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God is not directly named from a created attribute, that attribute is not truly from Him.
Correction: Non-derivation of a name does not negate the attribute; e.g., not naming God 'Describer' does not remove knowledge as an attribute.

Checkpoint
What would be the consequence, according to the objector's logic, if God were required to be named from every action?
A) He would be called 'Just' from justice.
B) He would not be called 'Just' from justice.
C) He would be called 'Willing' from will, but not 'Harmful'.
D) He would have no names.

Key Takeaway
God's names are derived from some of His actions, not all, and the absence of a name from an action does not negate that action's reality.

Division of God's Names into Essence and Attribute Names
Baqillani divides God's names into two categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani categorizes God's names into those that are identical to His essence and those that are attributes, and why attribute names are not other than Him.

Core Idea
God's essence and attribute names are not separate—they are Him.

Before we explore the specific attributes of God, we need a clear classification of His names. Baqillani begins by asking a fundamental question: How are God's names divided? His answer distinguishes between names that are identical to God's essence and those that are attributes subsisting in Him. This passage sets the conceptual framework for understanding divine attributes without falling into anthropomorphism or separation.

Guided Path
1
Read the question and answer
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the passage begins with a hypothetical question: 'On how many aspects are the names of God divided?' The answer is 'Into two types.'
2
Identify the two main types
The first type (al-darb al-awwal) are names that are identical to God's essence (huwa huwa). The second type (al-darb al-akhar) are names that are attributes subsisting in Him (as-sifa al-hasila lahu).
These two categories are the backbone of the classification.
3
Understand the first type: essence names
Baqillani lists examples: existing, thing, eternal, essence, one, etc. These are 'He Himself' because God's essence is not a meaning separate from Him. Proof shows He is these by Himself, not by an added attribute.
This avoids the idea that God's essence is composed of separate parts.
4
Understand the second type: attribute names
The second type is further subdivided into attributes of essence (sifat dhat) and attributes of action (sifat fi'l). For essence attributes, like 'Knowing' and 'Powerful', these are names of God but not said to be 'other than Him' because they cannot be separated from Him.
The passage ends while introducing this subdivision; it continues in the next lesson.
5
Reflect on the key distinction
Consider why this distinction matters: it safeguards tawhid by affirming that God's attributes are not separate entities, yet they are real and meaningful. Essence names are identical to God; attribute names are inseparable from Him.
This prevents both anthropomorphism (tashbih) and negation (ta'til).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: فإن قال قائل: فعلى كم وجه تنقسم أسماء الله تعالى؟ قيل له: على ضربين. فضرب منها هو هو تعالى، إذا كان اسماً عائداً إلى نفسه ككونه موجوداً وشيئاً وقديماً وذاتاً وواحداً وغيراً لما غايره وخلافاً لما خالف وأمثال ذلك من الأسماء الراجعة إلى ذاته تعالى. لأن ذاته ليست بمعنى سواه أو معنى لا يقال هو هو. وقد دلت الدلالة على أنه شيء موجود قديم وذات وواحد وغير لما غايره وخلاف لما خالفه لنفسه لا لمعنى. فوجب أن تكون هذه الأسماء هي الله تعالى. والضرب الآخر اسم هو الله تعالى وهو الصفة الحاصلة له. وهي على ضربين: إما أن تكون صفة ذات أو صفة فعل. فإن كانت صفة ذات كقولنا «عالم» الراجع إلى العلم، و «قادر» و «حي» وما جرى مجرى ذلك فهي أسماء له ولا يقال هي غيره لاستحالة مفارقتها له على بعض وجوه المفارقات الموجبة للغيرية وما
Issue: If someone says: 'On how many aspects are the names of God Most High divided?' It is said to him: 'Into two types. One type is He Himself, Most High, when it is a name referring to His essence, such as His being existing, a thing, eternal, essence, one, other than what is other than Him, and contrary to what is contrary to Him, and the like of those names that go back to His essence—because His essence is not a meaning other than Him or a meaning of which it is said 'He is He.' And proof has indicated that He is a thing, existing, eternal, essence, one, other than what is other than Him, and contrary to what is contrary to Him, by Himself, not by a meaning. So it is necessary that these names are God Most High. The other type is a name that is God Most High, and it is the attribute subsisting in Him. It is of two types: either it is an attribute of essence or an attribute of action. If it is an attribute of essence, like our saying 'Knowing,' which goes back to knowledge, and 'Powerful' and 'Living' and what runs in that course, then they are names for Him, and it is not said that they are other than Him, because their separation from Him is impossible in certain aspects of separations that necessitate otherness, and what...'
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the names of God Most High. It begins with a question: 'On how many aspects are the names of God divided?' The answer is: 'Into two types.' The first type (al-darb al-awwal) consists of names that are identical to God's essence (huwa huwa). These include names like 'existing' (mawjud), 'thing' (shay'), 'eternal' (qadim), 'essence' (dhat), 'one' (wahid), 'other than what is other than Him' (ghayr li-ma gharahu), and 'contrary to what is contrary to Him' (khilaf li-ma khalafahu). These are said to be 'Himself' because God's essence is not a meaning separate from Him—it is simply Him. Proof establishes that He is these things by Himself (li-nafsihi), not by an added attribute. Therefore, these names are none other than God Himself. The second type (al-darb al-akhar) consists of names that are God Most High but are the attribute subsisting in Him (as-sifa al-hasila lahu). This type is further subdivided into two categories: attributes of essence (sifat dhat) and attributes of action (sifat fi'l). The author then gives examples of attributes of essence: 'Knowing' (alim), which refers to knowledge (al-ilm), 'Powerful' (qadir), 'Living' (hayy), and similar terms. These are names of God, but they are not said to be 'other than Him' because their separation from Him is impossible—since they are essential attributes, they cannot be separated from God in the way that would imply otherness (ghayriyya). The passage ends abruptly with 'and what...' (wa-ma), indicating that the discussion continues on the next page.

Names of Action vs. Essence, and the Identity of Name and Named
Baqillani distinguishes divine names by attribute type and states the name is the named.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's distinction between names of action and names of essence, and his view that the name is identical to the named.

Core Idea
Some divine names are other than Allah; others are Him.

Baqillani carefully classifies Allah's names to defend divine simplicity. Some names refer to actions that are not identical to His essence; they are 'other than Him' because He existed before them. But names referring to His essence are identical to Him. This passage also introduces the famous Ash'ari doctrine that the name is the named.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice Baqillani's concise style.
2
Identify the two categories of divine names
The passage presents a classification: names of action (e.g., Just, Life-Giver) vs. names of essence. Mark where each is discussed.
The words 'فهي غيره' are key for action names.
3
Understand the reasoning for action names being 'other than Him'
Ask: why are action names not identical to Allah? Baqillani says because He existed without those actions. This shows divine independence.
4
Examine the claim that all names are the named
Baqillani reports a view from his companions: every name (of Allah or others) is the named itself. Note the nuance: some names indicate essence only, others indicate an attribute.
This upholds divine simplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كان من أسمائه راجعاً إلى إثبات صفة من صفات فعله ككونه عادلاً ومحسناً ومتفضلاً ومحيياً ومميتاً فهي غيره. لأنه قد كان موجوداً ومع عدمها. غير أن تسميته سبحانه لنفسه ترجع إلى إثبات صفة لنفسه. وصفات ذاته غير مختلفة لا يقال هي الله ولا يقال هي غيره لأن تسميته هي، وقوله وكلامه من صفات نفسه كسائر صفاته الذاتية، فوجب أن يقال إنها كلام له فقط. وقد زعم قوم من أصحابنا أن جميع أسماء الله سبحانه وأسماء غيره هي المسماة، غير أن منها ما يفيد ذات المسمى فقط، ومنها ما يفيد صفة له غير أن الاسم المفيد للصفة هو نفس المسمى لا معنى سواه.
Among His names, some refer to affirming an attribute from among His attributes of action, such as His being just, benevolent, gracious, life-giver, and death-giver, so they are other than Him. Because He was existent even in their absence. However, His naming of Himself refers to affirming an attribute for Himself. And the attributes of His essence are not different; it is not said they are Allah nor that they are other than Him, because His naming is He, and His speech and words are from the attributes of His self like the rest of His essential attributes. So it must be said that they are speech belonging to Him only. And a group of our companions have claimed that all the names of Allah and the names of others are the named, except that some of them indicate only the essence of the named, and some of them indicate an attribute for Him, but the name that indicates the attribute is itself the named, not something else.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage opens with a classification of Allah's names. Baqillani states that some names refer to attributes of action (e.g., 'Just', 'Benevolent', etc.); these names are 'other than Him' because Allah existed before these actions and without them. In contrast, names that refer to His essence (e.g., 'Allah' itself) affirm an attribute of the essence. The attributes of the essence are neither said to be Allah nor other than Allah, because the name is He. He then extends this to God's speech and words, which are attributes of the self and must be considered His speech only. Finally, he reports the position of some of his companions (Ash'aris) that all names—of Allah and of other things—are identical to the named (al-ism huwa al-musamma). However, some names indicate only the essence, while others indicate an attribute; in both cases the name is the named itself, not something additional.

Correction: Baqillani clarifies that names of action are 'other than Him' because Allah existed without those actions; they are not part of His essence.

The 99 Names as Designations: Baqillani's Interpretation and Refutation
Baqillani interprets the 99 names as 99 designations and refutes the absurd inference about fire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's interpretation of the hadith of 99 names as 99 designations referring to Allah's attributes, and his dismissal of the naive objection that identifying a name with its named leads to absurd consequences.

Core Idea
The 99 names are designations for attributes, not identical to Allah's essence.

Imam Baqillani tackles a common misunderstanding: does the hadith 'Allah has 99 names' mean 99 distinct entities? He explains that 'names' here means designations (tasmīyāt) that describe various attributes of Allah—some essential, some related to action. He then refutes a popular but naive objection that if a name were identical to its named, saying 'fire' would burn your mouth. This lesson unpacks his interpretation and refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the key terms like تسمية and عبارات. Let the author's own words set the stage.
The passage ends abruptly with 'لأن'; the full argument is incomplete.

Understand the interpretation of 99 names
Notice how Baqillani interprets 'names' as designations (تسميات) that express various attributes of Allah, including those of essence (صفات ذات) and those of action (صفات أفعال).
He also ties this to Quran 7:180, interpreting 'names' the same way.
3
Analyze the refutation of the naive objection
See how Baqillani dismisses the argument that if name equals named, then saying 'fire' would burn your mouth. He calls it 'the talk of the common folk and the clinging of idiots.'
The refutation is cut off, but the dismissal is clear.

وتأويل قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم: الله تسعة وتسعون اسماً من أحصاها دخل الجنة أي: له تسع وتسعون تسميةً عبارات عن كون الباري تبارك وتعالى على أوصاف شتى منها ما يستحقه لنفسه، ومنها ما يستحقه لصفة تتعلق به. وأسماؤه العائدة إلى نفسه هي هو وما تعلق منها بصفة له، فهي أسماء له، فمنها صفات ذات ومنها صفات أفعال. وهذا هو تأويل قوله تعالى: والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها أي: تسميات.

And the interpretation of the Prophet's statement: 'Allah has ninety-nine names; whoever enumerates them enters Paradise' is that He has ninety-nine designations, expressions about the fact that the Creator is upon various attributes: some He deserves for Himself, and some He deserves for an attribute that pertains to Him. Commentary
This passage provides the interpretation of the well-known hadith of the 99 names. Baqillani explains that the '99 names' actually mean 99 designations (tasmīyāt) that describe the various attributes of Allah: some relate to His essence and some to His actions. These designations are expressions (ʿibārāt) about the different states of the Creator. He then links this to the Quranic verse 'And to Allah belong the most beautiful names' (7:180), interpreting 'names' as designations. Following this, he refutes a popular objection: if the name is identical to the named, then uttering 'fire' should burn one's mouth, and uttering 'Zayd' should make Zayd physically present in the mouth. Allah's Names as Designations for Essence and Attributes
Baqillani explains that Allah's names are designations for His essence and attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's names are designations for His essence and attributes, not separate entities, and how the 99 names are interpreted in this framework.

Core Idea
Allah's names are designations for His attributes and essence, not separate entities.

In this passage, Baqillani clarifies a crucial theological principle: the names of Allah are not distinct beings but rather designations that describe His essence and attributes. He addresses a potential confusion by using the analogy of a person named Zayd, and then interprets the famous hadith about 99 names in light of this understanding. This lesson will unpack his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice Baqillani's opening statement about names and attributes.
The passage uses a technical distinction between 'ism' (name) and 'tasmiyah' (designation).
2
Understand the Zayd analogy
Baqillani uses the example of Zayd to show that a single entity can have multiple designations but only one name. Reflect on how this applies to Allah.
The analogy illustrates that multiple designations do not imply multiple essences.
3
Interpret the 99 names
Notice how Baqillani explains the hadith as referring to 99 designations for various attributes, not 99 separate entities.
This interpretation avoids anthropomorphism and upholds tawhid.
4
Classify the names
Observe the two categories: attributes of essence (e.g., al-Quddus) and attributes of acts (e.g., al-Khaliq). Understand the significance.
This classification helps in understanding the nature of Allah's names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خالف تسميات وعبارات عن أسماء الله تعالى غير أن ذاته على أحكام، وتلك الأحكام هي الأسماء نعبر عنها بتسميات وعبارات مختلفة التي هي ذاته فقط. فإن قالوا : فإذا سُمِّي زيد من نايحة كونه زيداً بعشرة أسماء، فهي هي؟ قيل لهم : أما اسم زيد فهو زيد وليس له من حيث هو زيد أكثر من اسم واحد ؛ ولكن له تسمياتٍ كثيرةً متغايرةً . وتأويل قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : الله تسعة وتسعون اسماً من أحصاها دخل الجنة أي : له تسع وتسعون تسميةً عبارات عن كون الباري تبارك وتعالى على أوصاف شتى منها ما يستحقه لنفسه، ومنها ما يستحقه لصفة تتعلق به . وأسماؤه العائدة إلى نفسه هي هو وما تعلق منها بصفة له، فهي أسماء له، فمنها صفات ذات ومنها صفات أفعال. وهذا هو تأويل قوله تعالى : والله الأسماء الحسنى فادعوه بها أي : تسميات .
Contrary to designations and expressions regarding the names of Allah the Exalted, but His essence possesses attributes, and those attributes are the names, which we express by various designations and expressions, which are only His essence itself. For if they say: 'If Zayd is called by ten names from the aspect of his being Zayd, are they the same?' It is said to them: 'As for the name of Zayd, it is Zayd, and he has, inasmuch as he is Zayd, no more than one name; but he has many different designations.' And the interpretation of the saying of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'Allah has ninety-nine names; whoever enumerates them will enter Paradise' is that He has ninety-nine designations: expressions about the Creator, Blessed and Exalted, being upon various attributes, among them what He deserves for His essence, and among them what He deserves for an attribute connected to Him. And His names that refer to His essence are He Himself, and those that are connected to an attribute of His are names for Him; among them are attributes of essence and among them are attributes of acts. And this is the interpretation of His saying, the Exalted: 'And to Allah belong the beautiful names, so call upon Him by them' (Quran 7:180) meaning: designations.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between Allah's names and attributes. Baqillani begins by stating that Allah's essence has attributes, and those attributes are the names, which we express through various designations and expressions. He then addresses an objection: if a person like Zayd can be called by ten different names, does that mean the names are multiple entities? The answer distinguishes between a name (ism) and a designation (tasmiyah): Zayd as such has only one name, but he can have many designations referring to different aspects. Similarly, the Prophet's statement about 99 names is interpreted as 99 designations that describe various attributes of Allah: some pertain to His essence (e.g., al-Quddus) and some pertain to actions (e.g., al-Khaliq). The Quranic verse 'And to Allah belong the beautiful names' is thus understood to mean 'beautiful designations.'

Misconception: The 99 names of Allah are 99 separate beings or entities.
Correction: They are 99 designations for different attributes of the one essence.

Allah's Names Are Not Shared with Creation
Baqillani distinguishes between names of essence (not shared) and designations (some shared).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani distinguishes between the essence-based names of Allah (which cannot be shared) and the designations (some of which may be applied to creation).

Core Idea
Allah's names are His essence; designations may be shared, but not the reality.

Baqillani addresses a critical question: can the names of Allah be applied to creation? He argues it is impossible for them to be shared in reality, but he allows that the verbal designations (tasmiyah) used for Allah may also be used for creatures in some cases. This distinction safeguards divine transcendence while accounting for common language.

Read the Arabic and translation to get the flow of the argument.

2

Look for the shift from 'names' to 'designations' and the list of attributes.
Baqillani uses 'except' to introduce the exception.
3
Reflect on the implications
Think about why some designations like 'Creator' are exclusive to Allah.

فصل آخر من الكلام في هذا الباب. إن قال قائل : فهل تزعمون أن أسماء الله مشتركة بينه وبين خلقه؟ قيل له هذه مسألة محال لأن أسماءه هي نفسه أو صفة تتعلق بنفسه، ونفسه تعالى وصفات نفسه لا يجوز أن تكون مشتركة بينه وبين خلقه . إلا أن التسمية التي تُجرى عليه التي يُدَلّ بها على اسمه، يجوز أن يُجرى بعضها على خلقه وليُدَلّ بها على أن للخلق أسماء أو أوصاف نحو القول بأن الله حي عالم قادر سميع بصير متكلم مريد و خالق و رازق وعادل ومنها تسميات لا يجوز أن تُجرى إلا على الله .
Another chapter of discourse in this section. If a questioner says: 'Do you claim that Allah's names are shared between Him and His creation?' It is said to him: 'This matter is impossible, because His names are His essence or an attribute connected to His essence, and His essence, Exalted, and the attributes of His essence cannot be shared between Him and His creation. Except that the designation that is applied to Him, by which His name is indicated, it is permissible to apply some of them to His creation, and to indicate by them that the creation has names or descriptions, such as the saying that Allah is Living, Knowing, Powerful, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, Willing, Creator, Provider, Just; and among them are designations that cannot be applied except to Allah.'
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a new section (fasl) answering whether Allah's names can be shared with creation. Baqillani states the question and gives a clear answer: it is impossible (muhal) because Allah's names are either His essence itself or an attribute connected to His essence, neither of which can be common to created beings. However, he makes a crucial distinction: the designation (tasmiyah) that is applied to Allah and indicates His name can be used for creation in some cases. He lists attributes like Living, Knowing, Powerful, etc., which can be applied to both Allah and creatures, though in different ways (mutafariq). But some designations like Creator (Khaliq) and Provider (Raziq) are exclusive to Allah and cannot be applied to creation. This distinction preserves divine uniqueness while allowing linguistic sharing of certain terms.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani distinguishes between Allah's names (which are His essence and cannot be shared) and designations (which may be applied to creation, except those exclusive to Allah like Creator).

?
If you truly believed that sharing Allah's names in their real sense is impossible, would you still envy the power or knowledge of others? How does Baqillani's distinction between name and designation cut the root of envy and fear of creation?

God's Names and Appellations: The Shared and the Exclusive
Baqillani explains why God's names are not shared with creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will distinguish between God's names (asma') and appellations (tasmia), and understand why some divine appellations are exclusive to God.

Core Idea
God's names are not shared, but some appellations may apply to creation in a different sense.

In this passage, Imam al-Baqillani addresses a common objection about God's names being shared with creation. He clarifies a crucial distinction between the names themselves, which are tied to God's essence and attributes, and the verbal appellations that can be applied to creatures in a limited way. Understanding this distinction protects divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the question and answer.
Pay attention to the structure: question, then answer with a distinction.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice how Baqillani separates the concept of the name (ism) from the appellation (tasmia). This is the core point.
The name is the essence/attribute itself; the appellation is the verbal expression.
3
Analyze the lists
Examine the two sets of appellations: those that may be applied to creation and those that are exclusive to God.
List the appellations from the passage in each category.
4
Reflect on the implication
Think about why this distinction is important for tawhid—it prevents comparing God to creation.
Even when using similar words, the reality of the attribute is incomparable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Another chapter of discourse in this section. If a questioner says: 'Do you claim that the names of God are shared between Him and His creation?' It is said to him: This is an impossible matter because His names are His essence or an attribute pertaining to His essence, and His essence — exalted is He — and the attributes of His essence cannot be shared between Him and His creation. However, the appellation (tasmia) that is applied to Him, by which His name is indicated, it is permissible to apply some of it to His creation in order to indicate that creation has names or attributes, such as saying that God is Living, Knowing, Powerful, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, Willing, Creator, Provider, and Just. And among them are appellations that may not be applied except to God—glorified is He—such as our saying 'Allah', 'al-Rahman', 'al-Ilah', 'al-Khaliq', and whatever runs in that vein, which may not be applied to creation.

Commentary
This passage addresses whether God's names are shared with creation. Baqillani states that the question is impossible because God's names are either His essence (dhat) or an attribute (sifa) pertaining to His essence, and neither can be shared. However, the appellations (tasmia) used to indicate His names can sometimes be applied to creation in a different sense, like 'Living', 'Knowing', etc., which describe both God and creatures, but the meaning is not identical. Some appellations like 'Allah', 'al-Rahman', 'al-Ilah', 'al-Khaliq' are exclusive to God. This distinguishes between the name itself (ism) and the verbal appellation (tasmia).

The Verse 'Be' and the Impossibility of an Infinite Regress
Baqillani uses Quran 16:40 to prove the Quran is uncreated via infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses Quran 16:40 to prove that the Quran is uncreated by showing that its createdness would lead to an impossible infinite regress.

Core Idea
If the Quran were created, an endless chain of creations would be needed—an impossibility.

Imam Baqillani begins his proof against the createdness of the Quran by turning to the Quran itself. He cites a verse that describes how God creates simply by saying 'Be,' and then argues that if the Quran were a created thing, it would need a prior speech to create it, and that speech would need another, leading to an infinite regress. This regress is logically impossible, so the Quran must be uncreated.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage from Baqillani's text once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the argument's flow.
Notice the precise wording of the verse and the logical consequence that follows.
2
Identify the key elements
Identify: the verse cited, the conditional statement ('if the Quran were created'), the conclusion (infinite regress), and the final judgment (impossible).
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument is: verse shows God's word 'Be' is sufficient for creation. If Quran is created, it must be created by another speech. That speech must also be created, leading to an endless chain of speeches before any act, which is impossible.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the significance
Why does this prove the Quran is uncreated? Because the alternative leads to an absurdity. This is a foundational proof in Sunni theology for the eternity of the Quran.
This argument depends on the principle that an infinite regress of actual causes is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو بكر: والذي يدل على نفي خلق القرآن من القرآن قوله تعالى: "إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه أن نقول له كن فيكون" فلو كان القرآن مخلوقاً، لكان مخلوقاً بقول آخر. وذلك يوجب أن لا يوجد الله تعالى فعل أصلاً، إذا كان لا بد أن يوجد قبله أفعال لا غاية لها. وذلك محال باتفاق منا ومنهم.
Abu Bakr said: That which indicates the negation of the creation (khalq) of the Quran from the Quran is His saying, exalted: 'Our saying to a thing when We will it is that We say to it "Be," and it becomes.' (Quran 16:40) So if the Quran were created, it would be created by another speech. And that necessitates that God, exalted, would not perform any act at all, since there must be before it acts with no end. That is impossible by agreement between us and them.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the Quran itself against the createdness of the Quran. Baqillani cites the verse that God's command 'Be' (kun) is sufficient for creation. If the Quran were created (makhluq), then God would need another speech (qawl) to create it, leading to an infinite regress of acts before any act, which is impossible (muhal). This argument shows the Quran must be uncreated.

Key Terms

المخلوق
القول
الكن
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Be' refers to a separate created thing rather than God's eternal attribute of speech.
Correction: The verse describes God's immediate creative command, which is an eternal attribute, not a created entity. If it were created, an infinite regress would occur.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Quran cannot be created because its createdness would lead to an impossible infinite regress of acts before any divine act.

The Quran Cannot Be a Body: Baqillani's Disjunctive Proof
Baqillani presents a disjunctive argument against the createdness of the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's disjunctive argument that if the Quran were created, it would have to be either a body or an accident, both of which are impossible, with a focus on why it cannot be a body.

Core Idea
If created, Quran must be body or accident; both are impossible.

Baqillani presents a tight logical disjunction: every created thing is either a body subsisting by itself or an accident existing in something else. Since the Quran is neither, it cannot be created. This passage unfolds the reasoning against the possibility of the Quran being a body.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic Passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Notice the conditional 'if the Quran were created' and the subsequent disjunction.
Arabic uses 'لا يخلو أن يكون' to express the logical necessity of one of two options.
2
Identify the Disjunctive Premise
Baqillani asserts that any created entity must be either a body (jism) or an accident (ʿaraḍ). Understand that this dichotomization is foundational to his argument.
This premise relies on the classification of all existing things into bodies and accidents.
3
Examine the Reasons Against Body
Baqillani gives two reasons why the Quran cannot be a body: (1) a body does not have the kind of relation to something else that attributes have, and (2) all bodies are of one genus, so if some bodies were speech, all would be.
The second reason appeals to the homogeneity of bodies – if one body is speech, then the genus would entail speech for all.
4
Conclude the Argument
The impossibility of the Quran being a body, together with the disjunctive premise, forces the conclusion that the Quran cannot be created. Thus, it must be an uncreated attribute of Allah.
The passage ends by stating that the corruption of the second consequence proves speech cannot be a body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل آخر وهو أنه لو كان القرآن مخلوقاً، لكان لا يخلو أن يكون جسماً قائماً بنفسه أو عرضاً مفعولاً في غيره. ويستحيل أن يكون جسماً، كما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون بنفسه قائماً، وأن يكون كلاماً لا لمتكلم، لأن الجسم ليس له تعلق بغيره كتعلق الصفات، ولأن الأجسام كلها من جنس واحد؛ فلو كان بعضها كلاماً لخالق أو مخلوق، لوجب أن تكون جميعاً كلاماً. وفي فساد ذلك دليل على أن الكلام لا يجوز أن يكون جسماً.
Another proof: that if the Quran were created, it would not be free from being either a body (jism) subsisting by itself (qā'im bi-nafsihi) or an accident ('araḍ) made (maf'ūl) in something else. It is impossible for it to be a body, just as it is not permissible for it to be subsisting by itself, and to be speech (kalām) without a speaker, because the body has no relation to something else like the relation of attributes, and because all bodies are of one genus (jins wāḥid); so if some of them were speech for a creator or created, then all of them would necessarily be speech. In the corruption (fasād) of that is a proof that speech cannot be a body.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents a disjunctive argument: a created thing is either a body (jism) or an accident ('araḍ). The Quran cannot be a body because bodies are homogeneous (all of one genus) and if one body could be speech, all would be, which is false. Also, a body cannot have the relation to its maker that attributes have. The Quran cannot be an accident because accidents do not subsist by themselves and would inhere in something else, but the Quran is speech that is not located. So it must be uncreated, a divine attribute.

Key Terms

القائم بنفسه

Checkpoint

B) All bodies are of one genus, so if one body is speech, all would be.
C) Bodies cannot be described by attributes.
D) Bodies are created.

Key Takeaway
The Quran cannot be a body because all bodies are homogeneous and cannot bear the relation of attributes; hence it must be an uncreated attribute of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Baqillani argues that speech cannot be a body. If the Quran is not a physical thing, how should your heart approach it? Does your reverence match the reality that you are engaging with Allah's eternal attribute? Ask Allah to grant you awe when reciting.

The proof shows that the Quran cannot be created because it is neither body nor accident. Does this truth impact your trust in its commands? If it is eternal and from Allah, how seriously do you take its warnings and promises? Write one command you have neglected and resolve to act on it.

Baqillani's First Proof: Infinite Regress and the Uncreated Quran
Baqillani adduces Quranic verse to prove the Quran is not created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the Qur'anic verse and the impossibility of infinite regress to prove that the Qur'an is not created.

Core Idea
If the Quran were created, it would require an endless chain of words—an impossibility.

Baqillani begins his defense of the uncreated Qur'an with a powerful logical proof rooted in a Qur'anic verse. He shows that accepting the Qur'an as created leads to an absurd infinite regress, a conclusion rejected by all rational thinkers. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Notice the verse quoted and the logical flow that follows.
The verse is from Surah an-Nahl (16:40).
2
Identify the key premise
Baqillani assumes that every created thing comes into existence by a word from God. Apply this to the Quran itself.
3
Trace the infinite regress
If the Quran were created by a word, that word would itself need a word, leading to an endless chain. Understand why this is impossible.
This is called tasalsul (infinite regress).
4
Grasp the conclusion
Baqillani concludes this is impossible by consensus (of Muslims and rational thinkers). The Quran therefore cannot be created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في نفي خلق القرآن
قال أبو بكر : والذي يدل على نفي خلق القرآن من القرآن قوله تعالى : إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه أن نقول له كن فيكون . فلو كان القرآن مخلوقاً ، لكان مخلوقاً بقول آخر. وذلك يوجب أن لا يوجد الله تعالى فعل أصلاً ، إذا كان لا بد أن يوجد قبله أفعال من لا غاية لها . وذلك محال باتفاق منا ومنهم .
Regarding the negation of the creation of the Qur'an. Abu Bakr said: That which indicates the negation of the creation of the Qur'an from the Qur'an is His statement, exalted: 'Our saying to a thing when We will it is only to say to it 'Be,' and it is.' So if the Qur'an were created, it would be created by another word. And that would necessitate that Allah, exalted, does not perform any act at all, since there must exist before it acts without end. And that is impossible by consensus of us and them.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the createdness of the Qur'an. Baqillani begins by citing the Qur'anic verse (Surah an-Nahl: 40): 'Our saying to a thing when We will it is only to say to it 'Be,' and it is.' He then constructs an argument: if the Qur'an were itself created (makhluq), then it would have to be brought into existence by another word (qawl), because every created thing comes into being by God's command. That would require an infinite chain of words, each requiring another word, which is impossible. Such an infinite regress (tasalsul) would mean that God never actually performs any act, since an endless series of preceding acts would be needed. This conclusion is unanimously rejected by both Muslims and opponents. Thus, the Qur'an cannot be created.

Key Terms
خلق القرآن
قول
تسلسل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse only shows that God creates by command, but doesn't relate to the Quran's status.
Correction: Baqillani uses the verse to argue that if the Quran were created, it would need a command, leading to an impossible infinite chain, proving it is uncreated.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's first proof, why would the creation of the Qur'an lead to an impossibility?
A) Because the Qur'an would then be eternal.
B) Because it would require an infinite chain of words.
C) Because the verse says 'Be' only once.
D) Because God cannot create speech.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an cannot be created because that would require an infinite chain of creative words, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you treat it as merely a created text, or as the very speech of the uncreated Creator? How does this lesson challenge your daily engagement with the Qur'an?
?

?
Reflect on a time when you easily dismissed a difficulty in faith as 'just an interpretation'. Does Baqillani's strict logical proof here force you to take a stronger stance on the Qur'an's nature? What will you do differently starting today?
