Why the Qur'an Cannot Be a Body: Baqillani's Second Proof
Baqillani argues the Qur'an cannot be a body if created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that the Qur'an cannot be a body, based on the impossibility of speech inhering in bodies.

Core Idea
Bodies are uniform; if one were speech, all would be—an impossibility.

Baqillani continues his defense of the Qur'an as uncreated by addressing the possibility that it could be a body if created. He presents a clean logical argument based on the nature of bodies and speech. This lesson walks through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through before interpreting.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if created... then not free from...'
2
Understand the Disjunction
Baqillani sets up two possibilities for a created thing: either it is a self-subsistent body or an accident in something else. Recognize this as the starting point.
3
Focus on the Body Argument
The passage argues that the Qur'an cannot be a body. The key reason: bodies are of one genus, so if some bodies were speech, all bodies would have to be speech—which is absurd.
This relies on the principle of genus uniformity.
4
Recognize the Absurdity
The conclusion that all bodies would be speech is false. Therefore, the initial assumption that the Qur'an could be a body must be false.
5
Conclude
Thus, speech (including the Qur'an) cannot be a body. This supports the overall argument that the Qur'an is not created in the manner of bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل آخر وهو أنه لو كان القرآن مخلوقاً، لكان لا يخلو أن يكون جسماً قائماً بنفسه أو عرضاً مفعولاً في غيره. ويستحيل أن يكون جسماً، كما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون بنفسه قائماً، وأن يكون كلاماً لا لمتكلم، لأن الجسم ليس له تعلق بغيره كتعلق الصفات، ولأن الأجسام كلها من جنس واحد؛ فلو كان بعضها كلاماً لخالق أو مخلوق، لوجب أن تكون جميعاً كلاماً. وفي فساد ذلك دليل على أن الكلام لا يجوز أن يكون جسماً.
Another proof: And that is that if the Qur'an were created, it would not be free from being either a self-subsistent body or an accident effected in something else. And it is impossible that it be a body, just as it is not permissible that it be self-subsistent, and that it be speech without a speaker, because a body has no attachment to something else like the attachment of attributes, and because all bodies are of one genus; so if some of them were speech for a creator or a created being, it would be necessary that all of them be speech. And the corruption of that is evidence that speech cannot be a body.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage contains the second proof against the createdness of the Qur'an, focusing on the impossibility of it being a body (jism). Baqillani first states that if the Qur'an were created, it would have to be either a self-subsistent body or an accident in something else. He then argues against the first possibility: it cannot be a body because a body does not relate to something else the way attributes do; rather, bodies are independent substances. Moreover, all bodies are of the same genus (jins). If some bodies were speech (kalam) for a creator or created being, then all bodies would have to be speech, which is patently false. This absurdity proves that speech, including the Qur'an, cannot be a body.

Key Terms
جسم
عرض
كلام
جنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think speech can be a body.
Correction: Baqillani shows that bodies are uniform and cannot bear such a quality; if they could, all bodies would be speech.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani argue the Qur'an cannot be a body?
A) Because a body must be created.
B) Because all bodies are the same genus, so if one were speech, all would be speech.
C) Because bodies are not made of speech.
D) Because a body cannot be created by God.

Key Takeaway
Speech, including the Qur'an, cannot be a body because bodies are of one genus and cannot be speech.

?

The Qur'an Cannot Be an Accident: Baqillani's Three Arguments
Baqillani disproves the possibility that the Qur'an is an accident if created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the Qur'an, if created, would be an impossible accident, and thus must be uncreated.

Core Idea
An accident cannot exist without a locus; God is not a locus.

Baqillani continues his second proof by examining the possibility that the Qur'an is an accident (arad). He systematically eliminates each possible locus for such an accident: in God Himself, in nothing, or in another thing. Each option leads to absurdity, demonstrating that the Qur'an cannot be created and therefore must be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three options
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the three possible loci Baqillani considers for an accident: in God, in nothing, or in another thing.
These three options are exhaustive for any created accident.
2
Understand why God cannot be a locus
Baqillani states that God is not a locus for accidents (mahall li al-hawadith). Reflect on why this is a foundational principle in Islamic theology.
This is based on God's transcendence and immutability.
3
Grasp why an accident cannot exist without a locus
Baqillani argues that an accident cannot be self-subsistent; it requires a subject. He gives examples like movement, color, and life. Also, if it existed in nothing, it would bear attributes, which is impossible.
Attributes cannot bear other attributes; only substances can.
4
Recognize the problem with creating speech in another thing
If God created the Qur'an in another being, that speech would become an attribute of that being, not of God. This would be absurd because the Qur'an is uniquely God's speech.
This mirrors the problem of knowledge and will created in bodies.
5
Conclude that all options are impossible
Since each possible locus leads to contradiction, the initial assumption that the Qur'an is a created accident is false. Therefore, the Qur'an is uncreated and eternal.
This proof uses reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل أيضاً أن يكون عرضاً لأنه لو كان عرضاً مفعولاً، لم يخل أن يكون الباري سبحانه فاعلاً له في نفسه أو في غيره أو لا في مكان. ويستحيل من قولنا جميعاً أن يفعله في نفسه تعالى، لأنه ليس بمحل للحوادث. ويستحيل أن يفعله لا في شيء، كما يستحيل فعل حركة ولون وحياة لا في شيء. ولأنه لو فعله لا في شيء، لصحّ أن يحمل الصفات. لأن الشيء إنما جاز قيام الصفات به لاستغنائه في الوجود عن شيء يوجد به، ولذلك لم يجز أن تحمل الصفات الصفات. وإذا استحال كون الكلام حاملاً للصفات، استحال قيامه بنفسه. ويستحيل أيضاً أن يخلقه في غيره. لأن ذلك يوجب أن يكون صفةً وكلاماً لمن خلق فيه، كما أن العلم والإرادة المخلوقين في الأجسام صفتان لمن وجدا به دون الخالق بهما. ولما لم يجز أن يكون كلام الباري صفةً لغيره وكلاماً لغيره، لم يجز أن يكون مخلوقاً في غيره. وإذا استحال أن يخلقه تعالى في نفسه أو في غيره أو قائماً بنفسه، استحال أن يكون خالقاً له.
And it is also impossible that it be an accident, because if it were an effected accident, it would not be free from the Exalted Creator being its agent in Himself or in something else or not in a locus. And it is impossible, from all our statements, that He do it in Himself, exalted, because He is not a locus for accidents. And it is impossible that He do it not in something, just as it is impossible to do a movement, a color, and a life not in something. And because if He did it not in something, it would be valid for it to bear attributes. Because a thing is only permissible for attributes to subsist in it due to its independence in existence from something by which it exists, and therefore it is not permissible for attributes to bear attributes. And if it is impossible for speech to be a bearer of attributes, it is impossible for it to be self-subsistent. And it is also impossible that He create it in something else. Because that would necessitate that it be an attribute and speech for the one in whom it is created, just as knowledge and will created in bodies are attributes of the one in whom they exist, not of the creator of them. And since it is not permissible for the speech of the Exalted to be an attribute of another and speech of another, it is not permissible for it to be created in another. And if it is impossible for Him, exalted, to create it in Himself or in another or self-subsistent, it is impossible that He be its creator.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage continues the second proof by examining the possibility that the Qur'an, if created, would be an accident (arad). Baqillani presents three options for where an accident could exist: in God Himself, in nothing, or in another thing. First, it cannot be in God because He is not a locus (mahall) for accidents (hawadith). Second, it cannot be in nothing because accidents cannot exist without a locus; just as a movement or color cannot exist without a body, speech cannot exist without a speaker. Moreover, if it existed in nothing, it would itself bear attributes, but attributes cannot bear other attributes, so speech cannot be self-subsistent. Third, it cannot exist in another thing because then the speech would become an attribute of that other thing (e.g., a body), not of God. This would be absurd, as the Qur'an is God's speech and cannot be attributed to anyone else. All these options lead to impossibility, proving that God cannot be the creator of the Qur'an in any sense, hence the Qur'an is uncreated.

محل
حوادث
صفات

Misconception: Some might think an accident can exist without a locus.
Correction: Baqillani shows that accidents like movement, color, and life cannot exist without a subject, and neither can speech.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani say the Qur'an cannot be an accident created in God Himself?
A) Because God is not a body.
B) Because God is not a locus for accidents.
C) Because accidents cannot be created.
D) Because the Qur'an is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that the Qur'an cannot be an accident because it would require an impossible locus, proving that it is not created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Baqillani argues that if the Qur'an were created, it would be an attribute of something other than God. Reflect: do you ever treat the Qur'an as if it were a human product, subject to your judgment or cultural norms? What would it mean for you to submit completely to its authority, even when it contradicts your preferences?
?
The proof shows that three logical possibilities for a created Qur'an are all impossible. Does your belief that the Qur'an is from God lead you to approach it with the reverence it demands? In what specific area of your life are you failing to let the Qur'an's eternal speech govern you, and what step will you take today to realign?

Third Proof: The Qur'an Cannot Be Created Because It Would Be Fleeting
Baqillani argues that if the Qur'an were created, it would be a temporary accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that if the Qur'an were created, it would be a fleeting accident, and therefore cannot be created.

Core Idea
Created speech would vanish instantly—but Allah's speech endures.

Baqillani presents a third proof against the createdness of the Qur'an, building on the metaphysical nature of accidents. He argues that if the Qur'an were created and not a body, it would have to be an accident. But accidents are transient, perishing the moment they arise. If that were true, then the Qur'an would be gone now—and with it all divine commands and promises. That cannot be.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how the argument is structured as a conditional chain: if A then B, if B then C.
The Arabic is cut off but the reasoning is clear.
2
Understand the conditional reasoning
Identify the two conditionals: (1) If the Qur'an were created and not a body, it must be an accident. (2) If it were an accident, it must perish in the second moment of its existence.
3
Grasp the implication for divine address
If the Qur'an perished, then at the present moment Allah would not be commanding, forbidding, promising, etc. The text breaks off but the conclusion is that this is impossible, so the premise is false.
4
Reflect on why this refutes the opponent
This proof shows that the createdness view leads to absurdity: the Qur'an would have no lasting authority. Therefore the Qur'an must be uncreated.

Source Text

And if it were an accident, it would necessarily be perishing in the second moment of its occurrence, and that the Exalted would not be, at our present time, commanding something nor forbidding about it nor promising nor
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof against the creation of the Qur'an, based on the nature of accidents. Baqillani states that if the Qur'an were created and not a body (a point of agreement between Muslims and their opponents), then it would have to be an accident (arad). But accidents are impermanent; they perish in the very next moment after coming into existence (fānin fī al-thānī min ḥāl ḥudūthihi). If that were the case, then at the present time, God would not be commanding anything, forbidding anything, promising, or (the text cuts off). The implication is that the Qur'an as God's speech would cease to exist, which is impossible since it contains eternal commands, prohibitions, and promises. The argument is incomplete in this window but the reasoning is clear: created speech would be fleeting, contradicting the ongoing applicability of divine address.

Proof That Divine Speech Cannot Be an Accident or Created
Baqillani proves divine speech cannot be an accident, created in self or other, or self-subsisting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that divine speech cannot be an accident or created, and how he refutes each possible scenario.

Core Idea
If something is created, it must exist somewhere—but none of those places works for Allah's speech.

Imam Baqillani presents a rigorous proof that divine speech cannot be an accident or a created entity. He systematically examines every possible way something could be created—either in its agent, in another, or self-subsisting—and shows each is impossible for Allah's speech. This argument is foundational for the Ash'ari doctrine that the Quran is uncreated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: a series of refutations.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the three possibilities
Baqillani lists three ways speech could be created if it were an accident: created in Allah Himself, created in no place (self-subsisting), or created in another. Find each in the text.
3
Understand why each is impossible
For each possibility, Baqillani gives a distinct reason. Trace each reason: (1) Allah is not a locus for accidents; (2) nothing self-subsisting can carry attributes except substances; (3) if created in another, it becomes that other's attribute.
This is the core of the proof.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Since all possibilities are ruled out, divine speech cannot be created at all. Therefore it is eternal and uncreated.
This refutes the Mu'tazilite view that the Quran is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل أيضاً أن يكون عرضاً لأنه لو كان عرضاً مفعولاً، لم يخل أن يكون الباري سبحانه فاعلاً له في نفسه أو في غيره أو لا في مكان. ويستحيل من قولنا جميعاً أن يفعله في نفسه تعالى، لأنه ليس بمحل للحوادث. ويستحيل أن يفعله لا في شيء، كما يستحيل فعل حركة ولون وحياة لا في شيء. ولأنه لو فعله لا في شيء، لصح أن يحمل الصفات. لأن الشيء إنما جاز قيام الصفات به لاستغنائه في الوجود عن شيء يوجد به ولذلك لم يجز أن تحمل الصفات الصفات. وإذا استحال كون الكلام حاملاً للصفات، استحال قيامه بنفسه ويستحيل أيضاً أن يخلقه في غيره. لأن ذلك يوجب أن يكون صفة وكلاماً لمن خلق فيه، كما أن العلم والإرادة المخلوقين في الأجسام صفتان لمن وجدا به دون الخالق بهما. ولما لم يجز أن يكون كلام الباري صفة لغيره وكلاماً لغيره، لم يجز أن يكون مخلوقاً في غيره. وإذا استحال أن يخلقه تعالى في نفسه أو في غيره أو قائماً بنفسه، استحال أن يكون خالقاً له. إذ لو خلقه، لم يخل من ذلك.
It is also impossible that it be an accident, because if it were a created accident, it would be inevitable that Allah, exalted, is its doer either in Himself, or in another, or not in a place. And it is impossible by all of our saying that He does it in Himself, exalted, because He is not a locus for accidents. And it is impossible that He does it not in a thing, just as it is impossible to do movement, color, and life not in a thing. And because if He did it not in a thing, it would be valid for it to carry the attributes. Because a thing is only permissible for attributes to subsist in it due to its independence in existence from something by which it exists, and therefore it is not permissible for attributes to carry attributes. And if it is impossible for speech to be a carrier of attributes, it is impossible for it to subsist by itself. And it is also impossible that He create it in another, because that would necessitate it being an attribute and speech of the one in whom it is created, just as knowledge and will created in bodies are attributes of those in whom they exist, not of the creator of them. And since it is not permissible for the speech of Allah to be an attribute of another and speech of another, it is not permissible for it to be created in another. And if it is impossible for Him, exalted, to create it in Himself, or in another, or subsisting by itself, it is impossible for Him to be its creator. For if He created it, it would not be free from that.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a proof (دليل) that divine speech cannot be an accident or created. Baqillani begins by stating it is impossible (يستحيل) for speech to be an accident (عرض) because if it were a created accident, then Allah would have to produce it either in Himself, in another, or in no place. He refutes each: producing it in Himself is impossible because Allah is not a locus for accidents (محل للحوادث). Producing it in no place is impossible because accidents like movement and color cannot exist without a substrate. If it were in no place, it would be possible for speech to carry attributes (يحمل الصفات), but only self-subsistent things can carry attributes; attributes cannot carry attributes. So it cannot be self-subsistent. And producing it in another is impossible because then it would be an attribute of that other, as knowledge and will created in bodies are attributes of the body, not the creator. Since Allah's speech cannot be an attribute of another, it cannot be created in another. Therefore, since all possibilities are impossible, speech cannot be created. This refutes the claim that the Quran is created.

Key Terms
عرض
محل للحوادث
صفة
كلام
مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani argue that speech cannot be created in another?
A) Because Allah does not create in others.
B) Because it would then become an attribute of that other, not of Allah.
C) Because speech is not an accident.
D) Because the other would be a locus for accidents.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech cannot be created because every possible mode of creation leads to an impossibility; thus it is eternal and uncreated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Baqillani's argument leaves no room for thinking divine speech is like our speech. When you read 'Indeed, prayers have been decreed upon the believers at specific times,' do you believe that command is eternal and uncreated? Will you commit to reciting one verse today with the awareness that it is not created but is from your Lord directly?

Divine Speech Cannot Be Created: The Argument from Inimitability
Baqillani argues that the Quran's inimitability proves it is not created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's proof that the Quran's inimitability demonstrates it is not created, and how he refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that humans can produce something equivalent.

Core Idea
If the Quran were created, it would be imitable—but Allah's challenge proves it is not.

Baqillani presents a powerful argument from inimitability: if the Quran were created, it would be composed of the same letters as human speech, making it theoretically imitable. Yet Allah has challenged all of creation to produce a single like, and they have failed. This lesson unpacks the logic of this proof and its refutation of those who claim to match or exceed the Quran's eloquence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then'.
2
Identify the conditional argument
Baqillani starts with an implication: if the Quran were created, it would be like human speech and thus imitable.
This is the logical foundation of the proof.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
Allah's challenge (17:88) shows the impossibility of imitation, refuting the idea that the Quran is created.
This is the key evidence.
4
Understand the Mu'tazilite objection and rebuttal
The Mu'tazilites claim they can produce more eloquent speech. Baqillani counters with a reductio: if so, why has no one done so?
Baqillani uses rhetorical questioning to expose the flaw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل آخر : وهو أن كلام الله تعالى لو كان مخلوقاً، لكان من جنس كلام المخلوقين وغير خارج عن حروف المعجم ولوجب أن تكون الألف منه مثل الألف من كلامنا وكذلك الدال والواو وغيرها من الحروف. ولوجب أن يكون الخلق قادرين على مثله وما هو من جنسه من حيث صحت قدرتهم على ضروب الكلام الذي لا تخرج جملته من حروف المعجم. وقد أكذب الله تعالى من قال ذلك حيث يقول: قل لئن اجتمعت الإنس والجن على أن يأتوا بمثل هذا القرآن لا يأتون بمثله وأبطل قول من قال: إن هذا إلا قول البشر وسحر يؤثر وإنه أساطير الأولين والمعتزلة تزيد على ذلك أجمع، لأنهم يقولون إنهم يقدرون على ما هو أفصح وأحسن وأوجز من كلام الله، وإن القدرة على الخطابة والنشر والنظم وضروب كلام البشر هي القدرة على مثل كلام الله تعالى فيقال لهم: فما يؤمننا أن يأتي بمثله وبما هو أفصح منه بعض البشر إذا قصد ذلك وتوفرت دواعيه على التعمق في طلب العلم بنظمه؟
Another proof: And it is that if the speech of Allah, exalted, were created, it would be of the genus of the speech of creatures and not outside the letters of the alphabet, and it would be necessary that its alif be like the alif of our speech, and likewise the dal, waw, and other letters. And it would be necessary that the creation be able to produce the like of it and what is of its genus, since their ability over the kinds of speech whose totality does not depart from the letters of the alphabet is sound. And Allah, exalted, has declared false the statement of those who said that, where He says: 'Say: If mankind and jinn gathered to bring the like of this Quran, they could not bring its like.' And He invalidated the statement of those who said: 'This is nothing but the speech of a human' and 'sorcery that is learned' and 'tales of the ancients.' And the Mu'tazilites add to all that, for they say that they are able to produce what is more eloquent, better, and more concise than the speech of Allah, and that the power over eloquence, prose, poetry, and kinds of human speech is the power over the like of the speech of Allah. So it is said to them: What assurance do we have that some humans will not produce its like or something more eloquent, if they intend that and their motives are focused on delving into the knowledge of its composition?
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof (دليل آخر) that divine speech cannot be created. Baqillani argues that if the Quran were created, it would necessarily be composed of the same letters as human speech, making it possible for humans to produce something similar. However, Allah has challenged humanity and jinn to produce a single like of the Quran, affirming their inability. Allah also refutes those who call the Quran magic or human speech. Baqillani then points out that the Mu'tazilites go even further, claiming they can produce speech more eloquent and concise than Allah's word, and that their ability over human speech constitutes ability over the like of Allah's speech. Baqillani refutes this by posing a rhetorical question: if that were true, what assurance do we have that some human, with sufficient effort, would not produce something like the Quran? The inability to produce anything like it throughout history proves its divine origin and that it is not created speech.

Key Terms
حروف المعجم
إعجاز
مثل
قدرة
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies human ability to speak eloquently.
Correction: Baqillani is not denying human eloquence; he is denying that any human can produce the exact like of the Quran in its unique inimitability.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani argue would be possible if the Quran were created?
A) It would be eternal.
B) Humans would be unable to imitate it.
C) It would be similar to human speech and thus imitable.
D) It would not consist of letters.

Key Takeaway
The Quran's inimitability is a decisive proof that it is not created, because if it were, it would be imitable by humans.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazilites claimed they could produce better speech than the Quran. In what quiet ways do you prioritize your own thoughts, cultural wisdom, or intellectual idols over what Allah has said? Identify one area and resolve to submit it to the Quran this week.
?
Allah's challenge remains unanswered for 1400 years. Have you ever personally experienced the Quran's uniqueness in a way that silenced your doubts? If not, commit to a specific daily recitation with reflection for the next month, asking Allah to open your heart to its inimitability.

Proof from the Distinction Between Creation and Command
Baqillani uses Quran 7:54 to argue that the Quran is not created because God distinguishes between creation and command.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani uses Quran 7:54 to prove that the Quran is uncreated, based on the logical absurdity of conflating creation and command.

Core Idea
God's distinction between creation and command proves the Quran is not created.

Imam Baqillani presents a concise scriptural proof for the uncreatedness of the Quran. He points to a verse that clearly separates creation (khalq) from command (amr), arguing that if the Quran were created, the verse would become a self-defeating tautology. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Proof Passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice how Baqillani cites the verse and builds his argument.
The passage is short but dense. Pay attention to the logical flow.
2
Understand the Key Distinction
Identify the two terms: 'khalq' (creation) and 'amr' (command). Baqillani argues that the verse treats them as distinct. Why is that significant?
3
Follow the Conditional Argument
Baqillani says: 'If the Quran were created, it would be khalq, and then the verse would read: To Him belongs creation and creation.' This is the core logical step. See how he labels this as absurd speech.
This is the heart of the proof—the reductio ad absurdum.
4
Conclude with the Implication
The absurdity shows that the Quran cannot be created. Therefore, the Quran is 'command' (amr), not 'creation'. Reflect on how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل آخر: قوله عزّ وجلّ: الا له الخلق والأمر تبارك الله. ففصل بين الخلق والأمر. فلو كان القرآن مخلوقاً، لكان خلقاً لأن الخلق هو المخلوق فيصير الكلام في تقدير القول: «ألا له الخلق والخلق». وذلك عيّ من الكلام مستهجن مستغثّ، والله يتعالى عن التكلم والإخبار بما لا فائدة فيه. فدل ذلك على أن الأمر ليس بخلق.
Another proof: His saying, may He be glorified and exalted: 'Surely to Him belongs the creation and the command; blessed is God.' So He made a separation between creation and command. So if the Quran were created, it would be creation (khalq), because creation is the created thing; thus the speech would be, in the estimation of the saying: 'To Him belongs the creation and the creation.' That is a fault of speech, offensive and repugnant, and God is far above speaking and informing with no benefit. So that indicates that the command (amr) is not creation.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a proof (dalīl) from the Quran itself. Baqillani cites verse 7:54 where God says 'To Him belong creation and command' (ألا له الخلق والأمر). The verse separates creation (khalq) from command (amr). If the Quran were created, it would be part of 'creation', and the verse would redundantly read 'To Him belongs creation and creation', which is absurd speech (ʿayy min al-kalām) and devoid of benefit. Therefore, the Quran—being God's speech—is 'command' (amr), not 'creation', and thus not created.

Key Terms
الخلق
الأمر
مخلوق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Quran 7:54 simply lists two domains of God's activity without implying anything about the Quran's nature.
Correction: Baqillani argues that the verse's explicit distinction implies that the Quran, being God's speech (amr), cannot be part of creation (khalq), or else the verse would be a tautology, which is unworthy of God's speech.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what would be the implication if the Quran were created?
A) It would be part of 'command'.
B) It would be part of 'creation', leading to a tautology.
C) It would be uncreated.
D) It would be a miracle.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is 'command' (amr), not 'creation' (khalq), and therefore it is uncreated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly believe you are speaking the words of the eternal, uncreated God? How does that belief—or the lack of it—affect the way you read, memorize, and apply its verses?
?
If the Quran is not a created book but the very command of Allah, what does that mean for your obedience to it? Are you treating any of its commands as optional or outdated?
?
The verse distinguishes between 'creation' and 'command'. In your daily life, do you focus more on the created world around you or on the command of Allah that speaks to you through the Quran? Which one has a greater impact on your decisions?

Refuting the Objection from the 'Be' Verse
Baqillani enumerates and refutes four possibilities for God saying 'Be' to His speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the Quran cannot be created, focusing on his refutation of the objection based on the verse 'Be'.

Core Idea
God cannot say 'Be' to His speech, so the Quran is not created.

Baqillani addresses those who use the verse 'Our saying to a thing is "Be" and it is' (Quran 16:40) to argue that the Quran could be created. He first shows that the verse refers to God's creative command for created things, not to His own speech. Then he examines every possible way God might say 'Be' to His speech and finds each impossible. This lesson walks through his rigorous disproof.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
The objectors cite the 'Be' verse. Baqillani replies that the verse refers to what God creates, so if the Quran were created, God would be saying 'Be' to it, which is impossible.
2
Identify the four possibilities
When asked why it's impossible, Baqillani lists four ways God could address His speech with 'Be'. Find them in the text.
The four are: by essence, not by essence nor by another speech, by another speech, and by the speech itself.
3
Examine each refutation
For each possibility, Baqillani shows why it fails. Notice how he uses logical consequences and consensus.
Pay attention to the argument against infinite regress.
4
Conclude the proof
Since all possibilities are impossible, God cannot create His speech, so the Quran is uncreated.
This completes the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا فما وجه الاستدلال على نفي خلق القرآن بما قدمتموه من قوله : إنما قولنا لشيء إذا أردناه أن نقول له كن فيكون؟ قيل لهم وجه التعلق به هو أنه أخبر تعالى أنه يقول لما يخلقه «كن». فلو كان كلامه مخلوقاً، لكان قائلاً له «كن» وذلك محال باتفاق الأمة. فبطل أن يكون قوله «كن» مخلوقاً. فإن قالوا : من أين استحال أن يكون قائلاً لقوله؟ قيل لهم : لأنه لو كان قائلاً له، لم يخل من أن يقول له بنفسه تعالى، أو لا بنفسه ولا بقول آخر أو بقول آخر، أو بنفسه أعني بنفس القول فيستحيل أن يقول له بنفسه تعالى، لأن ذلك أن تكون نفسه يوجب قولاً، وأن يكون قائلاً لكل شيء بنفسه، وأن تكون نفسه لم تزل متكلمة، كما أنه لو كان عالماً بنفسه، لوجب أن تكون نفسه لم تزل عالمةً وهذا ما أبوه وكرهوه. ويستحيل أن يكون قائلاً لقوله لا بنفسه ولا بقول آخر، كما يستحيل أن يكون قائلاً لكل شيء كلّمه وقال له لا بنفسه ولا بقول. ويستحيل أن يكون قائلاً له بقول آخر، لأن ذلك يوجب تعلق كل قول بقول إلى غير غاية وذلك مما اتفقنا على فساده وإحالته. ويستحيل أن يكون قائلاً لقوله بنفس قوله لأن ذلك يوجب أن يكون قوله مخلوقاً، وأن يكون كل قول هو كن من جنسه مقولاً له بنفسه. وذلك محال، لأنه قد يقول «كن» من لا يعتقد أن له كلاماً أصلاً، إذا كان ممن ينفي الأعراض؛ فكيف يقول لقوله بنفس قوله من لا يعتقد أن له قولاً؟ وإذا فسد ذلك، استحال أن يخلق الله سبحانه كلامه وأن يقول لقوله.
If they say: What is the aspect of inference negating the createdness of the Quran from what you presented of His saying: 'Our saying to a thing when We intend it is that We say to it "Be" and it is'? Say to them: The aspect of attachment to it is that He, exalted, informed that He says to what He creates 'Be'. So if His speech were created, then He would be saying to it 'Be', and that is impossible by consensus of the Umma. So it is invalid that His saying 'Be' is created. If they say: From where is it impossible that He says to His saying? Say to them: Because if He were saying to it, it would not be free from that He says to it by His own essence, or not by His own essence and not by another saying, or by another saying, or by His own saying—I mean by the very saying. So it is impossible that He says to it by His own essence, because that would require that His essence entails a saying, and that He is a sayer of everything by Himself, and that His essence has never ceased to be speaking, just as if He were knowing by His own essence, it would be necessary that His essence has never ceased to be knowing—and this is what they refused and disliked. And it is impossible that He says to it not by His own essence and not by another saying, just as it is impossible that He says to everything He speaks to and says to it not by His own essence and not by a saying. And it is impossible that He says to it by another saying, because that would entail the attachment of every saying to a saying ad infinitum, which we agreed upon its corruption and impossibility. And it is impossible that He says to it by His own saying, because that would entail that His saying is created, and that every saying which is 'Be' is of its genus spoken to by itself. And that is impossible, because one who does not believe that he has any speech at all, if he is among those who negate accidents, may say 'Be'; so how can one who does not believe that he has a saying say to his saying by his own saying? And when that is corrupted, it is impossible that God, exalted, creates His speech and says to His saying.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer pair. The objectors cite the verse 'Our saying to a thing when We intend it is that We say to it "Be" and it is' (Quran 16:40) and ask how it supports the uncreatedness of the Quran. Baqillani responds that the verse shows God says 'Be' to what He creates (mā yakhluquhu). If the Quran were created, God would be saying 'Be' to His own speech, which is impossible by consensus. The objectors then ask: why is it impossible for God to say 'Be' to His speech? Baqillani enumerates the logical possibilities: (1) by His own essence, (2) not by His essence nor by another speech, (3) by another speech, or (4) by the speech itself. Each is shown impossible: (1) would imply God's essence produces speech eternally, which the objector (likely a Mu'tazili) rejects; (2) is incoherent; (3) leads to infinite regress; (4) would make the speech created, and the objector does not believe God has speech in the sense of a created attribute. Thus all possibilities are impossible, so God cannot address His speech with 'Be', and the Quran is not created.

Key Terms
كن
قول
بنفسه
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'Be' can be applied to the Quran because the Quran is a thing, so God could create it by saying 'Be'.
Correction: Baqillani shows the verse refers only to what God creates, not to His own speech. If the Quran were created, God would have to say 'Be' to His speech, which leads to impossible consequences.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani argue that God cannot say 'Be' to His own speech?
A) Because speech is incapable of hearing.
B) Because it would be saying to a saying, which leads to infinite regress or impossibility in all cases.
C) Because God does not speak.
D) Because the Quran is eternal and cannot be addressed.

Key Takeaway
God's speech cannot be created because any attempt to apply the 'Be' command to it leads to logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you truly believe that these words are from Allah Himself, uncreated and eternal? How does that conviction shape the awe and reverence you feel in your heart?
?
What would it mean for your trust in Allah if the Quran were a created thing, subject to change or error? Are there any areas of your life where you unknowingly treat His words as less than absolute?
?
Baqillani's argument is built on logical impossibility. How often do you apply careful reasoning to your beliefs about Allah, or do you sometimes accept contradictions without scrutiny? Will you commit to examining your faith with the same rigor?

The Impossibility of God Addressing His Own Speech
Baqillani proves God cannot address His speech via four impossibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that God cannot address His own speech, by examining the four logical possibilities and their refutations.

Core Idea
God cannot speak to His speech — every logical possibility leads to absurdity.

Baqillani presents a rigorous proof showing that it is impossible for God to address His own speech, such as saying 'Be' to His command. The argument works by enumerating every conceivable way such an address could occur and demonstrating that each leads to a logical contradiction. This lesson walks through each of those four possibilities and the reasons for their impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Problem
Recognize the question: Can God address His own speech (e.g., say 'Be' to His command)? Baqillani's opponents claimed He could. The lesson examines why this is impossible.
This is a key issue in divine attributes—whether God can speak to His own words as if they were separate.
2
Enumerate the Four Possibilities
If God were to address His speech, there are four logical modes: by His essence, not by essence nor by speech, by another speech, or by the speech itself. Each will be examined.
The Arabic terms: بنفسه, لا بنفسه ولا بقول آخر, بقول آخر, بنفس القول.
3
Examine the Refutations
Read the passage carefully. For each possibility, Baqillani shows a contradiction: essence leads to eternal speech; no means is incoherent; another speech causes infinite regress; speech itself makes speech created and self-addressing absurd.
Notice the analogy with knowledge by essence—it implies perpetuity.
4
Grasp the Conclusion
All four possibilities are impossible, so God cannot create or address His speech. This affirms that God's speech is uncreated and not subject to command.
The conclusion reinforces the orthodox Ash'ari position on the uncreatedness of the Qur'an.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : لأنه لو كان قائلاً له، لم يخل من أن يقول له بنفسه تعالى، أو لا بنفسه، لا بقول آخر أو بقول آخر، أو بنفسه أعني بنفس القول فيستحيل أن يقول له بنفسه تعالى، لأن ذلك أن تكون نفسه يوجب قولاً، وأن يكون قائلاً لكل شيء بنفسه، وأن تكون نفسه لم تزل متكلمة، كما أنه لو كان عالماً بنفسه، لوجب أن تكون نفسه لم تزل عالمة وهذا ما أبوه وكرهوه. ويستحيل أن يكون قائلاً لقوله لا بنفسه ولا بقول آخر، كما يستحيل أن يكون قائلاً لكل شيء كلّمه وقال له لا بنفسه ولا بقول. ويستحيل أن يكون قائلاً له بقول آخر، لأن ذلك يوجب تعلق كل قول بقول إلى غير غاية وذلك ممّا اتفقنا على فساده وإحالته. ويستحيل أن يكون قائلاً لقوله بنفس قوله لأن ذلك يوجب أن يكون قوله مخلوقاً، وأن يكون كل قول هو كن من جنسه مقولاً له بنفسه. وذلك محال، لأنه قد يقول «كن» من لا يعتقد أن له كلاماً أصلاً، إذا كان ممن ينفي الأعراض؛ فكيف يقول لقوله بنفس قوله من لا يعتقد أن له قولاً؟ وإذا فسد ذلك، استحال أن يخلق الله سبحانه كلامه وأن يقول لقوله.
It was said to them: Because if He were speaking to it, it would be inevitable that He speaks to it by Himself, or not by Himself, not by another speech or by another speech, or by Himself meaning by the speech itself. So it is impossible that He speaks to it by Himself, because that would require that His self necessitates a speech, and that He is speaking to everything by Himself, and that His self has always been speaking—just as if He were knowing by Himself, it would be necessary that His self has always been knowing—and this is what they rejected and disliked. And it is impossible that He speaks to His speech not by Himself and not by another speech, just as it is impossible that He speaks to everything He spoke to and said to it not by Himself and not by speech. And it is impossible that He speaks to it by another speech, because that would entail the attachment of every speech to a speech ad infinitum, and that is what we agreed on its corruption and impossibility. And it is impossible that He speaks to His speech by His speech itself, because that would entail that His speech is created, and that every speech is 'Be' from its genre spoken to it by itself. And that is impossible, because one who does not believe that he has any speech at all, if he is among those who deny accidents, may say 'Be'; so how could one who does not believe that he has a speech say to his speech by his speech itself? And if that is corrupted, it becomes impossible that God, exalted be He, creates His speech and speaks to His speech.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a proof by Baqillani demonstrating that it is impossible for God to address His own speech (i.e., to say 'Be' to His command or utterance). The argument proceeds by enumerating all possible ways such an address could occur and showing each to be impossible. First, if God were to address His speech, there are four logical possibilities: (1) He does so by His essence (i.e., without any instrument or distinct act), (2) not by His essence and not by another speech, (3) by another speech, or (4) by the speech itself. The proof eliminates each: (1) speaking by His essence would require His essence to be perpetually speaking, analogous to the attribute of knowledge—since knowledge by essence entails eternal knowledge, speech by essence would entail eternal speech, which contradicts the temporality of created speech. (2) Speaking not by essence and not by another speech is impossible because it would mean God speaks to something without any means, which is incoherent. (3) Speaking by another speech leads to an infinite regress of speeches, which is absurd. (4) Speaking by the speech itself would make that speech created and imply that every speech is a 'Be' that addresses itself, impossible because someone who denies accidents may utter 'Be' without having a speech attribute; it is absurd to think a speech can address itself. Therefore, all possibilities are impossible, so God can neither create His speech nor address it.

Key Terms
كلام (speech)
قول (utterance)
نفسه (by Himself)
بنفس القول (by the speech itself)
بلا نهاية (ad infinitum)
أعراض (accidents)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God can address His command (e.g., 'Be') as if it were a separate entity, similar to a king commanding a subject.
Correction: Baqillani shows that this leads to logical absurdities such as infinite regress, created speech, or requiring God to have eternal speech—all of which are unacceptable.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what follows if God were to address His speech by another speech?
A) It would imply God's speech is eternal.
B) It would lead to an infinite regress of speeches.
C) It would mean God's speech is created.
D) It would contradict God's omnipotence.

Key Takeaway
All possible ways for God to address His speech are logically impossible, proving that God cannot speak to His own words.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech is eternal and uncreated, then the Quran you recite is not merely a historical message but an attribute of God. Does that truth change how you prepare your heart before reciting? What would it mean for you to approach every verse with the awareness that you are encountering God's very attribute?
?
The argument shows that any attempt to treat God's speech as something separate from Him leads to absurdity. Have you ever, even unintentionally, thought of the Quran as a 'thing' God created rather than His eternal word? How might that subtle shift affect your reverence and submission to its commands?
?
Baqillani proves that God cannot 'speak to His speech.' But do you sometimes act as if you can negotiate with God's commands—as if they are distant from Him and therefore negotiable? What is one command of God that you have been treating as less than eternal? Will you now submit to it without reservation?

Refuting the Metaphor Claim for God's Command 'Be'
Baqillani refutes the claim that God's command 'Be' is metaphorical and gives the first reason.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani rejects the metaphorical interpretation of God's command 'Be' and the first key reason based on the nature of the entities described as speaking.

Core Idea
Metaphorical speech requires a literal speaker; inanimate objects and animals cannot speak.

In this passage, Baqillani addresses an objection from those who claim that God's saying 'Be' to creation is metaphorical, like other figurative expressions in Arabic. He presents the first of several reasons: the entities used in those metaphors (like eyes, walls, camels) are inanimate or animals that literally cannot speak. This undermines the analogy to God's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Recognize the opposition's use of poetic and Quranic examples to support a metaphorical interpretation of God's command.
The objection cites verses and poetry where non-human entities are described as speaking.
2
Understand the examples
Notice that all examples involve non-human entities (eyes, a wall, a camel, a tank) attributed with speech or will.
3
Grasp Baqillani's first reason
He points out that these entities are inanimate (jamad) or animals (hayawan), which cannot literally speak, so the metaphorical usage cannot be extended to God.
The argument is that the metaphor works only because the listener knows the entity cannot speak literally.
4
Reflect on the implication
This shows that the opponents' analogy fails because God's speech is real and not metaphorical.
The first reason is the beginning of a multi-reason refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة فإن قالوا : ما أنكرتم أن يكون قوله: {أَنْ نَقُولَ لَهُ كُنْ} مجازاً واتساعاً؟ وهو بمنزلة قوله: {أَتَيْنَا طَائِعِينَ} وقوله: {جداراً يُرِيدُ أَنْ يَنقَضَّ} وبمثابة قول الشاعر: وَقَالَتْ لَهُ الْعَيْنَانِ سَمْعاً وَطَاعَةً وَأَحْدَرَنَا كَالدُّرّ لَمَّا يُنَضِّدُ وقول الآخر: وتُخْبِرُنِي الْعَيْنَانِ مَا الْقَلْبُ كَاتِمُ وقول الآخر: يَشْكُو إِلَى جَمَلِي طُولَ السُّرَى صَبْراً جَمِيلًا فَكِلانَا مُبْتَلَى وقول الآخر: فَازْوَرَّ مِنْ وَقْعِ الْقَنَا بِلُبَانِهِ وَشَكَا إِلَيَّ بِعَبْرَةٍ وَتَحَمْحُم وقولهم: امْتَلَأَ الْحَوْضُ وَقَالَ قَطْنِي مَهْلًا رُوَيْداً قَدْ مَلَأْتِ بَطْنِي في أمثال هذا مما ورد مجازاً واتساعاً . قيل لهم : أنكرنا ذلك لأمور. أحدهما أن هذه الأمور التي وُصفت بالكلام والقول والإخبار منها جماد يستحيل أن يتكلم عندكم، ومنها حيوان
Issue: For if they say: What prevents you that His saying 'that We say to it: Be' is metaphorical and figurative? And it is like His saying 'We came willingly' and His saying 'a wall that wants to collapse' and like the poet's saying: 'And the two eyes said to him hearing and obedience and they lowered us like pearls not yet strung' and the other's saying: 'And the two eyes inform me what the heart hides' and the other's saying: 'He complains to my camel of the length of the night journey, patience beautiful, both of us are afflicted' and the other's saying: 'He turned aside from the blow of the spear on his chest and complained to me with a tear and clearing the throat' and their saying: 'The tank filled and said: Enough for me, gently, slowly, you have filled my belly' and similar instances that occurred as metaphor and figurative speech. It is said to them: We reject that for reasons. The first: these things that are described with speech, utterance, and informing include inanimate objects that cannot speak according to you, and animals
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the beginning of Baqillani's response. Opponents argue that the phrase 'We said to it: Be' (from Qur'an 41:11) can be interpreted metaphorically (majaz) and figuratively, just as many other verses and poetic expressions use speech metaphorically. They cite examples: Qur'anic verses like 'We came willingly' (41:11) and 'a wall that wants to collapse' (18:77), and several poetic lines where eyes, camels, or a tank are described as speaking, complaining, or informing. The implication is that God's 'saying' to creation could be similar metaphorical attribution. Baqillani refutes this by giving multiple reasons, but the text only provides the first: these objects and animals described as speaking are, in reality, incapable of speech. Inanimate objects (jamad) cannot speak at all, and animals (hayawan) cannot speak in the way humans do. So the metaphorical attribution relies on a known literal capability in the speaker, but God's speech is not analogous to these because there is no literal speech in those objects. The first reason undermines the analogy: the examples involve entities that literally do not speak, whereas God does speak literally; hence the metaphor fails to prove anything.

Key Terms
مجاز (metaphor)
اتساع (figurative speech)
قول (utterance)
جماد (inanimate)
حيوان (animal)
استحالة (impossibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any figurative language in the Qur'an proves God's speech is figurative.
Correction: Baqillani shows the examples involve entities incapable of speech, so the comparison is invalid. God's speech is literal.

Checkpoint
What is the first reason Baqillani gives for rejecting the metaphorical interpretation of God's command 'Be'?
A) Metaphors are not found in the Qur'an.
B) The objects described as speaking are inanimate or animals that cannot literally speak.
C) The poetic examples are not valid analogies.
D) God's speech is eternal and cannot be metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
God's command 'Be' is literal, not metaphorical, because the metaphorical examples involve entities that cannot literally speak, unlike God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you read the Qur'an, do you truly believe that every word is literally from God, or do you sometimes treat His speech as metaphorical to avoid its weight on your actions? How would your life change if you internalized that God's command 'Be' is literal and immediate?
?
The opponents cited poetic metaphors as excuses. Are there areas of your faith where you use intellectual excuses to avoid submitting to the literal commands of God? What would it look like to take His speech at face value starting today?
?
Baqillani's refutation shows that metaphorical speech in creatures is based on their incapacity. But God is all-powerful and speaks literally. How does this affect your trust in the Qur'an's promises and warnings? Will you respond with literal obedience today, without diluting His words?

Refutation of 'Fa' Implying Immediate Sequence
Baqillani refutes the claim that 'fa' in 'Kun fayakun' implies immediate sequence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the particle 'fa' in 'Kun fayakun' does not necessarily imply immediate sequence, as argued by Imam Baqillani.

Core Idea
'Fa' does not always mean immediate sequence; context matters.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani responds to an objection that the particle 'fa' in the Quranic phrase 'Kun fayakun' (Be, and it becomes) indicates immediate sequence, which would imply that creation is temporal. He uses examples from Arabic usage and Quranic verses to show that 'fa' in commands and conditionals does not necessitate immediate sequence. This lesson will walk through his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Recognize the opponent's claim that 'fa' always implies 'ta'qib' (immediate sequence) and the implication that anything preceded by a finite time is created.
The objection is based on a linguistic rule for 'fa' when used for coordination (nasq).
2
Identify Baqillani's Counterexamples
Read the examples: 'Do not hurt me so that I hurt you', 'Whoever returns, Allah will take vengeance on him', etc. Notice that none imply immediate sequence.
These examples show 'fa' indicating requital or consequence, not temporal immediacy.
3
Examine the Distinction
Understand the difference between 'fa' for coordination (nasq) and 'fa' in answer to a command (jawab al-amr). The latter does not require immediate sequence.
Baqillani cites the example of a master commanding 'When you enter Mecca, buy me a slave...' – the purchase is not immediate upon entry.
4
Apply to 'Kun fayakun'
See that 'fa' in 'Kun fayakun' is the answer to the command 'Be', so it does not indicate immediate sequence but rather response and obedience.
Baqillani also references Arab usage: a master commanding a slave to act later does not imply immediate obedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كُنْ» بحق قوله «فَيَكُونُ». لأن الفاء موضوع في اللغة للتعقيب، وقد اتفقنا على أن ما لم يسبق المحدّث إلا بقدر زمان واحد أو أزمان متناهية فمحدث مخلوق. قيل لهم: لا يجب ما قلتم لأن الفاء وإن كان إذا جاء للنسق أوجب الترتيب والتعقيب، فإنه لا يوجب ذلك في جواب الأمر وجواب جملة تقدمت ولا في الجزاء أيضاً. لأن القائل إذا قال «لا تسؤني فأسوك» ليس يريد به التعقيب وإنما يقصد الإخبار عن إيقاع المجازاة فقط. وكذلك لم يوجب قوله تعالى: «ومن عاد فينتقم الله منه» التعقيب. وكذلك قوله: «فيسحتكم بعذاب» لا يوجب التعقيب. فأما الفاء الداخل في جواب الأمر وجواب جملة الكلام، فلا خلاف بينهم في أنه لا يوجب التعقيب. لأن القائل إذا قال لعبده: «إذا دخلت مكة فاشتر لي عبداً وبعيراً وثوباً»، فليس يريد تعقيب شراء العبد بدخوله. وكذلك قوله: «إذا قمتم إلى الصلاة فاغسلوا وجوهكم وأيديكم» لم يُرد به تعقيب القيام بغسل الوجه دون غيره من الأعضاء. كذلك قوله: «أَنْ نَقُولَ لَهُ كُنْ فَيَكُونُ» جواب قوله «كن»، وهو الأمر، فلم يقتض التعقيب لأن ليس هذا فاء التعقيب. ويدل على ذلك أيضاً أن العرب تقول: «إنما جاءك زيد مع عبده أن يأمره بالفعل فيفعله». وهو لا يريد بذلك تعقيب إيقاع الفعل عقيب الأمر. لأنه قد يأمره بأن يفعل الفعل بعد حول وشهر ولا يقتضي الأمر تعقيب الأمر به معجلاً، لأن ذلك عصيان. وإنما المراد بقولهم «فيفعل» طاعته. وإذا كان ذلك كذلك، بطل ما توهموه من كون الفاء موجباً للتعقيب في كل مكان.
His saying: 'Be' and it becomes. Because the particle 'fa' is placed in the language for immediate sequence, and we have agreed that whatever is preceded by the originated (al-muhdath) only by the measure of one time or finite times is originated and created. It was said to them: What you have said is not necessarily the case, because the particle 'fa', even if when it comes for coordination (al-nasaq) necessitates order and immediate sequence, it does not necessitate that in the answer to a command (jawab al-amr) and the answer to a preceding sentence, nor in the apodosis (al-jaza') either. Because the speaker when he says: 'Do not hurt me so that I hurt you', he does not intend immediate sequence but rather intends only to inform about the occurrence of requital. Likewise, His saying, the Exalted: 'And whoever returns, Allah will take vengeance on him' did not necessitate immediate sequence. Likewise, His saying: 'So that He may destroy you with a punishment' does not necessitate immediate sequence. As for the 'fa' that enters in the answer to a command and the answer to a sentence of speech, there is no disagreement among them that it does not necessitate immediate sequence. Because the speaker when he says to his slave: 'When you enter Mecca, buy me a slave, a camel, and a garment', he does not intend immediate sequence of the purchase of the slave upon his entry. Likewise, His saying: 'When you rise for prayer, wash your faces and your hands' did not intend immediate sequence of standing with washing the face over other limbs. Likewise, His saying: 'That We say to it: Be, and it becomes' is an answer to His saying 'Be', and it is the command, so it does not necessitate immediate sequence because this is not the 'fa' of immediate sequence. And this is also indicated by the fact that the Arabs say: 'Zaid only came to you with his slave so that he commands him to do the deed and he does it.' He does not intend thereby immediate sequence of the occurrence of the deed consequent upon the command. Because he may command him to do the deed after a year or a month, and the command does not necessitate that the command be executed immediately, because that would be disobedience. Rather, what is meant by their saying 'and he does it' is his obedience. And if that is the case, then what they imagined, that the particle 'fa' necessitates immediate sequence in every place, is invalid.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection concerning the particle 'fa' (فاء) in the Quranic phrase 'Kun fayakun' (Be, and it becomes). The opponents argue that 'fa' indicates immediate sequence (ta'qib), so if something is not preceded by an older thing except by one time or finite times, it is created (hadith). Baqillani responds by explaining that 'fa' does not always imply immediate sequence. He gives several examples to show that 'fa' in the answer to a command (jawab al-amr) or in the apodosis of a conditional does not necessarily indicate immediate sequence. For instance, when someone says 'Do not hurt me so that I hurt you', the 'fa' does not mean immediately after, but rather indicates consequence in a general sense. Similarly, Quranic verses like 'And whoever returns, Allah will take vengeance on him' and 'So that He may destroy you with a punishment' do not imply immediate sequence. He further explains that the 'fa' in 'Kun fayakun' is the answer to the command 'Be', and thus does not necessitate immediate sequence. He cites Arab usage: a master commanding his slave to buy items when he enters Mecca does not intend the purchase to happen immediately upon entry. Likewise, the verse about washing for prayer does not mean immediate sequence of washing after standing. He concludes by citing a common Arab expression: 'Zaid only came to you with his slave so that he commands him to do the deed and he does it', which does not imply immediate action but rather obedience. Thus, the opponents' claim that 'fa' always implies immediate sequence is invalid.

Key Terms
فاء التعقيب
جواب الأمر
التعقيب
الجزاء
النسق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The particle 'fa' always implies immediate sequence (ta'qib).
Correction: Baqillani shows that 'fa' in answer to a command or conditional does not necessitate immediate sequence; it can indicate consequence, requital, or obedience without temporal immediacy.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, does the particle 'fa' in the context of a command always imply immediate sequence?
A) Yes, always.
B) No, it depends on context; in commands it often does not imply immediate sequence.
C) Only in conditional sentences.
D) Only in the Quranic verse 'Kun fayakun'.

Key Takeaway
The particle 'fa' in 'Kun fayakun' does not imply immediate sequence; it indicates response and obedience, not temporal immediacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Grammatical Objection to Eternal Speech: 'an' + Present Verb Indicates Future
Opponents argue that 'an' (أن) with a present verb indicates future, so the speech is originated

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' grammatical argument that the particle 'an' (أن) with a present verb indicates future tense, and how they use this to claim that divine speech is originated.

Core Idea
Grammar alone cannot decide theology—even opponents use it to argue.

The debate over whether divine speech is eternal or originated often turns on subtle linguistic points. In this passage, opponents of the Ash'ari position deploy a grammatical rule: when the light 'an' (أن الخفيفة) is followed by a present tense verb, the resulting verbal noun (masdar) refers to the future. They apply this to the Qur'anic verse 'an naqula lahu kun fayakun' (that We say to it: Be, and it becomes) to argue that the speech (qawl) mentioned there is future and therefore originated. This objection challenges the view that Allah's speech is eternal and uncreated. Let us examine their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how the opponents begin with a question formula ('fain qalu') and then present their grammatical evidence.
The passage is an objection, not a complete argument; the author's response is not included here.
2
Identify the grammatical rule
The opponents cite the rule: light 'an' with a verb functions like a masdar. If the verb is past, the masdar is past; if present, the masdar is future. They give examples: 'It pleased me that you stood' (past) and 'It pleases me that you stand' (future).
Note that they claim 'an' with present verb cannot refer to the present moment (hal).
3
Apply the rule to the verse
The verse contains 'an naqula' – 'an' + present verb 'naqula' (we say). According to the rule, this indicates a future saying. Therefore, the speech (qawl) is in the future, implying it is originated (hadith) and not eternal.
4
Understand the conclusion
The opponents conclude that the verse 'an naqula lahu kun fayakun' is evidence for the origination (huduth) of divine speech. This directly challenges the Ash'ari doctrine of eternal speech.
Remember that this is only the objection; the author will provide a refutation later.
5
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself: Does the grammatical rule necessarily apply to Allah's speech in the same way as human speech? What assumptions are the opponents making? This reflection prepares you for the author's response.
This lesson focuses on the objection itself, not its refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
فإن قالوا : فما أنكرتم أن يكون قوله : «أَنْ نَقُولَ لَهُ كُنْ فَيَكُونُ» دلالةً على حدث الكلام واستئنافه؟ لأن أهل العربية قالوا: «أن» الخفيفة إذا دخلت مع الفعل كانت معه بمنزلة المصدر فإن كان الفعل ماضياً، كان معنى المصدر ماضياً كقولك «سرِّني أن قمت»، معناه : سرني قيامك . وإن دخلت على فعل مضارع كان المصدر للاستقبال كقولك: «يعجبني أن تقوم»، فيكون معناه : يعجبني قيامك في المستقبل. ويحسن فيه ذكر «غداً»، ويحسن في المصدر لما مضى ذكر «أمس». ولا يجوز أن يكون الفعل الواقع بعد أن الخفيفة للحال. فوجب أن يكون قوله تعالى: «أن نقول له كن فيكون» دلالةً على استقبال القول وحدوثه.
A question: For if they say: What prevents you from accepting that His saying: 'That We say to it: Be, and it becomes' is an indication of the origination (hadath) of speech (kalam) and its renewal? Because the Arabic grammarians said: The light 'an' (أن الخفيفة) when it enters with a verb, it becomes with it like a masdar. If the verb is past, the meaning of the masdar is past, like your saying: 'It pleased me that you stood', its meaning: Your standing pleased me. And if it enters on a present verb, the masdar is for the future, like your saying: 'It pleases me that you stand', so its meaning: Your standing in the future pleases me. And it is appropriate to mention 'tomorrow' in it, and it is appropriate to mention 'yesterday' in the masdar for what is past. And it is not permissible that the verb occurring after the light 'an' be for the present (al-hal). Therefore, it becomes necessary that His saying, the Exalted: 'That We say to it: Be, and it becomes' is an indication of the futurity of the speech (qawl) and its origination (huduth).
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (likely from opponents of the Ash'ari position) regarding the speech of Allah. The opponents argue that the verse 'an naqula lahu kun fayakun' (that We say to it: Be, and it becomes) indicates that the speech (qawl) is originated (hadith) and renewed (musta'naf). Their reasoning is based on Arabic grammar: the particle 'an' (أن) when combined with a verb functions like a verbal noun (masdar). If the verb is past tense, the masdar refers to the past; if the verb is present tense, the masdar refers to the future. They give examples: 'It pleased me that you stood' (past) and 'It pleases me that you stand' (future). In the verse, the verb after 'an' is 'naqula' (we say), which is present tense, so the masdar (say-saying) is in the future. Therefore, they conclude, the act of saying (qawl) is in the future, meaning it is originated (hadith) and not eternal. They also note that a present verb after 'an' cannot refer to the present moment (al-hal), so the speech cannot be eternal or present. This objection is posed against an opponent who holds that the divine speech is eternal. The author's response is not included in this passage.

المصدر
الفعل المضارع
الاستقبال
حدث الكلام

B) Present tense

D) Continuous action

Key Takeaway
The opponents use Arabic grammar to argue that the divine speech mentioned in the verse is originated (hadith) because the particle 'an' with a present verb indicates future tense.

Proof that the Quran is Created
The author concludes that the Quran is created based on the createdness of all other speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument from consensus that all speech is created, and why the Quran is included in that category.

Core Idea
All speech is created, so the Quran must be created too.

This passage presents a concise argument for the createdness of the Quran based on the nature of speech (dhikr) and scholarly consensus. The author reasons that since every instance of speech other than the Quran is agreed to be created, the Quran itself falls under the same category. Pay close attention to the logical steps he uses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the translation. Notice the compact style and the key terms dhikr (mention/speech) and hadath (createdness).
2
Identify the premises
The passage makes two key claims: (1) describing something as 'created' indicates that there is something else of its kind that is not created, and (2) by consensus, every instance of mention/speech other than the Quran is created. Find these in the text.
3
Trace the logical deduction
Follow the argument: if all other speech is created, and the description of speech as 'created' points to some speech being uncreated, then the only candidate for uncreated speech cannot be other speech—so it must be the Quran. But the author concludes the opposite: since the description applies to all speech, the Quran must be created. Work through this reasoning.
This is a subtle point; the author is arguing that because the Quran is the only speech not covered by the consensus, it must be created like the rest.
4
Consider the disagreement
The author notes that the dispute is about the speech of Allah. He claims the verse supports his view more strongly. Think about why this argument would be convincing to someone who already accepts that all other speech is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إلا ذكراً والهاشمي لا يكن إلا شريفاً. فوجب أن يكون نعت الذكر بالحدوث دلالةً على أنه منه ما ليس بمحدث. فيجب أن يكون هو القرآن للإجماع على أن كل ما عداه من الذكر محدث. واختلافنا في كلام الله سبحانه، والآية بأن تدلّ على قولنا أقرب.
Except as mention, and the Hashimi is not except noble. So it is necessary that the description of mention by createdness be an indication that there is from it that which is not created. So it is necessary that it be the Quran, by consensus that everything other than it from mention is created. And our disagreement is about the speech of Allah exalted, and the verse indicates our saying more closely.

Commentary
This passage is a logical proof for the createdness of the Quran. The author establishes a premise: all speech (ذكر) other than the Quran is created by consensus. Then he argues that if speech is described as created, that description indicates that there is some speech that is not created—but since all other speech is created, the only possible exception is the Quran itself. Therefore, the Quran must be created. He concludes that the verse in question supports his position more strongly.

Correction: The author argues that createdness is a property of all speech, including the Quran. The fact that the Quran is divine does not exempt it from being created; rather, the consensus that all other speech is created implies that the Quran is also created.

The Versatile Meaning of 'Amr' in the Quran
Answering an objection about the meaning of 'amr' in a Quranic verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'amr' in the Quran can refer to actions and decrees, not merely verbal commands, and how this refutes a common objection against divine speech being uncreated.

Core Idea
'Amr' is not always speech; it often means an affair or decree.

The objection that 'the command of Allah is accomplished' proves divine speech is created rests on a linguistic misunderstanding. The author responds by showing that 'amr' in Arabic can mean actions, affairs, or decrees—not just verbal commands. This lesson unpacks his argument using Quranic verses and poetry, clarifying the distinction between 'amr' meaning 'affair' and 'amr' meaning 'command'.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and initial response
Start by reading the objector's question and the author's direct reply. Notice how the author immediately shifts the meaning of 'amr' from speech to action.
The key is to see the author's strategy: he reinterprets the verse in context.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
The author cites two other Quranic uses of 'amr' that clearly mean 'affair' or 'punishment', not speech. Identify these examples and note how they support the broader meaning.
This is the core of the linguistic argument.
3
Consider the poetic evidence
Read the two lines of poetry the author provides. They show that even in common usage, 'amr' can mean personal affairs without implying a spoken command.
Poetry is used as authoritative evidence in classical Arabic.
4
Learn the plural distinction
The author ends with a grammatical point: the plural of 'amr' meaning 'affair' is 'umūr', while the plural of 'amr' meaning 'command' is 'awāmir'. This solidifies the distinction.
This rule helps avoid confusion in other verses.
5
Reflect on the motive
The author concludes that the objectors' misinterpretation arises from incapacity to accept the truth. Consider how forced interpretations can arise from theological bias.
This is a theological warning, not just a linguistic point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قوله تعالى : وكان أمر الله مفعولاً و قدراً مقدوراً؟ قيل لهم : أراد تعالى عقابه وانتقامه من الكافرين، ونصره للمؤمنين ومن حكم به وقدره من الأفعال ومن ذلك قوله تعالى جاء أمرنا، وقوله : وما أمر فرعون برشيد يعني شأنه وأفعاله وطرائقه . قال الشاعر : لها أمرها حتى إذا ما تبوأت بأخفافها مرعى تبراً مضجعا وقال آخر: فقلت لها أمري إلى الله كله وإني إليه في الإياب لراجع يعني «شؤوني وأفعالي ولم يرد بذلك الأمر الذي هو القول. هذا «أمور» وجمع الأمر من القول «أوامر». ولولا العجز، لم يلجأوا إلى مثل هذا التمويه.
If they say: What is the meaning of His statement exalted: 'And the command of Allah was an accomplished deed and a determined decree'? It is said to them: He exalted meant His punishment and vengeance upon the disbelievers, and His victory for the believers, and from that which He judged and decreed of actions. And from that is His statement: 'Our command came', and His statement: 'And the command of Pharaoh was not right' meaning his affair and his actions and his ways. The poet said: 'She has her command until when she settled with her hooves a pasture of gold a resting place.' And another said: 'I said to her: My command is entirely to Allah, and indeed to Him in return I am returning' meaning 'my affairs and my actions' and he did not intend by that the command which is speech. This is 'affairs', and the plural of command from speech is 'commands'. And if not for incapacity, they would not resort to such obfuscation.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors cite the verse 'And the command of Allah is accomplished' to argue that 'command' (أمر) is an act of speech, implying that divine speech is created. The author counters that 'amr' here means action, affair, or decree, not speech. He supports this with other Quranic verses where 'amr' means affair or action (e.g., 'Our command came' meaning the punishment, and 'the command of Pharaoh' meaning his way). He also cites poetry where 'amr' means personal affairs. Finally, he notes that the plural of 'amr' meaning speech is 'أوامر' (commands), while the plural of 'amr' meaning affair is 'أمور' (affairs). The claim that 'amr' in the verse refers to speech is a forced interpretation due to inability to accept the truth.

Key Terms
أمر
مفعول
قدر
أمور
أوامر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'And the command of Allah is accomplished' proves that divine speech is created because 'amr' always means a verbal command.
Correction: The term 'amr' in this verse refers to Allah's decree and actions, not to speech. The same word can mean 'affair' or 'action' in many Quranic and Arabic contexts.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the objection that 'And the command of Allah is accomplished' refers to speech?
A) By denying the verse
B) By showing that 'amr' here means action or affair
C) By claiming the verse is abrogated
D) By arguing that 'amr' is metaphorical

Key Takeaway
The Arabic word 'amr' has a broad semantic range; interpreting it as 'speech' in every occurrence leads to theological errors that are corrected by linguistic evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'And the command of Allah was an accomplished deed,' do you automatically assume it refers to His speech? What does this reveal about how you approach the Quran—do you let the context define the meaning, or do you bring preconceived interpretations?
?
The author accuses the objectors of resorting to 'obfuscation' due to 'incapacity.' Is there any area of your own understanding of Allah's attributes where you might be forcing a meaning rather than accepting the clear language? What would it cost you to surrender that interpretation?
?
If 'amr' can mean both speech and decree, how does this affect your daily trust in Allah's command? Do you live as though His decree is already accomplished, or do you worry as if it might fail? What would change if you truly believed His 'amr' is always 'maf'ul'?
