The Two Meanings of 'Ja'ala' in the Quran
Imam al-Baqillani explains the linguistic rule of 'ja'ala' with one vs two objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Arabic verb 'ja'ala' changes meaning based on transitivity, and why the verse 'We have made it an Arabic Quran' does not imply the Quran is created.

Core Idea
'Ja'ala' with two objects means naming, not creating.

The verse 'We have made it an Arabic Quran' might lead some to think the Quran is created, since 'ja'ala' often means 'to make'. However, Imam al-Baqillani explains that 'ja'ala' can mean either creation or naming, depending on whether it takes one or two objects. This lesson unpacks his linguistic argument from the Quran itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the opening objection and the author's direct response. Notice how he immediately clarifies the intended meaning.
The objection assumes 'ja'ala' means creation, but the author disagrees.
2
Examine the Quranic examples
The author provides two examples where 'ja'ala' appears with two objects: 'those who made the Quran into portions' and 'they made the angels females'. In both cases, the meaning is naming/judging, not creation.
Focus on the phrase 'they named it' (سموه) in the commentary.
3
Understand the linguistic rule
Learn the key rule: 'ja'ala' with one object means creation, but with two objects it usually means naming or judging. The author cites Quranic verses to support this.
Example with one object: 'made darkness and light'. Example with two: 'made the son of Mary and his mother a sign'.
4
Apply the rule to the verse
Now apply the rule: In 'إنا جعلناه قرآناً عربياً', 'ja'ala' is transitive to two objects ('it' and 'Arabic Quran'), so it means 'We named it an Arabic Quran'. The Quran is not created.
This refutes the objection about the Quran being created.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider the broader theological point: if 'ja'ala' here meant creation, it would contradict the Islamic belief that the Quran is the uncreated speech of Allah. The linguistic analysis protects this doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قوله : إنا جعلناه قرآناً عربياً ؟ قيل لهم : معنى ذلك : إنا جعلنا العبارة عنه بلسان العرب، وأفهمنا أحكامه والمراد به باللسان العربي، وسميناه عربياً لأن الجعل قد يكون بمعنى التسمية والحكم. قال الله عزّ وجلّ : الذين جعلوا القرآن عضين يعني : سموه كذباً وحكموا عليه بذلك؛ ولم يرد أنهم خلقوه. وقال: وجعلوا الملائكة الذين هم عباد الرحمن إناثاً يعني : سموهم بذلك وحكموا لهم به؛ ولم يرد أنهم خلقوهم إناثاً. والجعل إذا عُدّي إلى مفعول واحد كان بمعنى الفعل لا محالة؛ وإذا عُدّي إلى مفعولين صار بمعنى الحكم والتسمية في أكثر الاستعمال مثل قوله تعالى : وجعلنا ابن مريم وأمه آية وأويناهما، وهذا بمعنى الخلق؛ ومثل قوله : وجعلنا الليل والنهار آيتين وأما الذي يُعدى إلى مفعول واحد، فمثل قوله: الحمد لله الذي خلق السموات والأرض وجعل الظلمات والنور. ولذلك لم يجز أن يقول قائل : جعلت النجم والرجل»، ويقطع حتى يصله بقوله: «جعلت النجم هادياً ودليلا، وجعلت الرجل صديقاً ومتألفاً». وقوله : إنا جعلناه قرآناً عربياً متعد إلى مفعولين، فصار بمعنى الحكم والتسمية.
If they say: What is the meaning of His statement: 'We have made it an Arabic Quran'? It is said to them: The meaning of that is: We made the expression about it in the tongue of the Arabs, and We made understand its rulings and the intended meaning in the Arabic tongue, and We named it Arabic because 'ja'ala' can be in the sense of naming and judging. Allah almighty said: 'Those who made the Quran into portions' meaning they named it lies and judged it as such; He did not mean that they created it. And He said: 'And they made the angels, who are servants of the Most Merciful, females' meaning they named them that and judged that for them; He did not mean that they created them as females. And 'ja'ala' when transitive to one object is inevitably in the sense of creation; and when transitive to two objects it becomes in the sense of judging and naming in most usage, like His statement: 'And We made the son of Mary and his mother a sign and gave them refuge' and this is in the sense of creation; and like His statement: 'And We made the night and day two signs'. As for what is transitive to one object, like His statement: 'Praise be to Allah who created the heavens and the earth and made darkness and light'. Therefore it is not permissible for one to say: 'I made the star and the man', and stop, until he connects it with his saying: 'I made the star a guide and a guide, and I made the man a friend and a companion'. And His statement: 'We have made it an Arabic Quran' is transitive to two objects, so it becomes in the sense of judging and naming.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer about the verse 'إنا جعلناه قرآناً عربياً' (We have made it an Arabic Quran). The objectors might argue that 'ja'ala' here means creation, implying the Quran is created. The author answers that 'ja'ala' when transitive to two objects indicates naming or judging, not creating. He proves this with Quranic examples: 'those who made the Quran into portions' means they named it lies, not created it; 'they made the angels females' means they called them females, not created them. He then explains the linguistic rule: 'ja'ala' with one object means creation (e.g., 'made darkness and light'), but with two objects it means naming or judging (e.g., 'made the sun and moon as signs'). The verse 'We have made it an Arabic Quran' has two objects ('it' and 'Arabic Quran'), so it means 'We named it an Arabic Quran and judged it as such', not that We created it. He further illustrates by noting that one cannot say 'I made the star and the man' without specifying what they were made into, showing that 'ja'ala' with two objects requires a second object as a predicate.

Key Terms
جعل
عربي
تسمية
حكم
مفعولين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verb 'ja'ala' always means 'to create', so 'We have made it an Arabic Quran' implies the Quran is created.
Correction: The passage shows that 'ja'ala' with two objects means 'to name or judge', not 'to create'. The Quran is uncreated.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'ja'ala' mean when it is transitive to two objects?
A) To create
B) To name or judge
C) To destroy
D) To change

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'ja'ala' depends on its grammatical structure: with one object it is creation, with two it is naming or judging, a key principle for understanding Quranic verses about divine actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse like 'We have made it an Arabic Quran', do you ever pause to consider the precise meaning of each word, or do you rely on a familiar translation? How often do you check the original Arabic to ensure you understand what Allah is telling you?
?
Al-Baqillani uses grammar to defend the uncreatedness of the Quran. Have you ever used your knowledge of Arabic or Islamic sciences to defend the truth against misconceptions? What step can you take this week to strengthen your understanding of the language of the Quran?
?
The objectors twisted a verse to support a false belief. Are there verses that you might be misinterpreting because you assume you already know their meaning? Will you commit to studying Tafsir from reliable scholars to correct any misunderstandings in your own practice?

Refutation of the Claim that Divine Speech Must Be Sounds
Baqillani refutes the analogy from sensory experience and defends inner speech (kalam nafsi).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine speech cannot be reduced to sounds and letters, and how Baqillani establishes speech as an inner meaning subsisting in the soul.

Core Idea
Real speech is inner meaning, not outer sounds.

The objection that God's speech must be composed of letters and sounds arises from a mistaken equation of all speech with sensory experience. Baqillani first shows that such reasoning would lead to absurdities about God's essence, then corrects the very definition of speech. This passage thus clarifies a fundamental principle: divine speech is an eternal attribute, not a temporal composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the first refutation
Read from 'If they say...' to 'contradicts sensory evidence'. Notice how Baqillani uses a counter-analogy: if we judge God by sensory experience alone, we would have to say He is a body, which is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
2
Study the correction of the definition of speech
Read from 'Then it is said to them...' to the end. Baqillani argues that speech in our own experience is a meaning in the soul expressed variably by sounds. This shows that true speech is not the sounds themselves.
The variety of expression for the same meaning is key.
3
Ask: How does this defend divine speech?
Consider how defining speech as inner meaning allows God's speech to be eternal and not composed of created sounds. This is the Ash'ari doctrine of kalam nafsi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا فيجب على كل حال أن تقولوا إن كلام الله أصوات وحروف، متبعض متغاير، لأنكم لم تعقلوا كلاماً إلا كذلك. ويجب أن تقولوا إن الأمر منه غير النهي، والخبر غير الاستخبار. يقال لهم : لو وجب ما قلتموه لأجل الشاهد لوجب إذا كان القديم سبحانه موجوداً، أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً؛ وإذا كان بنفسه قائماً، أن يكون جوهراً ذا حيّز في الوجود؛ وإذا كان متكلماً، أن يكون الكلام موجوداً به أو أسباب الكلام؛ وإذا كان حيّاً عالماً قادراً، أن يكون ذا حياة وعلم وقدرة. لأنكم لم تعقلوا شيئاً إلا كذلك، ولا متكلماً حياً عالماً قادراً إلا كما ذكرناه ولوجب أيضاً عليكم أن لا يكون الباري سبحانه حيّاً عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً بنفسه، لأنكم لم تجدوا نفساً واحدةً تستحق هذه الأوصاف لنفسها. فكل هذا الذي تقولونه خلاف الشاهد والوجود. ثم يُقال لهم : قد وهمتم علينا في قولكم إنا لم نعقل كلاماً إلا حروفاً وأصواتاً لأننا لم نعقل قط ذلك. لأن الكلام فيما بيننا إنما هو معنى قائم بالنفس يُعبّر عنه بهذه الأصوات المسموعة تارةً وبغيرها أخرى. ولذلك ما يختلف الناس في الفصاحة والبلاغة في العبارة عن الكلام الذي هو مع اتفاقه واختلاف العبارة عنه بالإطالة مرةً والاختصار أخرى.
If they say: It is necessary in any case that you say that the speech of Allah is sounds and letters, divisible and varied, because you have not understood speech except as such. And you must say that His command is other than His prohibition, and the report is other than the question. It is said to them: If what you say were necessary because of sensory evidence, then it would be necessary, when the Eternal, glorified, is existent, that He be a body or a substance or an accident; and if He is subsistent by Himself, that He be a substance occupying space in existence; and if He is speaking, that speech exist through Him or the causes of speech; and if He is living, knowing, powerful, that He have life, knowledge, and power. Because you have not understood anything except like that, nor any speaker, living, knowing, powerful except as we mentioned. And it would also be necessary for you that the Creator, glorified, not be living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing by Himself, because you have not found any single soul that deserves these attributes by itself. So all of this that you say contradicts sensory evidence and existence. Then it is said to them: You have erred concerning us in your statement that we have not understood speech except as letters and sounds, because we have never understood that. For speech among us is nothing but a meaning subsisting in the soul, expressed by these audible sounds sometimes and by other things at other times. And for that reason people differ in eloquence and rhetoric in the expression of speech, while the speech itself is in agreement, and the expression of it varies, sometimes in length and sometimes in brevity.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that God's speech must be composed of letters and sounds because all speech we experience is like that. Baqillani first uses a counter-analogy: if we judge the divine by sensory experience alone, we would have to say God is a body, substance, or accident, and that His attributes are created, which is false. The same logic would also deny that God can be living, knowing, etc. by Himself, since no human soul has such attributes intrinsically. Thus the objector's method leads to absurdity. Then Baqillani corrects their claim that we only know speech as sounds: in reality, speech (al-kalam) is a meaning subsisting in the soul (ma‘nan qa'imun bil-nafs), expressed variously by sounds. Proof is that people can express the same inner speech with different phrasing—eloquent or not, long or short. This shows that the true locus of speech is the soul, not the articulated letters. Thus God's speech is an eternal attribute, not composed of temporal sounds.

Key Terms
كلام (kalam: speech)
معنى قائم بالنفس (meaning subsisting in the soul)
الشاهد (al-shāhid: the witnessed/sensory)
الأصوات والحروف (sounds and letters)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech must be composed of letters and sounds because all speech we know is like that.
Correction: Baqillani shows that speech in our own experience is a meaning in the soul, not the sounds themselves. Therefore, God's speech can be an eternal inner attribute without sounds.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Baqillani gives that divine speech cannot be judged solely by sensory experience?
A) Sensory experience is unreliable
B) The same method would force heretical conclusions about God's essence and attributes
C) The Quran says so
D) God's speech is not real speech

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, not a composition of temporal sounds and letters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you listen to the Quran, do you stop at the beauty of the sound, or do you press into the inner meaning that demands a response from your life? What is one verse you have heard repeatedly but never allowed to change you?
?
If God's speech is a living meaning that addresses your soul directly, how would your attitude in prayer shift from reciting words to engaging with a Person? What one change will you make tomorrow to treat the Quran as inner speech rather than mere sound?
?
Baqillani argues that the same inner meaning can be expressed in many ways. Are you more attached to a particular style of recitation or the unchanging truth behind it? What would it cost you to prioritize meaning over method?

Baqillani's Condemnation of Mu'tazila Doctrines
Baqillani enumerates Mu'tazila doctrines on vision, speech, actions, and the intermediate position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand four key Mu'tazila doctrines that Baqillani condemns as disgraceful, and why each contradicts Sunni orthodoxy.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila deny God's eternal attributes and human dependence on God for action.

This passage from Baqillani's Tamhid al-Awa'il lists several Mu'tazila positions that he considers scandalous. Before addressing them, he notes their problematic view of attributes implying the world's pre-eternity. Then he outlines four agreed-upon doctrines: denial of seeing God, created speech, human self-creation of actions, and the intermediate status of the sinful Muslim. Each directly challenges core Sunni beliefs about God's attributes, predestination, and faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of Baqillani's enumeration.
The Arabic begins mid-sentence; the omitted part likely referred to attributes.
2
Understand Baqillani's initial remark
Notice how Baqillani first critiques the Mu'tazila view that attributes exist before creation, which he says implies the pre-eternity of the world.
This sets up the broader problem of their denial of God's eternal attributes.
3
Identify the four disgraceful views
Look for the repeated phrase 'مما اتفقوا عليه من فضائحهم' (among their disgraceful agreed views). There are four such views listed.
Each introduces a distinct doctrinal error.
4
Analyse the denial of God's visibility
The first view is that Allah cannot be seen, even by Himself. This rejects the Sunni belief in beatific vision in the afterlife.
Many Mu'tazila also said He cannot see anything at all.
5
Examine the remaining three doctrines
The second is that God's speech is created. The third is that humans create their own actions. The fourth is the intermediate status of sinful Muslims.
These three deny God's eternal attribute of speech, divine omnipotence over human actions, and the clear classification of believers and disbelievers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقولون إن هذه الصفات كلها متحققة قبل الوجود، وإذا وجد لم يزدد في صفاته شيء بل هو الجوهر والعرض والسواد في حال الوجود على حقائقها المتحققة في حال العدم. وهذا منهم تصريح بقدم العالم . ومما اتفقوا عليه من فضائحهم قولهم : إن الله تعالى لا يرى وأنه لا يرى نفسه. وقال كثير منهم إنه لا يرى شيئاً ولا يبصر بحال . ومما اتفقوا عليه من فضائحهم أيضاً بقولهم : إن كلام الله تعالى مخلوق له يخلق لنفسه كلاماً في جسم من الأجسام فيكون فيه متكلماً ، وأنه لم يكن متكلماً قبل أن خلق لنفسه كلاماً. ومما اتفقوا عليه قولهم : إن أفعال العباد مخلوقة لهم ، وأن كل واحد منهم ومن جملة الحيوانات خالق خلق أفعاله وليس الباري خالقاً لأفعالهم ولا قادر على شيء من أعمالهم وأنه قط لا يقدر على شيء مما يفعله الحيوانات كلها . ومما اتفقوا عليه من فضائحهم قولهم إن حال الفاسق الملي منزلة بين منزلتين، لا هو مؤمن ولا هو كافر.
And they say that these attributes are all realized before existence, and when [a thing] exists, nothing increases in His attributes; rather He is the substance, the accident, and the blackness in the state of existence upon their realities that are realized in the state of non-existence. And this from them is a declaration of the pre-eternity of the world. And among their disgraceful [views] upon which they all agree is their saying: Indeed Allah, exalted, is not seen and that He does not see Himself. And many of them said that He does not see anything nor perceive anything at all. And among their disgraceful [views] upon which they all agree also is their saying: Indeed the speech of Allah, exalted, is created; He creates for Himself speech in a body from among bodies, thereby becoming speaker therein, and that He was not a speaker before He created speech for Himself. And among [the views] upon which they all agree is their saying: Indeed the actions of the servants are created by them, and that each one of them, including the animals, is a creator who creates his own actions, and the Creator is not the creator of their actions nor is He able to do anything of their deeds, and that He is never able to do anything of what all animals do. And among their disgraceful [views] upon which they all agree is their saying: Indeed the state of the sinful milla adherent is a station between two stations: he is not a believer nor is he a disbeliever.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage lists several core doctrines of the Mu'tazila that Imam Baqillani condemns as disgraceful. First, he notes their claim that attributes (like blackness) exist prior to creation, implying the world is eternal—a rejection of creation ex nihilo. Then he outlines five agreed-upon views: (1) God cannot be seen, even by Himself; (2) God's speech is created, occurring within a body, implying He was not eternally speaking; (3) human actions are created by the humans themselves, including animals, making them creators and denying God's omnipotence over their deeds; (4) the sinful Muslim occupies an intermediate status (al-manzila bayna al-manzilatayn), neither believer nor disbeliever. Each point contradicts Sunni orthodoxy on divine attributes, predestination, and faith.

Key Terms
رؤية الله
كلام الله مخلوق
أفعال العباد
المنزلة بين المنزلتين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Mu'tazila denied all attributes, but here they affirm 'attributes' as pre-existent qualities.
Correction: The Mu'tazila did not deny attributes entirely; they asserted that attributes like blackness exist in non-existence, which Baqillani argues implies the pre-eternity of the world.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila as described, what is the status of the sinful Muslim?
A) He is a believer despite his sin.
B) He is a disbeliever.
C) He is in a station between belief and disbelief.
D) He is a hypocrite.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila oppose Sunni orthodoxy on divine vision, speech, human agency, and the status of sinners.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila said they create their own actions. Have you ever felt pride in your achievements as if they were solely your doing? How does Baqillani's condemnation force you to re-evaluate your view of your own accomplishments and failures?
?
If you truly believed that Allah sees every sin you commit and every good deed, would you live differently? In what specific area of your life do you act as if He does not see?
?
The Mu'tazila held that a sinful Muslim is in an intermediate state—not a believer. Does this make you fear for your own faith? What sin in your life would you need to repent from to ensure you are not in that category?

Mu'tazila Denial of Divine Attributes
Baqillani reports the Mu'tazila denial of God's will, speech, face, and hand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extreme Mu'tazila denials of God's attributes—will, speech, face, and hand—as reported by Imam Baqillani.

Core Idea
Some Mu'tazila went so far as to deny God's will, speech, face, and hand entirely.

In this passage, Baqillani exposes the most radical Mu'tazila positions on divine attributes. While some Muslims debate the nature of attributes, these theologians outright denied that God possesses will, speech, a face, or a hand—directly contradicting Quranic verses. This lesson examines each denial and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the sequence of denials: will (Baghdadis), speech (Mu'ammar), face (all), hand (all).
The passage cuts off mid-sentence, but the pattern is clear.
2
Identify each denial and its proponent
Locate who denied what: the Baghdadis denied will; Mu'ammar denied speech; all denied face and hand.
Mu'ammar's denial of speech is especially drastic.
3
Examine the argument for denial of speech
Mu'ammar claimed that the speech Moses heard was not God's but the speech of a tree. That means God never actually spoke.
This reduces revelation to a natural phenomenon.
4
Understand the Quranic backdrop
Baqillani references Quranic verses about God's face and hand. The Mu'tazila rejected the apparent meaning without valid reason.
The lesson connects to tanzih (transcendence) taken to extremes.
5
Reflect on the danger of extreme tanzih
Consider how denying attributes can lead to stripping God of His perfection. Baqillani criticizes this as interpretive heresy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم البغداديون منهم أنه لا إرادة له تعالى . وجحد معمر أن يكون الله سبحانه كلاماً . وزعم أن الكلام الذي سمعه موسى عليه السلام كلام للشجرة التي وُجد بها لم يأمر قط ولم ينه عن شيء ولا رغب في شيء ولا زجر عنه ولا كلم أحداً ولا أخبر بخبر بتة، وزعموا جميعاً أنه لا وجه الله تعالى مع قوله عزّ وجلّ : ويبقى وجه ربك ذو الجلال والإكرام؛ وأنه لا يد له مع قوله
And the Baghdadis among them claimed that He, exalted, has no will. And Mu'ammar denied that Allah, glorified, is speech. And he claimed that the speech which Moses, peace be upon him, heard was speech of the tree in which it was found; it never commanded nor forbade anything, nor desired anything, nor discouraged anything, nor spoke to anyone, nor informed of any news at all. And they all claimed that Allah, exalted, has no face, despite His saying, mighty and majestic: 'And there will remain the Face of your Lord, Owner of Majesty and Honor'; and that He has no hand, despite His saying.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage reports further extreme Mu'tazila denials of divine attributes. First, the Baghdadis (a subgroup) deny God's will. Second, Mu'ammar ibn 'Abbad denies that God is speech (kalam), asserting that the speech heard by Moses was merely the speech of the tree—an accident—so God never actually spoke. This reduces revelation to a natural phenomenon. Third, all Mu'tazila are said to deny that God has a face and a hand, despite clear Quranic verses like [Quran 55:27] which affirm a divine face. Baqillani implies they are guilty of interpretive heresy, rejecting the apparent meaning without valid reason. The passage is incomplete but shows their tendency toward extreme transcendence (tanzih) that negates attributes.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الكلام
الوجه
اليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila are often said to affirm the attribute of speech, but according to Baqillani, Mu'ammar denies it outright.
Correction: Mu'ammar explicitly denied that God is speech, claiming that the speech of Moses was the speech of a tree. Thus, not all Mu'tazila affirmed divine speech.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what did Mu'ammar claim about the speech Moses heard?
A) It was God's eternal speech.
B) It was a created sound in a body.
C) It was the speech of the tree, not God's.
D) It was an illusion.

Key Takeaway
Extreme tanzih led some Mu'tazila to deny fundamental divine attributes like will, speech, face, and hand, contradicting clear Quranic verses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Mu'tazila denied God's will and speech because they thought affirming them would compromise tawheed. Is there any attribute of Allah that you are tempted to reinterpret because it makes you uncomfortable or doesn't fit your logic? What would it cost you to accept it as He described?
?
Mu'ammar heard the story of Moses and the tree but refused to believe it was God speaking. Have you ever experienced a clear sign from Allah—a verse, a sermon, a du'a answered—and then attributed it to coincidence or a natural process to avoid the weight of His direct address? What are you protecting yourself from by doing that?
?
The Mu'tazila strove for a pure transcendence, but it led them to deny what Allah affirmed about Himself. In your own life, are there ways you are so focused on 'protecting' Allah's majesty that you end up minimizing His nearness, His will over your decisions, or His personal speech to you? What would it look like for you to embrace both His transcendence and His immanence today?

Baqillani refutes denial of God's will, speech, face, and hands
Baqillani enumerates and refutes denials of God's attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the erroneous claims of the Baghdadis and Muammar regarding God's attributes of will, speech, face, and hands, using Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
False beliefs about God's attributes contradict clear Quranic texts.

Imam al-Baqillani confronts the heretical claims of two groups who denied essential divine attributes. The Baghdadis rejected God's will, while Muammar denied God's speech and even attributed it to a tree. Both groups also denied God's face and hands, despite explicit Quranic affirmations. This passage demonstrates the methodological principle of grounding theology in scriptural authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

2

3
Examine Muammar's denial of God's speech

The commentary explains the details.
4
See the joint denial of face and hands
Observe that both groups denied God's face and hands. Baqillani cites three Quranic verses as direct evidence against them.
The verses are part of the passage.
5
Reflect on Baqillani's method
Think about how Baqillani uses Quranic texts as the primary refutation. This highlights the importance of scripture in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم البغداديون منهم أنه لا إرادة له تعالى. وجحد معمر، شيخ من شيوخهم، أن يكون الله سبحانه كلاماً. وزعم أن الكلام الذي سمعه موسى عليه السلام، كلام للشجرة التي وُجد بها لم يأمر قط ولم ينه عن شيء ولا رغب في شيء ولا زجر عنه ولا كلم أحداً ولا أخبر بخبر بتة. وزعموا جميعاً أنه لا وجه الله تعالى مع قوله عزّ وجلّ : ويبقى وجه ربك ذو الجلال والإكرام؛ وأنه لا يد له مع قوله عزّ وجلّ : بل يداه مبسوطتان، وقوله تعالى : ما منعك أن تسجد لما خلقت بيدي.
And the Baghdadis claimed among them that He has no will. And Muammar, a shaykh of their shaykhs, denied that Allah, exalted be He, is speech. Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of two deviant groups: the Baghdadis and Muammar. Baqillani first reports the Baghdadis' claim that God has no will (إرادة). Then he refutes Muammar, who denied that God is speech (كلام) and claimed that the speech Moses heard was actually the speech of a tree, not God's. Baqillani then reports that both groups denied that God has a face (وجه) and hands (يد), despite clear Quranic verses affirming these attributes. The author uses the verses to highlight their contradiction of scripture.

Refuting the Baghdadis' Denial of Divine Hearing and Sight
Baqillani refutes the Baghdadis who deny real divine hearing and sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Baghdadis' interpretation of divine hearing and sight as mere knowledge is untenable and how it undermines divine perfection.

Core Idea
Denying real hearing and sight reduces God to the level of the deaf and blind.

This short but powerful passage from Imam Baqillani confronts a subtle theological error. The Baghdadis claimed that God does not truly hear or see; instead, He merely knows what is heard and seen. Baqillani exposes the flaw in this reasoning by drawing a comparison that no believer would accept. Let's examine his refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without rushing. Note the key terms: سميع, بصير, مزية, ضرير, أصم. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
The passage is dense with theological implication.
2
Understand the Baghdadis' claim
The Baghdadis affirm that God hears sounds and sees persons, but they interpret this as merely knowing them. They deny any real faculty of hearing or sight beyond knowledge.
This reduces the attributes to omniscience alone.
3
Grasp Baqillani's refutation
Baqillani points out that if hearing and sight are just knowledge, then God has no superiority over a blind and deaf person who also knows things without sensing them. This implication is clearly unacceptable for God's perfection.
The argument relies on the logical consequence of the Baghdadis' view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم البغداديون منهم أن الله تعالى بسميع ولا بصير، وإنما يوصف بأنه يسمع الأصوات ويبصر الأشخاص على معنى أنه يعلم ذلك، لا مزية له في هذين الوصفين على الضرير والأصم الذي لا يسمع ولا يبصر.
And the Baghdadis claimed among them that Allah is neither hearing nor seeing, but rather He is described as hearing sounds and seeing persons in the sense that He knows that, with no superiority in these two descriptions over the blind and deaf person who does not hear or see.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage refutes the Baghdadis who deny that Allah has real attributes of hearing and sight. They claim that when God is said to hear and see, it merely means He knows those things, not that He actually hears or sees. Baqillani points out that this reduces God's attributes to mere knowledge, making Him equal to a deaf and blind person who also knows things without sense perception—a clear denial of divine perfection.

Key Terms
سميع
بصير
مزية
ضرير
أصم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that denying real attributes of hearing and sight avoids anthropomorphism, but Baqillani shows that this error actually degrades God's perfection.
Correction: The Baghdadis' view leads to the absurd conclusion that God has no superiority over a blind and deaf person, which is incompatible with divine majesty.

Checkpoint
How do the Baghdadis interpret God's hearing and sight?
A) As literal hearing and sight
B) As mere knowledge without real perception
C) As actions performed in time
D) As metaphorical expressions

Key Takeaway
God's attributes of hearing and sight are real and distinct from mere knowledge; denying them contradicts divine perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin in secret, do you act as though God is deaf to your whisper and blind to your deeds? In what ways does the Baghdadis' comfortable denial of real hearing and sight appeal to your own desire to hide from accountability? Will you reject that comfort and live in the full awareness that God actively sees and hears you?
?
Have you ever felt that your prayers go unheard or that your struggles go unseen? Did that feeling lead you to a faith that God merely 'knows' rather than personally attends to you? How does Baqillani's refutation call you to repentance for treating God as less than perfectly hearing and seeing?
?
If you truly believe that God hears and sees in a real, distinct way, does your worship reflect intimacy rather than distance? Are you content to serve a God who only knows about you, or do you long for a God who actively listens and watches over you with perfect attention? What will you change today to align your heart with the reality of His hearing and sight?

Mu'tazila Consensus on God's Inability and Bishr's Exception
Baqillani reports Mu'tazila consensus on divine inability and Bishr's exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila consensus that God cannot perform human actions and how Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir's view differed.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila held that humans are more powerful than God in certain respects.

Imam Baqillani reports a shocking claim by the Mu'tazila: that God is unable to do what humans can do, and that humans are more powerful. This passage lays out their consensus and notes the one exception within their ranks. We will examine their argument and its theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the boldness of the claims.
The passage contains two main sections: the general consensus and the exception.
2
Identify the two main claims
First, the Mu'tazila claim God cannot do human actions and that humans are more powerful. Second, they claim (except Bishr) that God cannot cause a disbeliever to believe.
3
Note the exception of Bishr
Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir is the only one who did not agree with the second claim. The passage does not state his own view, only that he dissented.
4
Reflect on the theological stakes
Ask yourself: why would anyone claim that God is unable to cause belief? What view of human free will does this imply?
This passage directly challenges the orthodox Sunni belief in God's omnipotence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعموا بأسرهم أن الله سبحانه لا يقدر على قليل من أفعالهم ولا على كثير منها، وأنه قد أقدرهم على ما لا يقدر عليه، وأنهم أقدر من ربهم . وقالوا جميعا إلا رجلا منهم يعرف ببشر بن المعتمر أن الله تعالى ليس في سلطانه ولا في خزائنه شيء يقدر أن يفعله بمن يعلم أنه يموت كافرا فيؤمن عنده ؛ وأن العبد نفسه يقدر أن يؤمن؛ وأن الله سبحانه لا يقدر أن يفعل به ما يؤمن عنده ؛ وأنه سبحانه يقدر أن يفعل بجميع الخلق ما يكفرون عند وجوده ويفسدون ويعطبون، ولا يقدر على فعل ما يؤمنون عنده ؛ وإنه يقدر على استفسادهم ولا يقدر على استصلاحهم .
And they all claimed that God, glorified be He, is not able to do a small part of their actions nor a large part, and that He has empowered them over what He is not able to do, and that they are more powerful than their Lord. And they all said, except for a man among them known as Bishr ibn al-Mu'tamir, that God Almighty has nothing in His dominion or His treasures that He is able to do to someone He knows will die a disbeliever such that he would believe through it; and that the servant himself is able to believe; and that God, glorified be He, is not able to do to him that by which he would believe; and that He, glorified be He, is able to do to all creatures that by which they disbelieve when it exists and they become corrupt and perish, but He is not able to do that by which they would believe; and that He is able to corrupt them but not able to reform them.
Source pages p. 292

Commentary
This passage enumerates the Mu'tazila consensus on divine inability. They assert God cannot perform human actions, but humans can, making humans more powerful. Then they specify (except Bishr) that God has no power to cause a person to believe if they are destined to die as a disbeliever; the servant has the power, but God does not. God can cause disbelief but not faith. This reverses the orthodox understanding of omnipotence and predestination. The exception of Bishr is noted, who held a more extreme view.

Key Terms

أقدرهم
بشر بن المعتمر
استفساد
استصلاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Create evil.
B) Cause a person to believe if He knows they will die a disbeliever.
C) Do anything at all.
D) Reform the believers.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila (except Bishr) argued that God cannot cause a person to believe if He knows they will die a disbeliever, limiting divine power in favor of human autonomy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you subconsciously believe that your faith is your own achievement, forgetting that it is God who guides? How does that affect your humility and gratitude when you reflect on your iman?

If a person truly believed that God cannot cause a disbeliever to believe, how would that affect their attitude toward da'wah? Examine your own conviction: do you pray for guidance for others as if it is entirely in God's hands, or do you rely on your own persuasion?

Al-Nazzam's Doctrine of Obligation on God
Baqillani reports and refutes al-Nazzam's doctrine of divine obligation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's claim that God is obligated to benefit the servant and why Baqillani refutes it as impossible.

Core Idea
Obligation on God is impossible; it would destroy His argument against disbelief.

This lesson presents the controversial doctrine of al-Nazzam, a prominent Mu'tazili scholar, who argued that God must do what is beneficial for the servant, otherwise He would be stingy. Imam Baqillani refutes this by showing that obligation upon God is rationally impossible, and that accepting such a doctrine would undermine God's own argument and authority over His servants.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Nazzam's claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Understand that al-Nazzam asserts an obligation on God to benefit the believer, and claims that even disbelievers receive benefit.
Note his reasoning: if God didn't benefit, He would be stingy.
2
Identify the refutation
Baqillani declares that obligation upon God is impossible (محال). Find this key statement and understand why it is a fundamental refutation.
He argues that a disbeliever has no benefit in his disbelief.
3
Consider the consequence
Reflect on the example of three children from one womb: if God causes one to die, accepting al-Nazzam's view would mean God has no argument against that child.
This example shows the absurd result of the doctrine.
4
Summarise the lesson
Combine the claim, refutation, and example into a coherent understanding of why obligation on God is rejected in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم النظام وهو شيخهم المعظم وكبيرهم المقدم أن الله فضائحه قوله يجب على الله تعالى أن يفعل بالعبد ما فيه صلاح العبد، لأنه لو لم يفعل ومن ما فيه صلاحه لكان قد بخل عليه وركب على هذا فقال : كل ما فعله الله بالكفار فهو به صلاحهم ولم يكن بمقدوره أصلح مما فعل . فالوجوب على الله تعالى محال وكل عاقل يعلم أن الكافر لا صلاح له في كفره ولا ما يحمل به من تبعات فعله، فعلى هذا يجب أن تكون حجة الله منقطعة حتى لا يكون له على عبيده ثلاثة ولدوا دفعة واحدة بطنا واحدا فأمات الله أحدهم في حال حجة، ويتصور ذلك في
And al-Nazzam, their great chief and eminent leader, claimed that God ... his scandals include his statement: It is obligatory upon God Almighty to do for the servant what is his benefit, because if He did not do what is his benefit, He would have been stingy towards him. And he built upon this and said: Everything God does to the disbelievers is their benefit, and He was not able to do what is more beneficial than what He did. So obligation upon God Almighty is impossible, and every rational person knows that the disbeliever has no benefit in his disbelief nor what he carries of the consequences of his action. On this basis, God's argument would be severed so that He would have no argument against His servants: three children born at once in one womb, God causes one of them to die... in a state of argument, and this can be conceived in
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents al-Nazzam's doctrine that God is obligated to do what benefits the servant, otherwise He would be stingy. He then claims that everything God does to disbelievers is for their benefit, and God could not have done better. The author refutes this by stating that obligation upon God is impossible. They argue that the disbeliever has no benefit in disbelief. Accepting al-Nazzam's position would remove God's argument against His servants, as illustrated by the example of three children from one womb: God causing one to die would supposedly lack a basis of argument. The passage is incomplete but states the core refutation.

Key Terms
النظام
وجوب على الله
صلاح العبد
بخل
حجة الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to benefit the servant; otherwise He would be stingy.
Correction: Obligation upon God is impossible; God is not subject to external obligations. His actions are free and cannot be constrained by human notions of benefit.

Checkpoint
What did al-Nazzam claim about God's actions toward the servant?
A) God must do what is beneficial for the servant.
B) God can do whatever He wants without obligation.
C) God is not involved in human benefit.
D) God only punishes without benefit.

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's doctrine of obligation on God is refuted because it is impossible for God to be obligated, and accepting it would sever God's argument against His servants.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that God owes you something—good health, a comfortable life, or success in your endeavors? How does Baqillani's firm statement that 'obligation upon God is impossible' challenge that feeling? What would change in your daily du'a if you truly believed God is never obligated to give you anything?
?
If you were to internalize that God's actions toward disbelievers are ultimately just and that He has no obligation to give anyone what they think is 'best', would you be able to accept God's decree when you face hardship, or would you still feel entitled to better? Examine your heart: where do you secretly think God should treat you differently?
?
Al-Nazzam's argument sounds compassionate—God must do what is beneficial. But this lesson shows it actually severs God's argument against disbelief. In your own life, are there ways you try to 'obligate' God by your good deeds, asking, 'Why did this happen to me?' in a way that undermines His wisdom? What step of repentance or trust do you need to take today?

A Heretical Doctrine on Divine Power and Human Ability
Presents a heretical view limiting God's power over belief and human ability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core claims of a heretical doctrine that limits God's power over belief and human actions.

Core Idea
This heresy claims God cannot cause belief, but can cause disbelief.

Imam Baqillani presents a distorted view attributed to certain heretics. This view denies God's omnipotence by asserting that God cannot cause belief in a person He knows will die a disbeliever, while the slave himself can. Understanding this doctrine is essential to appreciating the author's subsequent refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the claims about God's power and the slave's ability.
This is a heretical view, not the author's own.
2
Identify the key claims
List the claims: 1) God has no act to cause belief in the disbeliever, 2) The slave can believe, 3) God cannot cause belief, 4) God can cause disbelief and corruption, 5) God can corrupt but not reform.
Focus on the asymmetry between God's ability to cause disbelief vs. belief.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider what this doctrine implies about divine omnipotence and human free will. Notice that it places ultimate agency in human will.
This sets up the need for refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ليس في سلطانه ولا في خزائنه شيء يقدر أن يفعله بمن يعلم أنه يموت كافراً فيؤمن عنده؛ وأن العبد نفسه يقدر أن يؤمن؛ وأن الله سبحانه لا يقدر أن يفعل به ما يؤمن عنده؛ وأنه سبحانه يقدر أن يفعل بجميع الخلق ما يكفرون عند وجوده ويفسدون ويعطبون، ولا يقدر على فعل ما يؤمنون عنده؛ وإنه يقدر على استفسادهم ولا يقدر على استصلاحهم.
There is nothing in His dominion nor in His treasuries that He is able to do to one whom He knows will die a disbeliever, such that he would believe thereby; and that the slave himself is able to believe; and that Allah the Exalted is not able to do to him that by which he would believe; and that He the Exalted is able to do to all creation that by which they disbelieve when it occurs, and become corrupt and perish, and He is not able to do that by which they would believe; and that He is able to corrupt them and is not able to reform them.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage presents a distorted view of divine power and human ability, likely attributed to a Mu'tazilite figure. It makes the following claims: (1) God has no act in His power that can cause a person He knows will die a disbeliever to believe; (2) the slave himself has the power to believe independently; (3) God cannot do what would make the slave believe; (4) conversely, God can do to all people what makes them disbelieve and become corrupted, but cannot do what makes them believe; (5) God can corrupt them but cannot reform them. This doctrine effectively denies God's omnipotence and places ultimate agency in the human will, which the author will later refute.

Key Terms
قدرة
إيمان
كفر
عبد
استصلاح
استفساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage presents the author's own belief about divine power.
Correction: This passage actually presents a heretical doctrine that the author will refute.

Checkpoint
What does this heretical doctrine claim about God's ability regarding the disbeliever's belief?
A) God can cause belief in anyone.
B) God cannot cause belief in the one He knows will die a disbeliever.
C) God can cause belief but chooses not to.
D) God and the slave both equally cause belief.

Key Takeaway
This heretical doctrine dramatically limits divine power and elevates human will, setting the stage for a rigorous refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own faith, do you ever catch yourself thinking, 'I chose to believe, and that makes me better than others'? How does the heresy's claim that the slave himself is able to believe echo that pride? Examine your heart: is your gratitude for faith directed to the Giver or to yourself?
?
If God truly could not cause someone to believe, what would that mean for your prayers for guidance for loved ones who are lost? Do you pray with the confidence that Allah can change hearts, or do you secretly doubt His power over belief? Let this passage expose any hidden unbelief in His absolute ability.
?
The heresy says God can corrupt but cannot reform. In your life, have you ever attributed your good deeds to your own strength and your sins to circumstances or to God's 'not helping'? Repent of any thought that limits His power to reform you, and commit to relying on Him alone for every step of righteousness.

The Impossibility of Obligation on God
Baqillani refutes the claim that God must do what is beneficial for the slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari school rejects any obligation upon God, specifically the claim that God must do what is most beneficial for His slaves.

Core Idea
God is not obligated by anyone; obligation upon God is impossible.

Imam al-Baqillani begins his refutation of an-Nazzam's doctrine by stating the fundamental principle that nothing is obligatory upon God. An-Nazzam argued that if God did not do the most beneficial for the slave, He would be miserly. Baqillani counters that this would imply obligation, which is impossible, and that the disbeliever's disbelief itself is not beneficial.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the claim and the immediate response.
Focus on the key terms: وجوب and صلاح.
2
Identify an-Nazzam's claim
Find the statement where an-Nazzam says it is obligatory upon God to do what is beneficial for the slave.
3
Understand Baqillani's rebuttal
Note how he declares obligation impossible and that the disbeliever has no welfare in his disbelief.
4
Reflect on the principle
Consider why Ash'aris insist God is free from duty to creation.
This is a core Ash'ari principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فضائحه قوله : يجب على الله تعالى أن يفعل بالعبد ما فيه صلاح العبد، لأنه لو لم يفعل ومن ما فيه صلاحه لكان قد بخل عليه وركب على هذا فقال : كل ما فعله الله بالكفار فهو به صلاحهم ولم يكن بمقدوره أصلح مما فعل . فالوجوب على الله تعالى محال وكل عاقل يعلم أن الكافر لا صلاح له في كفره ولا ما يحمل به من تبعات فعله، فعلى هذا يجب أن يكون حجة الله منقطعة حتى لا يكون له على عبيده حجة، ويتصور ذلك في ثلاثة ولدوا دفعة واحدة بطناً واحداً فأمات الله أحدهم في حال الطفولية وبلغ منهم اثنان فكفر أحدهما وآمن آخر فيدخل الله يوم القيامة في الجنة من مات في حال الطفولية ولا يبلغه منها الدرجة العظيمة، ويدخل الذي آمن الجنة ويعطيه الدرجة العظيمة ويدخل الذي كفر النار. فيقول الطفل الذي مات في صغره : لم لم تبلغني درجة الذي آمن بعد البلوغ؟ فيقول له : لأنه آمن وأنت لم تؤمن . فيقول الذي مات طفلاً : هلا بلغتني حال البلوغ حتى كنت أؤمن بك كما آمن هو ؟ فيقول الله تعالى له : لم أبلغك حال البلوغ لأني علمت أنك لو بقيت لكفرت فاخترمتك قبل البلوغ لأن صلاحك كان فيه حتى سلمت من النار. سمع الذي في النار هذا الكلام يقول : فلم لم تخترمني قبل البلوغ حتى كنت أسلم النار وكان يكون فيه صلاحي فنعوذ بالله من مذهب يؤدي إلى مثل هذه الرذيلة.
Among his scandals is his saying: It is obligatory upon Allah the Exalted to do for the slave that which is his slave's welfare, because if He did not do that which is for his welfare, He would have been miserly toward him. And he built upon this saying: Everything that Allah does to the disbelievers is for their welfare, and He was not able to do something more beneficial than what He did. So obligation upon Allah the Exalted is impossible, and every rational person knows that the disbeliever has no welfare in his disbelief nor in what he bears of the consequences of his action. On this basis, the proof of Allah would have to be cut off so that He would have no proof against His slaves. And this can be imagined in the case of three children born at once from one womb: Allah causes one of them to die in childhood; two reach puberty, one disbelieves and one believes. On the Day of Resurrection, Allah causes the one who died in childhood to enter Paradise, but does not give him the great rank; He causes the believer to enter Paradise and gives him the great rank; He causes the disbeliever into the Fire. Then the child who died in his youth says: Why did you not give me the rank of the one who believed after puberty? He says: Because he believed and you did not believe. The child who died says: Why did you not let me reach puberty so that I could believe in You as he believed? Allah the Exalted says to him: I did not let you reach puberty because I knew that if you remained you would disbelieve, so I cut you off before puberty because your welfare was in that, so that you would be saved from the Fire. The one in the Fire hears this and says: Then why did you not cut me off before puberty so that I would be saved from the Fire, and that would have been my welfare? So we seek refuge with Allah from a school of thought that leads to such vileness.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage refutes an-Nazzam's claim that it is obligatory upon God to do what is most beneficial for the slave. The author states that obligation upon God is impossible (a key Ash'ari principle). An-Nazzam had argued that if God does not do the most beneficial, He would be miserly—implying a duty on God. The author counters that rational people know the disbeliever's disbelief and its consequences are not beneficial. He then constructs a reductio ad absurdum: imagine three triplets—one dies as an infant, one becomes a believer, one a disbeliever. God gives them different fates. The infant complains he was not given the believer's rank; God replies that He knew the infant would have disbelieved if he lived. Then the disbeliever in the Fire asks why God did not also kill him as a child. This shows the absurdity of the doctrine, as it would force God to kill all potential disbelievers, which does not happen. The author concludes by seeking refuge from such a vile doctrine.

Key Terms
وجوب
صلاح
الكافر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An-Nazzam believed God is obligated to do the most beneficial.
Correction: The Ash'ari school holds that obligation upon God is impossible; God does as He wills without being constrained.

Checkpoint
What does an-Nazzam claim is obligatory upon God?
A) To punish all disbelievers.
B) To do what is most beneficial for the slave.
C) To create all humans.
D) To reward believers.

Key Takeaway
No obligation is binding upon Allah; He is free from all duties to creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever feel that God owes you something because of your good deeds? How does this passage shatter that assumption? Will you repent for any entitlement you held towards Allah?
?
When you face a difficulty, do you catch yourself thinking 'Why is God doing this to me?' — as if He should have prevented it? How does the truth that obligation upon Allah is impossible change your response to trials?
?
If God is not obligated to give you any good, then every good you have is pure grace. Does your heart truly believe this? How will you live differently today knowing you deserve nothing but have received everything?

The Triplets Hypothetical: A Reductio Ad Absurdum
Baqillani uses a triplets hypothetical to expose the absurdity of an-Nazzam's obligation doctrine.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the triplets hypothetical illustrates the absurdity of claiming God must do what is most beneficial.

Core Idea
If God were obligated to do what is most beneficial, He would have to kill all potential disbelievers.

After establishing that obligation on God is impossible, Baqillani presents a hypothetical scenario to show the absurd consequences of an-Nazzam's view. The story of three triplets—one dies as an infant, one becomes a believer, one a disbeliever—demonstrates that the doctrine would require God to preemptively kill all who would disbelieve, which does not happen.

Guided Path
1
Read the hypothetical
Read the triplets story in Arabic and English.
Notice the dialogue between the characters.
2
Understand the logic
Analyze the conversation: the infant's question, God's response, and the disbeliever's objection.
3
Identify the reductio
The disbeliever asks why God did not kill him as a child, exposing the absurdity.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Why does this scenario refute an-Nazzam's view?
It shows the doctrine leads to an impossible consequence.

?

?
Consider the dead child's question: 'Why did you not let me reach puberty?' Have you ever questioned God's timing or plan for your life—why He allowed you to face trials when others had easier paths? Will you trust that His knowledge of your heart is deeper than your own desires, even when you don't understand?
?
The disbeliever in the Fire blames God for not killing him as a child. In your own life, are there ways you shift blame to God for your choices, secretly believing He should have intervened to prevent you from sinning? What step will you take today to own your responsibility before Him instead of asking why He didn't 'cut you off'?

The Absurdity of Ending Paradise and Hell
Refutes Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that paradise and hell will cease.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Hudhayl's misguided view that paradise and hell will end and why it leads to an absurd conclusion about God's power.

Core Idea
Claiming an end to paradise and hell contradicts God's eternal power.

This passage presents a controversial view attributed to Abu al-Hudhayl, a major Mu'tazilite thinker. He argued that the pleasures of paradise and the punishments of hell will eventually cease. Imam Baqillani refutes this by showing that it would render God powerless, making worship meaningless. We will examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the claim, then the logical consequence, then a further description.
2
Identify the core claim
What does Abu al-Hudhayl claim about paradise, hell, and God's acts? Look at the opening phrase.
The claim is that they have an end (آخر).
3
Analyze the refutation
The author shows the consequence: if God's acts end, then God can no longer be described as powerful (قادر) and worship becomes invalid. This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The author does not explicitly state he is refuting; but the implication is clear.
4
Consider the absurd outcome
Abu al-Hudhayl even says the people of paradise will be in a stupor. This contradicts the Qur'anic promise of eternal bliss.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو الهذيل العلاف أيضاً أن لنعيم أهل الجنة وعقاب أهل النار وسائر أفعال القديم سبحانه آخراً، لا يوصف الله بالقدرة، إذا فعله ووقع منه على قليل الأفعال ولا على كثيرها؛ ولا يصح حينئذ الرغبة إليه ولا الرهبة منه لأنه لا يقدر إذ ذاك على خير ولا شرّ ولا نفع ولا ضرّ . قال : ويبقى أهل الجنة خموداً سكوتاً، لا يفيضون بكلمة، ولا يتحركون.
And Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf also claimed that the bliss of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of the Fire and all acts of the Eternal (the Exalted) have an end. Then God cannot be described with power, when He does it and it occurs from Him, over few acts nor many; and at that time neither desire towards Him nor fear of Him would be valid, because He would then have no power over good or evil, benefit or harm. He said: The people of Paradise will remain in a state of stupor, silent, uttering no word, and not moving.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents Abu al-Hudhayl's view that the pleasures of Paradise and the punishments of Hell will eventually come to an end, along with all acts of God. The author then shows the absurd consequence: after that point, God can no longer be described as having power over any act, whether small or great. Consequently, prayer and fear of God become meaningless because He would have no power to reward or punish. Abu al-Hudhayl even claimed that the inhabitants of Paradise would be in a stupor, silent and motionless—a state that contradicts the promised eternal bliss. The author refutes this by implication: such a view undermines the very purpose of religion.

Key Terms
أبو الهذيل
نعيم
عقاب
آخر
قدرة
خمود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage represents the author's own view.
Correction: It is presented as a claim to be refuted. The author (Imam Baqillani) is showing the absurd consequences of this view.

Checkpoint
What does Abu al-Hudhayl claim about the end of paradise and hell?
A) They are eternal without end.
B) They will have an end, and God will cease to have power.
C) They will be replaced by a new creation.
D) Only paradise ends, hell does not.

Key Takeaway
The view that paradise and hell will end is incompatible with the eternal nature of God's power and the purpose of worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that paradise and hell had an end, would your motivation to obey God change? How does this knowledge affect your daily choices? Stop and honestly examine one area of your life where the fear of eternal consequences or the hope of eternal reward is currently shaping your actions.
?
Abu al-Hudhayl's view leads to a paradise of silence and immobility. What does that tell you about the importance of eternal activity and relationship with God? Are you living today as if your relationship with God will continue forever, actively engaged, or are you growing stagnant?
?
The author argues that worship becomes invalid if God's power ceases. Consider your own worship: do you sometimes act as if God's power is limited—perhaps by not trusting Him in a difficult situation, or by sinning as if He will not hold you accountable? What specific step can you take today to realign your heart with the belief that His power never ends?

Abu al-Hudhayl's Denial of God's Power over Injustice
Baqillani presents Abu al-Hudhayl's denial of God's power over unjust acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu al-Hudhayl's argument that God cannot be described as powerful over unjust acts and how this limits divine omnipotence.

Core Idea
Abu al-Hudhayl's view limits God's power by claiming He cannot do what is unjust.

Imam Baqillani presents the controversial position of Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, who argued that God cannot be described as having power over certain acts, such as throwing a child into Hell, because those acts would be unjust. This raises important questions about the relationship between divine power and justice. Let's examine the argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to understand the claim. The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the key claim
Note that Abu al-Hudhayl denies God's power over specific unjust acts. Ask: What acts are mentioned?
Acts: throwing children into Fire, cutting off reward, nullifying punishment.
3
Understand the reasoning
The reasoning is that if God were capable of injustice, we could not feel safe from its occurrence. Reflect on this logic.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this limits God's absolute power and why Baqillani is refuting it.
Baqillani will likely argue for God's absolute power over all things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبحانه لا يوصف بالقدرة على طرح بعض الأطفال في النار، ولا على قطع الثواب وإبطال العقاب؛ وأن بعض الإنس والشياطين يقدر على طرح طفل أو مجنون في جهنم، على ما لا يقدر الله عليه. لأن ذلك عنده ظلم ولو قدر عليه سبحانه على أصله، لم يأمن وقوعه منه.
Glorified is He. He is not described with power over throwing some children into the Fire, nor over cutting off reward and nullifying punishment; and that some humans and devils are able to throw a child or an insane person into Hellfire, that which God is not able to do. Because that, according to him, is injustice, and if He, glorified, were capable of it according to his principle, one would not be safe from its occurrence from Him.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf. He claims that God cannot be described as having power over certain acts like throwing children into Hell or cutting off reward, because such acts would be unjust. At the same time, he claims that humans and devils can do these things. The reasoning is that if God were capable of injustice, then one could not feel safe from its occurrence. This is a refutation by Baqillani, showing how this limits God's omnipotence.

Key Terms
القدرة
الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Abu al-Hudhayl is defending God's justice by denying Him power over unjust acts.
Correction: In reality, he limits God's omnipotence by claiming God cannot do certain things, which contradicts the Islamic understanding that God has absolute power over all possible things.

Checkpoint
According to Abu al-Hudhayl, why cannot God be described with power over throwing children into Hell?
A) Because He is too merciful.
B) Because that would be unjust.
C) Because He lacks power.
D) Because children are innocent.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Hudhayl's view restricts divine power by excluding acts deemed unjust, which contradicts the Islamic understanding of God's absolute omnipotence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself assuming that God 'cannot' do something because you think it would be unjust or unfair? How does this lesson expose the hidden limits you place on God's power in your own heart?
?
Abu al-Hudhayl argued that if God were capable of injustice, we could not feel safe. What does your trust in God rely on—His power or a limitation of His power? How can you learn to rest in the fact that He can do all things yet chooses only what is perfectly wise and just?
?
When you witness suffering or experience personal trials, do you ever doubt that God has the power to change your situation? How does this lesson call you to affirm His absolute omnipotence without compromising His perfect justice, even when you don't understand His ways?

Abu al-Hudhayl on the End of God's Acts and Inert Paradise
Baqillani relates Abu al-Hudhayl's claim that God's acts end and Paradise becomes inert

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the heretical view of Abu al-Hudhayl that God's power and actions will cease, leaving Paradise motionless and silent.

Core Idea
Abu al-Hudhayl's heresy: God's acts end, Paradise becomes silent and motionless.

Imam al-Baqillani records a shocking claim from Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf: that God's power will eventually cease, and the people of Paradise will become lifeless. This passage exposes a fundamental misunderstanding of divine eternity and human destiny. As we read, we will see the reasoning behind this view and why it contradicts the Qur'an and rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the boldness of the claim.
2
Identify the main claim
What does Abu al-Hudhayl assert about the end of God's acts and the state of Paradise?
He says the bliss of Paradise and punishment of Hell have an end.
3
Examine the consequences
List the three consequences Abu al-Hudhayl derives: God not described with power, supplication invalid, people of Paradise motionless and silent.
4
Analyze the argument
How does he argue by analogy? Events must have a beginning and an end; nothing after the end.
He claims that just as events had a beginning with nothing before, they must have an end with nothing after.
5
Prepare for refutation
Baqillani records this view to refute it. Consider why this view is problematic for divine eternity and human destiny.
The Qur'an describes paradise as eternal and God's power as unceasing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أبو الهذيل العلاف أيضاً أن لنعيم أهل الجنة وعقاب أهل النار وسائر أفعال القديم سبحانه آخراً، لا يوصف الله بالقدرة، إذا فعله وقع منه على قليل الأفعال ولا على كثيرها؛ ولا يصح حينئذ الرغبة إليه ولا الرهبة منه لأنه لا يقدر إذ ذاك على خير ولا شرّ ولا نفع ولا ضرّ. قال: ويبقى أهل الجنة خموداً سكوتاً، لا يفيضون بكلمة، ولا يتحركون بحركة، ولا يلتذون بلذة، ولا يقدرون ولا ربهم على شيء من ذلك. لأن الحوادث زعم كما لا بد لها من أوّل تنتهي إليه لم يكن قبله شيء، فكذلك لا بد لها من آخر تنتهي إليه لا يكون بعده شيء.
And Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf also claimed that the bliss of the people of Paradise and the punishment of the people of Hellfire and all acts of the Eternal, glorified, have an end; God is not described with power, if He does it and it occurs from Him, over a few acts nor over many; and then supplication to Him nor fear from Him are valid, because He is not able at that time over good nor evil, nor benefit nor harm. He said: The people of Paradise remain motionless, silent, they do not utter a word, nor do they move with a movement, nor do they experience pleasure with a pleasure, nor are they able, nor their Lord, over any of that. Because events, he claimed, just as they must have a beginning to which they end, there being nothing before it, likewise they must have an end to which they end, nothing after it.
Source pages p. 294

Commentary
This passage presents another heretical view of Abu al-Hudhayl. He claims that the bliss of Paradise, punishment of Hell, and all acts of God have an end. After that end, God is no longer described as having power over even a few acts; thus supplication and fear become meaningless. The people of Paradise become motionless and silent, devoid of pleasure, and neither they nor God have power over anything. He argues by analogy: just as events have a beginning with nothing before, they must have an end with nothing after. Baqillani records this to refute it.

Key Terms
القديم
الحوادث
القدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They continue in bliss forever.
B) They become motionless and silent.
C) They intercede for others.
D) They increase in pleasure.

Key Takeaway
Abu al-Hudhayl's view that God's acts end and Paradise becomes inert is a rejected heresy, based on a flawed analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act as if God's power is limited to what has already happened, forgetting that He is actively sustaining and creating every moment? How would your supplication change if you truly believed He can act right now?
?

?
What does the permanence of God's attributes demand of your relationship with Him today? Are there areas where you rely on your own power rather than His continuous, never-ceasing ability to act?

Baqillani's Refutation of al-Jubba'i on Conditional Oaths
Baqillani refutes al-Jubba'i's ruling on an oath conditioned on God's will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jubba'i's ruling on oaths conditioned on God's will and how Baqillani refutes it using the consensus of the community.

Core Idea
Swearing 'if God wills' does not nullify the oath's obligation upon failure.

Al-Jubba'i, a prominent Mu'tazilite theologian, held a controversial view on conditional oaths. He argued that if someone swears to do something 'if God wills' and then fails to do it when able, they are still an oath-breaker and must pay expiation. This passage presents his claim and Baqillani's succinct critique, highlighting a key difference between the Mu'tazilite position and the broader Sunni consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: al-Jubba'i's claim followed by Baqillani's refutation.
The passage is only two sentences, so read slowly.
2
Identify al-Jubba'i's reasoning
Find the basis for al-Jubba'i's ruling. He says God wills the payment and dislikes delay, so failure breaks the oath.
3
Observe Baqillani's response
Baqillani rejects the view as contrary to the community (al-ummah) and a rash advance. This is the key critique.
This shows the importance of consensus in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم الجبائي أن حالفاً، لو حلف بالله ليعطين زيداً حقه غداً إن شاء الله، ثم جاء الغد ولم يعطه مع التمكن منه كان حانثاً. لأن الله تعالى قد شاء أن يدفع إليه الحق وكره مطله، وأن الكفارة تلزمه خلافاً للأمة وتقحماً لمشاقها.
And al-Jubba'i claimed that if someone swears by God that he will give Zayd his right tomorrow, if God wills, then tomorrow comes and he does not give it despite being able to do so, he is an oath-breaker. Because God, exalted, has willed that he should pay the right and dislikes his delaying it, and that expiation is incumbent upon him, contrary to the community, and a rash advance against its difficulties.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jubba'i's view on an oath conditioned on God's will. He argues that the oath is still binding because God has willed the payment of the right and dislikes delay, so the failure to pay breaks the oath and requires expiation. The author notes that this contradicts the majority of the Muslim community and is a reckless departure from the consensus.

Key Terms
الحلف
المشيئة
الكفارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that adding 'if God wills' to an oath makes it non-binding, so failure is inconsequential.
Correction: Al-Jubba'i held the opposite extreme—that it remains absolutely binding. The Sunni consensus is moderate: the conditional phrase suspends the oath, so no expiation is due if the condition is not met, but the oath is still valid if the condition is met.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jubba'i, what is the ruling on the person who swears to give a right 'if God wills' but fails to do so?
A) He is not an oath-breaker.
B) He is an oath-breaker and must pay expiation.
C) He must only pay the right.
D) There is no consequence.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jubba'i's opinion that an oath conditioned on God's will is still binding upon failure is rejected by the Sunni community as contrary to consensus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'insha'Allah' before a promise, do you treat it as a genuine surrender to God's will or as a way to lessen your responsibility? How will you change after learning that the Sunni scholars hold you accountable even with the condition?
?
Think of a promise you made with 'if God wills' that you later broke. Did you feel obligated to fulfill it? This lesson shows a scholar arguing for that obligation. What would you say to God if He asked you about that broken promise?
?
Al-Jubba'i's opinion was rejected for opposing the community's consensus. In your own life, when you hold a belief that contradicts the mainstream Muslim understanding, do you seek to verify it? How can you ensure you are following the truth, not just a personal opinion?
