Attributes of Essence: The Eternal Descriptions of Allah
Baqillani lists the attributes of essence and their nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the list of attributes that Baqillani classifies as attributes of essence and why they are considered eternal.

Core Idea
Allah's essential attributes are eternal and inseparable from His essence.

Baqillani begins by enumerating the attributes that belong to Allah's essence — those descriptions that are true of Him from eternity. These are not acquired or temporal but are part of what He is. In this passage, you will see a detailed list that includes life, knowledge, power, and many others, as well as the early Ash'ari understanding of terms like 'face' and 'hands'.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of essential attributes
Carefully read the Arabic and translation of the first part, noting the diversity of attributes mentioned.
Pay attention to the order and groupings.
2
Identify the core attributes
Spot the attributes that are universally accepted: life, knowledge, power, hearing, sight, speech, will, eternity.
3
Understand scriptural attributes
Notice 'face', 'two eyes', 'two hands' — Baqillani includes them without interpretation, but the Ash'ari school affirms them as real without modality.
4
Reflect on emotional and perceptual attributes
Anger and pleasure are explained as aspects of will; mercy, displeasure, etc., are listed. Also, divine perception of sensory qualities is included.
This shows Allah's comprehensive knowledge without implying sensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحياة والعلم والقدرة والسمع والبصر والكلام والإرادة والبقاء والوجه وهما والعينان واليدان والغضب والرضى وهما الإرادة على ما وصفناه . الرحمة والسخط والولاية والعداوة والحبّ والإيثار والمشيئة وإدراكه تعالى لكل جنس يدركه الخلق من الطعوم والروائح والحرارة والبرودة وغير ذلك من المدركات . وصفات فعله الخلق والرزق والعدل والإحسان والتفضّل والإنعام والثواب والعقاب والحشر والنشر وكل صفة كان موجوداً قبل فعله لها. غير أن وصفه لنفسه ذلك قديم، لأنه كلامه بجميع الذي هو قوله: «إني خالق رازق باسط وهو تعالى لم يزل متكلماً بكلام غير محدث ولا مخلوق .
Life, knowledge, power, hearing, sight, speech, will, eternity, the face, and the two eyes, the two hands, anger, and pleasure — both being will as we described. Mercy, displeasure, guardianship, enmity, love, preference, volition, and His perception, Exalted is He, of every kind that creation perceives of tastes, odors, heat, cold, and other perceptibles. And the attributes of His action are creation, provision, justice, beneficence, favor, bounty, reward, punishment, gathering, and spreading — and every attribute that He existed before His doing of it. However, His description of Himself with that is eternal, because it is His speech comprising all that He says: 'Indeed I am the Creator, the Provider, the Extender' — and He, Exalted is He, has never ceased being a speaker with speech that is not originated nor created.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage classifies the divine attributes into two categories: attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) and attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl). The author first lists the essential attributes: life, knowledge, power, hearing, sight, speech, will, eternity, the face, the two eyes, the two hands, anger, pleasure (both interpreted as aspects of will), mercy, displeasure, guardianship, enmity, love, preference, volition, and divine perception of all sensible qualities like tastes, odors, heat, cold, and other perceptibles. Then he lists the attributes of action: creation, provision, justice, beneficence, favor, bounty, reward, punishment, gathering, and spreading. He notes that for each attribute of action, God existed before performing that act. Crucially, he affirms that God's description of Himself with these actions (e.g., 'I am the Creator') is eternal, because it is His speech — and His speech is uncreated and eternal. Thus, the speech by which He describes Himself as Creator is itself eternal, even though the act of creation happens in time.

Attributes of Action: How Allah's Descriptions Are Eternal
Baqillani lists action attributes and explains the eternity of descriptive speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani distinguishes attributes of action from attributes of essence, and why God's descriptive speech is eternal even though the actions occur in time.

Core Idea
Allah's speech about His actions is eternal, even though the actions themselves are temporal.

After listing the attributes of essence, Baqillani turns to attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fiʿl) such as creation, provision, and justice. He notes that while these actions happen in time, God existed before performing them. But crucially, His description of Himself as Creator, Provider, etc., is eternal because it is part of His speech — and His speech is uncreated and eternal. This passage resolves a potential theological puzzle: how can we say God is 'the Creator' eternally when creation is not eternal?

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of action attributes
Read the second part of the passage and note the attributes: creation, provision, justice, etc.
Contrast this list with the essence attributes.
2
Understand the temporal nature of acts
Baqillani says God existed before each act. This implies the act itself is not eternal.
3
Grasp the eternity of descriptive speech
Though the act is temporal, God's description of Himself as 'Creator' is eternal because it is His speech.
The speech is uncreated and encompasses all such descriptions.
4
Reflect on the proof
The proof is that the speech is uncreated and eternal; thus the attribute of being described as Creator is eternal.

Proof that Eternity is an Attribute of Essence
Baqillani proves that God's eternity (baqā') is an essential attribute using a rational argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's rational argument that God's eternity is a distinct attribute of His essence, not identical to it.

Core Idea
Eternity cannot be identical to God's essence because that would imply His origination.

Imam Baqillani tackles a key question in Islamic theology: Is God's eternity identical with His essence, or is it a distinct attribute? He begins with an imagined objection and then provides a logical argument that demonstrates the necessity of distinguishing between essence and attribute. This lesson walks through that proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the Arabic text and translation. Notice that the passage begins with a question about the evidence for eternity being an attribute of essence. Baqillani's response is a concise rational proof.
Pay attention to the logical flow: from premise to conclusion.
2
Identify the key premise
Baqillani argues that if God were eternal by Himself, He would be eternal at the moment of origination, which is impossible. Identify why this premise is crucial.
This is based on the observed impossibility of something being eternal at the instant it comes into existence.
3
Understand the analogy with created beings
Notice that Baqillani uses the case of created beings to establish that a thing's continued existence requires an attribute of eternity. This analogy supports the need for a distinct attribute in God's case.
The argument from created beings is a key move in kalam.
4
Conclude the necessity of an attribute
Trace how Baqillani reaches the conclusion that God's eternity must be through an attribute distinct from His essence, since His essence cannot be eternity itself.
This conclusion establishes that eternity is a real attribute of essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : وما الدليل على أن البقاء من صفات ذاته؟ قلنا: من قبل أنه لم يزل باقياً ، إذ كان كائناً من غير حدوث، والباقي منا لا يكون باقياً إلا ببقاء دليل ذلك استحالة بقاء الشيء في حال حدوثه . فلو بقي لنفسه، كان باقياً في حال حدوثه ، وذلك محال باتفاق . فصح أنه باقٍ ببقاء، إذ كان قديماً يستحيل أن تكون ذاته بقاءً أو في معنى الصفات .
If someone says: What is the evidence that eternity is among the attributes of His essence? We say: Because He has never ceased being eternal, since He was always existent without origination. And the eternal among us is not eternal except through an attribute of eternity; the proof of that is the impossibility of a thing's being eternal at the moment of its origination. For if He were eternal by Himself, He would be eternal at the moment of His origination, and that is impossible by consensus. So it is established that He is eternal through an attribute of eternity, since He is pre-eternal, and it is impossible that His essence be eternity itself or in the meaning of the attributes.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage contains a rational proof (dalīl ʿaqlī) that God's eternity (baqā') is an attribute distinct from His essence, i.e., one of the essential attributes (ṣifāt al-dhāt). The objection is phrased as a question: 'What is the proof that eternity is from the attributes of His essence?' The answer proceeds in several steps: First, God has never ceased being eternal, because He has always existed without coming into being (ḥudūth). Second, we observe that any created being that continues to exist (bāqī minnā) does so only by virtue of an attribute of eternity (bi-baqā'). The proof for this is the impossibility of something being eternal at the moment of its origination (istiḥālat baqā' al-shay' fī ḥāl ḥudūthihi). In other words, if something comes into existence, it cannot at that very moment be eternal; eternity requires continued existence beyond the moment of origination. Therefore, if God were eternal by Himself (i.e., without an attribute), He would have to be eternal at the moment of origination, which is contradictory. So it follows that He is eternal through an eternal attribute. The conclusion: since God is pre-eternal (qadīm), it is impossible that His essence itself be eternity or that it be like the attributes (i.e., that His essence is simply the attribute of eternity without distinction). Hence, eternity is a real, distinct attribute of His essence.

Key Terms
البقاء
صفات الذات
دليل
قديم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's eternity is identical with His essence, so no attribute is needed.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if eternity were identical to essence, then at the hypothetical moment of origination God would be eternal, which is impossible; hence, eternity must be a distinct attribute.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani argue that God's eternity must be an attribute and not identical to His essence?
A) Because if it were identical, God would be temporal.
B) Because if He were eternal by Himself, He would be eternal at the moment of origination, which is impossible.
C) Because we cannot prove eternity otherwise.
D) Because the Quran says so.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani proves that God's eternity is a distinct essential attribute because self-eternality would entail the impossible consequence of being eternal at the moment of origination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What does your confidence (or lack thereof) reveal about the depth of your understanding of God's nature and your commitment to intellectual precision in faith?
?
When you recite 'Yā Bāqī' (O Eternal One) in supplication, do you simply acknowledge that God lasts forever, or do you truly grasp that His eternity is a real, necessary perfection that distinguishes Him from every created thing that perishes? How might Baqillani's logical proof change the way you reflect on this name?

Consider a time when you assumed that 'God is eternal' is just an obvious fact that needs no justification. Will you now take the time to understand the proofs behind each of God's names and attributes, rather than accepting them passively?

The Three Meanings of 'How is He?'
Baqillani interprets the question 'how is He?' in three ways.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three possible interpretations of the question 'How is God?' and the appropriate answer for each.

Core Idea
How is God? must be answered by distinguishing its meaning, not by describing form.

Baqillani addresses a common query that can lead to misunderstanding. He shows that the question 'How is He?' can have different intentions, and each requires a different response. This lesson breaks down those meanings and preserves God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three distinct conditions introduced by 'if you mean...'.
The passage uses a conditional structure (إن أردت...).
2
Identify the three meanings
List the three possible interpretations of 'how is He?' that Baqillani addresses. For each, note the answer given.
First: composition/form/genus; second: attributes; third: treatment of creation.
3
Understand how each answer preserves tanzih
Reflect on how each answer protects God's transcendence: the first negates any physicality, the second affirms attributes without comparability, the third describes actions as just and benevolent.
This shows that the question itself is not invalid; it must be interpreted correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال قائل : وكيف هو؟ . قيل له : إن أردت بالكيفية التركيب والصورة والجنسيّة فلا صورة له ولا جنس فنخبرك عنه. وإن أردت بقولك كيف هو، أي: على أي صفة هو فهو حيّ عالم قادر سميع بصير. وإن أردت بقولك كيف هو أي: كيف صُنعه إلى خلقه فصنعه إليهم العدل والإحسان .
If someone says: How is He? He is told: If by 'how' you mean composition, form, and genericity, then He has no form nor genus about which we could inform you. And if by your saying 'how is He' you mean 'what attribute does He have?', then He is living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing. And if by your saying 'how is He' you mean 'how is His treatment of His creation?', then His treatment of them is justice and beneficence.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage addresses the question 'How is God?' (kayfa huwa) by distinguishing three possible meanings. First, if the questioner intends composition (tarkīb), form (ṣūra), or belonging to a genus (jinsiyya), then God has none of these—He is not composed of parts, has no physical form, and is not a member of any genus. Second, if the questioner asks about His attributes (ṣifa), the answer is that He is living, knowing, powerful, hearing, and seeing—these are His essential attributes. Third, if the questioner asks about His treatment (ṣunʿ) of creation, the answer is that His treatment is justice and beneficence. This again maintains tanzīh while affirming that God is known through His attributes and actions.

Key Terms
كيف
الكيفية
التركيب
الصورة
الجنسية
الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'how is God?' expects a description of form or composition.
Correction: Baqillani rejects that and redirects to attributes and actions.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the correct answer if 'how is He?' means 'what attribute does He have?'
A) He is incomparable.
B) He is living, knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing.
C) He is just and benevolent.
D) He has no attributes.

Key Takeaway
The question 'How is God?' must be understood in context; the answer depends on what is meant, and in every case God's transcendence is preserved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask 'How is God?' in your heart, which of the three meanings do you truly intend? Are you secretly hoping for a description of His form, or are you content to know Him through His attributes and actions?
?
Do you ever find yourself unsatisfied with knowing that God is living, knowing, and powerful because you crave a 'how' that satisfies your imagination? What does that craving reveal about your soul?
?
In moments of hardship, do you see God's treatment of you as justice and beneficence? If not, which of these three meanings are you neglecting, and how can you realign your understanding today?

Answering 'How is God?' by distinguishing meanings
Baqillani refutes the question 'how is God?' by distinguishing three possible meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question 'how is God?' must be answered by distinguishing its possible meanings, each receiving a different response.

Core Idea
God cannot be asked 'how?' without clarifying what you mean.

When someone asks 'how is God?', the question is ambiguous. Imam Baqillani explains that the answer depends on the intention behind the question: is it about physical composition, attributes, or actions? This lesson unpacks his three-part response and shows how it protects divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Baqillani immediately responds to the hypothetical question by setting up three conditional scenarios.
The passage uses 'in aradta...' (if you mean...) to structure the answer.
2
Identify the three meanings of 'how'
Find each of the three possible meanings Baqillani lists: composition/form/genus, attribute, and creation/treatment.
Each meaning leads to a different answer.
3
Understand the corresponding answers
For each meaning, note the answer: negation of form/genus, affirmation of divine attributes, and description of God's justice and beneficence.
The first answer negates, the second affirms attributes, the third affirms actions.
4
Reflect on the overall purpose
Consider why Baqillani begins with this distinction: it prevents any attempt to imagine God in physical or composite terms.
This is a foundational lesson in tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب
فإن قال قائل (۱۱) : وكيف (۱۲) هو؟ . قيل له : إن أردت بالكيفية التركيب والصورة والجنسيّة فلا صورة له ولا جنس فنخبرك عنه. وإن أردت بقولك كيف هو، أي: على أي صفة هو فهو حيّ عالم (١٤) قادر سميع بصير. وإن أردت بقولك كيف هو أي: كيف صُنعه إلى خلقه فصنعه (١٥) إليهم العدل والإحسان .
Section: If someone says: And how is He? It is said to him: If by 'how' you mean composition, form, and genus, then He has no form nor genus that we could inform you about. And if by your saying 'how is He' you mean 'by what attribute is He?', then He is Living, Knowing, Powerful, Hearing, Seeing. And if by your saying 'how is He' you mean 'how is His creation towards His creatures?', then His creation towards them is justice and beneficence.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the question 'how is God?' by distinguishing three possible meanings. The first meaning is asking about physical composition, form, or genus – God has none. The second meaning is asking about attributes – the answer is the divine attributes (living, knowing, etc.). The third meaning is asking about His treatment of creation – the answer is justice and beneficence. The passage prevents thinking of God in terms of physical or composite categories.

C) He is Living, Knowing, Powerful, Hearing, Seeing.
D) The question is invalid.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani teaches that the question 'how is God?' is only meaningful once the intended aspect (composition, attribute, or action) is clarified, thus protecting divine transcendence.

Existence as the Basis for Seeing God
Baqillani argues for the possibility of seeing God based on existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's rational argument that the existence of God is sufficient to make His vision possible, and how he refutes objections based on genus or temporality.

Core Idea
Existence alone makes a thing visible; God exists, so He can be seen.

Baqillani addresses a central theological question: can God be seen in the afterlife? He presents a rational proof grounded in the nature of visibility. The key insight is that visibility does not depend on specific qualities like temporality or genus, but simply on existence. This lesson unpacks his concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall argument.
2
Identify the objection and answer
Notice the question-and-answer structure. The objector asks for proof, and Baqillani provides it.
3
Analyze the core proof
Focus on the statement 'a thing is only valid to be seen by virtue of its existence.' Understand why this is the foundation.
4
Consider the refutations
Baqillani rejects two alternatives: visibility by genus and visibility by temporality. See how he argues against each.
5
Apply the reasoning to God
Conclude that since God exists (and is not subject to genus or temporality), He can be seen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكلام في جواز رؤية الله تعالى بالأبصار
فإن قال قائل: فهل يجوز أن يُرى القديم سبحانه بالأبصار؟ قيل له: أجل! فإن قال: فما الحجة في ذلك؟ قيل له: الحجة على ذلك موجود تعالى. والشيء إنما يصح أن يُرى من حيث كان موجوداً، إذا كان يرى لجنسه، لأنّا لا نرى الأجناس المختلفة؛ ولا يرى لحدوثه، إذ قد نرى الشيء في حال لا يصح أن يحدث فيها ولا لحدوث معنى فيه، إذ قد تُرى الأعراض التي لا تحدث فيها المعاني.
Chapter on Speech concerning the permissibility of seeing God, the Exalted, with the eyes. If someone says: Is it permissible for the Eternal, Glorified, to be seen with the eyes? It is said to him: Yes! If he says: What is the proof for that? It is said to him: The proof for that is that the Existent is [seen]. And a thing is only valid to be seen by virtue of its existence, if it is seen according to its genus, because we do not see different genera; nor is it seen due to its temporality, for we may see a thing in a state in which it is not valid to occur [temporality] nor due to the occurrence of an attribute in it, for the accidents are seen even though attributes do not occur in them.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage is a rational proof for the permissibility of seeing God. It begins with the chapter heading and then presents a question-and-answer format. The key argument is that visible things are seen because of their existence, not because of particular attributes like temporality or genus. Since God is Existent, He can be seen. The proof refutes objections that would limit visibility to certain types of beings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that only material bodies or temporal beings can be seen.
Correction: Baqillani argues that existence alone is sufficient, and we see non-temporal accidents, so God, being existent, can be seen.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Baqillani's rational proof for the possibility of seeing God?
A) Everything that exists can be seen.
B) God is eternal, and eternal beings are visible.
C) The Prophet Moses asked to see God, so it is possible.
D) Accidents are visible, so God as substance is visible.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that the possibility of seeing God is rationally derived from the principle that existence is the sole basis for visibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that existence alone makes a thing visible. Do you truly believe that God exists in a way that makes His vision possible? If so, why does your heart not yearn for that sight daily? What are you settling for instead?
?
If God can be seen because He exists, then the possibility of seeing Him is real. How would your life change if you woke up tomorrow with the absolute certainty that you will see God? Would your priorities shift? What would you do differently today to prepare for that moment?
?
Baqillani argues that visibility follows existence, not physicality. So the barrier to seeing God is not His nature but your own readiness. What is the greatest obstacle in your heart that prevents you from longing to see Him? Will you ask Him tonight to remove it?

Prophets Cannot Ask the Impossible: Proof of the Beatific Vision
Baqillani argues that prophets cannot ask God for the impossible, confirming the beatific vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the prophets' immunity from disrespect to argue for the possibility of seeing Allah.

Core Idea
Prophets only ask what is possible; their request to see Allah proves it is possible.

Imam Baqillani presents a concise argument for the beatific vision (ru'ya). He reasons that prophets are infallible and would never show disrespect by asking Allah for something impossible. Since the prophets asked to see Allah, the vision must be within Allah's power and therefore a real possibility. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the flow of the argument: a premise, an example, a principle, and a conclusion.
The Arabic uses a compact style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the Key Premise
Identify the initial claim: 'It is impossible to ask Him for the impossible regarding His attribute.' Understand that this sets the foundation for the argument.
3
Understand Disrespect (Istikhfaf)
Recognize that asking for the impossible is labeled as 'disrespect' (istikhfaf). Grasp why this is a serious charge, especially for prophets.
Istikhfaf implies belittling or showing irreverence.
4
Apply the Prophetic Infallibility
Connect the principle to prophets: they are immune from disrespect, so they would never ask for the impossible. Therefore, any request they make must be for something possible.
This is a key move in the argument.
5
Draw the Conclusion for the Vision
Understand that since the prophets (like Musa AS) asked to see Allah, that request must be possible. Hence, the beatific vision is valid.
The conclusion ties directly to the theological debate with the Mu'tazila.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن يسأله المستحيل في صفته، كما يستحيل أن يقول له : «ربّ كن عبداً مربوباً ومألوهاً مخلوقاً». لأن ذلك أجمع استخفاف بالله سبحانه، سواء سأله السائل لنفسه أو سأله لغيره. وليس يجوز على الأنبياء الاستخفاف بربّهم، ولا أن يكون أسلاف المعتزلة وأخلافها أعلم من الرسل بما يجوز على الله تعالى وما يستحيل في صفته. فدل ما وصفناه على صحة رؤيته.
That it is impossible to ask Him for the impossible regarding His attribute, just as it is impossible to say to Him: 'Lord, be a slave, enslaved and worshipped (مألوها), created.' Because all of that is disrespect (استخفاف) to God (سبحانه), whether the asker asks for himself or asks for another. And it is not permissible for the prophets to disrespect their Lord, nor that the predecessors of the Mu'tazila and their successors be more knowledgeable than the messengers about what is permissible for God (تعالى) and what is impossible in His attribute. Thus what we have described indicates the validity of seeing Him (رؤيته).
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a concluding argument for the validity of the beatific vision (ru'ya). Baqillani first states a premise: it is impossible to ask God for something that is impossible with respect to His attribute, such as asking Him to become a created slave. That would be disrespect. Then he argues that the prophets are free from such disrespect, and they are more knowledgeable than the Mu'tazilites about what is possible for God. Therefore, since the prophets did not ask for the impossible (and in fact asked to see God, as known from other contexts), it indicates that seeing God is possible and valid. The passage uses a reductio ad absurdum: if asking for something impossible were permissible, then prophets would not be immune from disrespect, but they are immune, so the request (for vision) must be possible.

رؤية
مستحيل
أنبياء

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the prophets asking to see God implies doubt or that it is an impossible request.
Correction: Baqillani argues that prophets are infallible and would not ask for the impossible, so their request must be for something possible, thus confirming the vision's validity.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why does the fact that the prophets did not ask for the impossible prove the validity of seeing God?
A) Because asking God for the impossible is disrespectful, and prophets are immune from disrespect.
B) Because the Mu'tazilites said it is impossible.
C) Because the vision is mentioned in the Quran.
D) Because prophets are all-knowing.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's argument shows that if the prophets, who are infallible, asked to see Allah, then seeing Allah must be possible, because they would not ask for the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The prophets, despite their closeness to Allah, humbly requested to see Him. How often do you ask Allah for something that feels 'impossible'—and if not, does that reveal a lack of trust in His power or a fear of being disrespectful? What will you ask Him for today that you've been holding back?
?
If the prophets are more knowledgeable about Allah than any scholar, including the Mu'tazila, then whose voice do you listen to more when doubts about Allah's attributes arise—the Qur'an and Sunnah, or your own limited reasoning? What step can you take this week to submit your mind to revelation?
?
Baqillani says asking for something impossible would be disrespect. Have you ever made a du'a that you thought might be 'too much' for Allah? How does knowing that the prophets asked to see Allah—the greatest request—change your willingness to ask for big things from Him? Write down one du'a you've been too shy to make, and commit to saying it today.

The Linguistic Proof for the Beatific Vision
Baqillani cites the verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة' as a proof for the beatific vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses linguistic analysis of the word 'نظر' in the Quran to prove that believers will see Allah with their eyes in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The word 'نظر' has specific linguistic conditions that prove it means physical sight, not mere reflection.

This lesson examines a key proof from Imam Baqillani for the beatific vision (ru'yah). He responds to a hypothetical question by citing a Quranic verse and providing a rigorous linguistic analysis. Understanding his argument requires careful attention to the Arabic conditions that determine the meaning of 'نظر' (looking/seeing). We will explore these conditions and see how they decisively support the doctrine that believers will see Allah in the afterlife.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the question-answer structure.
Notice that the passage begins with a hypothetical question.
2
Identify the linguistic conditions
Baqillani lists several conditions that must be met for 'نظر' to mean physical sight. Find each condition in the text. Note how he uses the example 'انظر إلى زيد بوجهك'.
There are four conditions: use with 'face', no tribal attribution, transitive with 'إلى', not transitive to two objects.
3
Understand the refutation
The argument implicitly refutes interpretations of 'نظر' as 'waiting' or 'expecting'. How does the linguistic analysis exclude those meanings?
4
Reflect on the doctrinal significance
Why is this proof important for the doctrine of seeing Allah in the afterlife? How does it connect to the broader theme of divine attributes?
This proof is part of the sam'iyyat (revealed attributes) in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب آخر فإن قال قائل : فما الدليل على وجوب رؤيته لا محالة في الآخرة؟ . قيل له: قوله تعالى : وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة . والنظر في كلام العرب، إذا قُرن بالوجه، ولم يُضَف الوجه الذي قُرن بذكره إلى قبيلة ولا عشيرة، وعُدّي بحرف الجر، ولم يُعد إلى مفعولين، فالمراد به النظر بالبصر لا غير ذلك . ألا ترى إلى قولهم انظر إلى زيد بوجهك يعنون بالعين التي في وجهك .
Another chapter. If someone says: What is the evidence for the necessity of seeing Him (رؤيته) without doubt in the afterlife? It is said to him: The saying of the Most High: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord.' And 'نظر' (looking) in the speech of the Arabs, when it is associated with the face, and the face that is mentioned is not attributed to a tribe or clan, and it is made transitive with a preposition (إلى), and it is not made transitive to two objects, then what is intended by it is vision with the eye (البصر), nothing else. Do you not see their saying: 'Look (انظر) to Zayd with your face,' meaning with the eye that is in your face.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of the beatific vision in the afterlife. Baqillani begins with a question: What is the evidence? He answers by quoting the Quranic verse from Surat al-Qiyama (75:22-23): 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة.' Then he provides a linguistic analysis of the word 'نظر'. He lays down several conditions for the word to mean actual sight: (1) it is used with 'face' (الوجه), (2) the face is not attributed to a tribe (i.e., it is not metaphorical like 'the face of the tribe'), (3) it is transitive with the preposition 'إلى' (and not with two objects, which would imply intellectual consideration), (4) it is not transitive to two objects (which would mean 'consider' or 'think'). When these conditions are met, 'نظر' means sight with the eyes. He gives an example: 'انظر إلى زيد بوجهك' — look at Zayd with your face, meaning with your eye. Therefore, the verse necessarily implies that believers will see God with their eyes in the afterlife.

Key Terms
نظر
رؤية
وجه
بصر
حرف الجر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'نظر' in the verse means waiting or expecting, not physical sight.
Correction: Baqillani's linguistic analysis shows that when used with 'face' and the preposition 'إلى', and not with two objects, it necessarily means physical vision with the eye, as confirmed by the Arabic idiom 'انظر إلى زيد بوجهك'.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's linguistic analysis, what does the word 'نظر' mean in the verse 'إلى ربها ناظرة'?
A) Waiting
B) Reflecting
C) Seeing with the eyes
D) Hoping

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's linguistic analysis of 'نظر' in the Quranic verse provides a clear proof that believers will see Allah with their eyes in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly yearn to see Allah with your own eyes, or have you become content with mere intellectual knowing? What would change in your daily worship if you lived with this certainty?
?
Are you willing to accept the clear linguistic proof of this verse even if it means abandoning a prior interpretation that felt more comfortable? What does that reveal about your trust in revelation over your own reasoning?
?
If you knew that on the Day of Judgment you would look upon your Lord as described, would you prepare for that meeting with the same urgency as you prepare for a worldly appointment?

Refuting the Mu'tazilite Objection to Beatific Vision
Baqillani refutes the Mu'tazilite objection from the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's refutation of the Mu'tazilite claim that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' negates the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The praise in the verse is about Allah's perception, not about being unseen.

The Mu'tazilites argued that if Allah praised Himself for not being seen, then seeing Him in the afterlife would contradict that praise. Baqillani responds by analyzing the structure of the verse and showing that the actual praise lies in Allah's comprehensive perception, not in the negation. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The Mu'tazilites claim that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is a praise, so if believers see Allah in the afterlife, that praise would cease, which is impossible.
This objection rests on assuming the negation is inherently praiseworthy.
2
Examine Baqillani's first response
Baqillani points out that the praise is actually in 'وهو يدرك الأبصار' (He perceives all sight), not in the negation. The negation alone is not praiseworthy, as many things are unseen without being praised.
He uses the example of tastes and accidents that cannot be seen.
3
Follow the second objection and answer
The objector suggests both attributes together form the praise. Baqillani replies that each attribute must be praiseworthy on its own, which 'not being seen' is not.
Non-existent things are also unseen yet not praised.
4
Grasp the analogy
Baqillani gives an analogy: if Allah said 'I am knowing, known, causing to exist, existent', the praise lies in 'knowing' and 'causing to exist', not in 'known' and 'existent' which are shared by others.
This shows that the negation is not the source of praise.
5
Conclude the argument
Thus, the verse does not negate the beatific vision; it only praises Allah's all-encompassing perception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة فإن قالوا : أفليس قد تمدح بقوله تعالى : لا تدركه الأبصار ، كما تمدح بقوله : بديع السموات والأرض أنى يكون له ولد ولم تكن له صاحبة فكيف يجوز أن تزول عنه مدحته ؟ قيل لهم : إنما تمدح بقوله : وهو يدرك الأبصار ولم يتمدح باستحالة إدراكه بالأبصار لأن الطعوم والروائح وأكثر الأعراض لا يجوز عندكم أن ترى بالأبصار، وليست ممدوحة بذلك. فإن قالوا : فما أنكرتم أن يكون إنما تمدح بأنه يدرك الأبصار وأنها لا تدركه؟ قيل لهم : هذا باطل، لأن الوصفين اللذين يتمدح بهما لا بد أن يكون في كل واحد منهما مدح بمجرده نحو قوله : إنه عزيز ، وعليم قدير وكل واحد من الوصفين مدحة تجرد أو انضم إلى غيره. ولما لم يكن كون المعدوم غير مدرك بالبصر مدحاً له عندنا وعندكم، بطل ما قلتم. لأن أكثر الموجودات عندكم لا يجوز أن يدرك بالأبصار، وكل المعلومات عندنا وعندكم لا يدرك بالأبصار وليست بذلك ممدوحات. ألا ترى أنه لو قال الله عز وجل : إني عالم معلوم وموجد موجود [؟]، لكان متمدحا بقوله : إني عالم موجد ولم يكن متمدحا بما ضامه من كونه معلوما وموجودا، إذا شاركه عندنا وعندكم في هذين الوصفين ما ليس بممدوح بهما؟ فكذلك المدح في قوله : وهو يدرك الأبصار ، دون قوله : ولا تدركه الأبصار.
A question: If they say: Did He not praise Himself by His saying: 'Sight does not perceive Him' (الأنعام:103), just as He praised Himself by His saying: 'Originator of the heavens and the earth, how can He have a son when He had no mate' (الأنعام:101)? So how can it be permissible that His praise should cease from Him? It is said to them: He only praised Himself by His saying: 'And He perceives all sight' (الأنعام:103), and He did not praise Himself by the impossibility of being perceived by sight, because tastes, smells, and most accidents (أعراض) cannot, according to you, be seen by sight, and they are not praised for that. If they say: Why do you not accept that He praised Himself by the fact that He perceives sight and that sight does not perceive Him? It is said to them: This is false, because the two attributes by which He praises Himself must each be a praise in itself, like His saying: 'He is Mighty, All-Knowing, All-Powerful.' And each of the two attributes is a praise whether it is isolated or joined to another. Since the fact that a non-existent thing (المعدوم) is not perceived by sight is not a praise for Him, according to us and you, what you said is invalid. Because most existents according to you cannot be perceived by sight, and all known things (المعلومات) according to us and you are not perceived by sight and are not praised for that. Do you not see that if God (عز وجل) said: 'I am knowing, known, and causing to exist, existent,' He would be praised by His saying: 'I am knowing, causing to exist,' and He would not be praised by what is included in it of being known and existent, since, according to us and you, something else shares with Him in these two attributes without being praised for them? Thus the praise in His saying: 'And He perceives all sight' is without His saying: 'Sight does not perceive Him.'
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (مسألة) where Baqillani refutes a Mu'tazilite argument. The objector argues: God praised Himself in the Quran by saying 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Sight does not perceive Him), just as He praised Himself by other verses. If Muslims claim that believers will see God in the afterlife, then that praise would cease to apply in the afterlife, which is impossible. Baqillani's answer is twofold. First, the actual praise is in the verse 'وهو يدرك الأبصار' (He perceives all sight), not in the negation of being perceived. The negation (لا تدركه) is not in itself a praise, because many things like smells, tastes, and accidents are not perceived by sight, and they are not praised for that. Second, he addresses a follow-up objection: why not say the praise includes both attributes? Baqillani argues that for an attribute to be a praise, it must be praiseworthy in itself when isolated. The attribute of 'not being perceived by sight' is not praiseworthy by itself, because non-existent things (المعدوم) also are not perceived, and they are not praised. Also, many existents (according to the Mu'tazila) cannot be seen, yet they are not praised. He gives an analogy: if God said 'I am knowing, known, causing to exist, existent', the praise is in 'knowing' and 'causing to exist', not in 'known' and 'existent', because other beings share those attributes without being praised. So the praise is confined to God's comprehension of sight, not to the inability of sight to perceive Him. Therefore, the verse does not negate the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife.

Key Terms
لا تدركه الأبصار
يدرك الأبصار
مدحة
أعراض
معدوم
موجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is a praise that would be lost if Allah is seen.
Correction: The actual praise is in 'وهو يدرك الأبصار'; the negation is not praiseworthy in itself.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani argue that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' does not negate the beatific vision?
A) Because the verse is abrogated.
B) Because the negation is not a praiseworthy attribute, as many things are unseen without being praised.
C) Because the Mu'tazila misinterpret it.
D) Because the verse only applies to this world.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani demonstrates that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' does not preclude the beatific vision because the negation is not a praiseworthy attribute in itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held onto a superficial understanding of a verse to limit Allah's power or mercy, as the Mu'tazilites did with 'لا تدركه الأبصار'? How would Baqillani's careful analysis challenge you to re-examine your own assumptions today? Commit to one verse you will study more deeply this week.
?
Baqillani shows that negation alone is not praise. In your own life, do you define your faith more by what you deny (e.g., 'I'm not like those people') than by what you affirm about Allah? How can you shift your focus to praising His positive attributes, especially those that demand action from you?
?
The verse emphasizes that Allah 'perceives all sight.' Do you truly live as if Allah sees every detail of your life, including your private moments? What would change today if you really believed that? List one specific change in habit or thought you will make starting now.

The Objection: Praise Only in Perception
Baqillani presents the objection about the verse's praise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the opponents' claim that the verse's praise is only in Allah's perception of sights, not in being unseen.

Core Idea
The opponents argue that being unseen is not inherently praiseworthy.

In this passage, Baqillani presents the objection of those who claim that the Quranic verse 'He perceives the sights' only bestows praise on the attribute of perception, not on the attribute of being unseen. They question why Muslims might accept otherwise. Let's examine this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to grasp the opponents' logic.
2
Identify the key claim
Note that they base their argument on the fact that many things, like tastes, are not seen and are not praised for it.
3
Consider their question
They ask why we don't accept that the praise is only in perception.
This sets up Baqillani's refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل لهم : إنما تمدَّح بقوله : وهو يدرك الأبصار ولم يتمدّح باستحالة إدراكه بالأبصار لأن الطعوم والروائح وأكثر الأعراض لا يجوز عندكم أن تُرى بالأبصار، وليست ممدوحة بذلك . فإن قالوا : فما أنكرتم أن يكون إنما تمدح بأنه يدرك الأبصار وأنها لا تدركه؟ قيل لهم : هذا باطل، لأن الوصفين اللذين يتمدح بهما لا بد أن يكون في كل واحد منهما مدح بمجرده نحو قوله: إنه عزيز ، وعليم قدير وكل واحد من الوصفين مـدحـة تجرَّد أو انضم إلى . ولما لم يكن كون المعدوم غير مدرك بالبصر مدحاً له عندنا وعندكم، بطل ما قلتم . لأن أكثر الموجودات عندكم لا يجوز أن يدرك بالأبصار ، وكل المعلومات عندنا وعندكم لا يدرك بالأبصار وليست بذلك ممدوحات. ألا ترى أنه لو قال الله عزّ وجلّ : «إني عالم معلوم وموجد ،(موجود)، لكان متمدحاً بقوله : إني عالم موجد» ولم يكن متمدّحاً بما ضامه من كونه معلوماً وموجوداً، إذا شاركه عندنا وعندكم في هذين الوصفين ما ليس بممدوح بهما؟ فكذلك المدح في قوله : وهو يدرك الأبصار ، دون قوله : و لا تدركه الأبصار
They were told: He only praised Himself by His saying 'And He perceives the sights' and did not praise Himself by the impossibility of perception by sights because tastes and smells and most accidents cannot, according to you, be seen by sights, and they are not praised by that. If they say: What prevents you from [acknowledging] that He only praised Himself by [the fact] that He perceives the sights and that they do not perceive Him? They were told: This is false, because the two attributes by which He praises Himself must each be praise by itself, like His saying: He is Mighty, Knowing, Powerful, and each of the two attributes is a praise whether isolated or combined. And since the state of non-existent being not perceived by sight is not praise for it according to us and you, what you said is invalid. Because most existing things according to you cannot be perceived by sight, and all known things according to us and you are not perceived by sight, and they are not praised by that. Do you not see that if Allah, Mighty and Majestic, said: 'I am Knowing, Known, and Making Existent, (Existent),' He would be praised by His saying 'I am Knowing, Making Existent' and would not be praised by what He included of being known and existent, since according to us and you what is not praised by these two attributes shares in them? So likewise the praise in His saying 'And He perceives the sights' is without [i.e., not including] His saying 'And sights do not perceive Him.'
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument about the interpretation of Quran 6:103. The objectors claim that the verse only praises God by saying 'He perceives the sights' and not by the phrase 'they do not perceive Him,' because qualities like tastes and smells are not seen and are not praised for that. They ask: why not accept that the praise is only in perception and not in being unseen? The refutation argues that the two attributes (perceiving and being unseen) must each be praiseworthy independently, like 'Mighty' and 'Knowing.' The non-existent's state of being unseen is not praiseworthy, so their objection fails. An analogy is given: if Allah said 'I am Knowing, Known, and Existent,' the praise is in being Knowing and Existent, not in being Known and Existent, because many things share those latter attributes without being praised. Thus the praise in the verse is in perception, not in being unseen.

Key Terms
يدرك الأبصار
لا تدركه الأبصار
المدح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponents believe that the verse only praises perception, not being unseen.
Correction: The verse includes both statements, but the objection claims praise is only in perception.

Checkpoint
According to the opponents, why is the attribute of being unseen not praiseworthy?
A) Because it is not mentioned in the verse.
B) Because many things like tastes are not seen and are not praised.
C) Because Allah cannot be seen.
D) Because perception is better.

Key Takeaway
The opponents argue that only the attribute of perception is praiseworthy based on the analogy with tastes and smells.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you sometimes find yourself limiting your worship of Allah to only those attributes you can perceive or understand, while neglecting the profound praise due to Him for being beyond your comprehension? What specific change can you make in your daily dhikr to include both His nearness and His transcendence?
?
The opponents here use a shallow analogy to deny the praiseworthiness of Allah being unseen. In your own heart, have you ever compared Allah to created things, even inadvertently, and thereby diminished your reverence? How can you actively guard against such comparisons in your thoughts?
?
If you truly believed that Allah's being beyond sight is a cause for praise, how would that change the way you approach Him in your prayers? Would it make you more humble, more awe-struck? Commit today to pause before each sujood and reflect on the One who sees you but whom you cannot see.

The Refutation: Each Praiseworthy Attribute Stands Alone
Baqillani refutes the objection using a logical principle and analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that both attributes (perceiving and being unseen) must be independently praiseworthy, and why the objection fails.

Core Idea
A praiseworthy attribute must be praiseworthy by itself, not by association.

Having presented the objection, Baqillani now refutes it. He argues that the two attributes in the verse must each be praiseworthy on their own. The state of non-existence being unseen is not praiseworthy, so the objection collapses. An analogy from divine names further clarifies.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation
Read the Arabic and translation of the response carefully.
2
Understand the principle
Baqillani states that each attribute (الوصفان) must be praise by itself.
This is the core logical rule.
3
Apply the principle
He shows that being unseen is not praiseworthy for non-existent things, and the same applies to most existent things.
4
Examine the analogy
Reflect on the example of 'Knowing, Known, Existent' to solidify the argument.
The praise is in knowing and making existent, not in being known or existent.

Refutation of the Objection from Negation of Sleep
Baqillani refutes an objection regarding the praiseworthiness of negating sleep.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's negation of sleep is praiseworthy and how it implies negation of all temporal attributes.

Core Idea
Negating sleep from Allah is a sign of negating all signs of temporality.

In this objection, the opponents claim that negating sleep alone is not praiseworthy because inanimate objects also lack sleep. Baqillani responds by explaining that the verse is a hint at the negation of all defects and temporal signs. Let's examine how he defends the divine praise for negating sleep.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the full passage, noting the objection raised by the opponents and Baqillani's response. Pay attention to the structure: a question followed by a detailed answer.
The objection begins with 'فإن قالوا' and the answer with 'قيل لهم'.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice that Baqillani distinguishes between Allah and other beings. He explains that Allah is praised because He negates defects that are possible for others. For other living beings, if defects are impossible, they are also praised, but they are also praised for being alive.
This distinction is crucial for understanding why the objection fails.
3
Understand the reliance on indication
Baqillani states that the mention of sleep and slumber is a hint (تنبيه) that all accidents and signs of temporality are negated from Allah. The negation is not limited to sleep alone.
This is the relied-upon (المعتمد) argument.
4
See how the objection falls
Finally, note that inanimate objects have other signs of temporality, so negating sleep alone does not make them praiseworthy. Therefore, the objection that negating sleep is not special is refuted.
The passage ends with 'فسقط بذلك ما اعترضوا عليه'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : أفليس قد تمدَّح بقوله : لا تأخذه سنة ولا نوم وما اتخذ الله من ولد ، ولم يجب أن يكون كل من شركه في ذلك ممدوحاً من الأعراض والموات؟ قيل لهم : إنما تمدَّح جل اسمه بنفي الآفات عنه مع جوازها على غيره من الأحياء، وكل حي يمتنع ذلك عليه فإنه ممدوح بـه وممدوح أيضاً بكونه حيّاً ليس بميت. فنحن إذا قلنا إنه متمدح بكونه حياً وأن السنة والنوم لا تأخذه، فقد مدحناه بالأمرين. والمعتمد في هذا أنه سبحانه ذكر السنة والنوم تنبيها على أن جميع الأعراض ودلالات الحدوث لا تجوز عليه، ولم يرد نفي السنة والنوم فقط. وفي الأعراض والميت والجماد من دلائل الحدوث مثل ما في النائم والوسنان وإنما يُمدح الشيء بنفي ما يدل على الحدوث إذا لم يكن فيه ما ينوب في الدلالة منابه . فسقط بذلك ما اعترضوا عليه
If they say: Did He not praise Himself by His saying 'Slumber does not overtake Him nor sleep' and 'Allah has not taken any son,' and it is not necessary that every thing that shares with Him in that be praised from among accidents and inanimate objects? They were told: He only praised Himself, may His name be exalted, by negating defects from Him while they are possible for other living beings, and every living being for which that is impossible is praised by it and also praised by being alive and not dead. So if we say that He is praised by being alive and that slumber and sleep do not overtake Him, we have praised Him by both matters. The relied-upon in this is that He, exalted, mentioned slumber and sleep as a hint that all accidents and indications of temporality are not permissible for Him, and He did not intend only the negation of slumber and sleep. And in accidents, the dead, and inanimate objects there are indications of temporality like those in the sleeping and the drowsy. And a thing is only praised by negating what indicates temporality when there is nothing in it that substitutes in indication in its place. So by that what they objected falls.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection-and-answer. The objectors cite verses where Allah negates sleep and having a son, and argue that not everything sharing those negations is praised (e.g., inanimate objects). The answer distinguishes that Allah's negation of sleep and slumber is meant to negate all defects and signs of temporality (ḥudūth) from Him, while these are possible for other living beings. A living being that impossibly has such defects is praised for that and for being alive. The mention of sleep is a hint that all accidents and temporal indications are impossible for Him. For inanimate objects, they have other signs of temporality, so negating sleep alone does not bring praise. Thus the objection collapses.

Key Terms
سنة
نوم
الآفات
الحدوث
دلالات الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Negating sleep alone is not praiseworthy because inanimate objects also lack sleep.
Correction: Negating sleep from Allah is praiseworthy because it indicates the negation of all temporal attributes and defects, which is not true for inanimate objects that have other signs of temporality.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani argue that the negation of sleep and slumber from Allah is praiseworthy?
A) Because sleep is a defect that is impossible for all living beings.
B) Because it indicates the negation of all signs of temporality from Allah.
C) Because only Allah can negate sleep.
D) Because inanimate objects also lack sleep, so it is not special.

Key Takeaway
The negation of sleep from Allah is praiseworthy because it implies the negation of all temporal attributes and defects.

Alternative Praise: God's Power to Prevent Perception
Baqillani proposes an alternative interpretation of the praise in the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's alternative interpretation of the praise in 'Sights do not perceive Him' as highlighting God's power to create impediments to vision.

Core Idea
The verse 'Sights do not perceive Him' praises God's power to prevent perception, not just His invisibility.

Baqillani offers a fresh perspective on the well-known verse. Instead of seeing the negation of vision as a mere description of God's transcendence, he argues that the real praise lies in God's ability to actively prevent us from seeing Him. This interpretation shifts the focus from what God is not to what God can do.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold naturally.
Notice the logical sequence: premises, contrast, conclusion.
2
Identify the key elements of the argument
Identify the premises: God perceives, exists, can be perceived, has power to prevent. Note the contrast with creation and the conclusion about praise.
The key is the idea of creating a contrary state (ḍidd) that blocks vision.
3
Reflect on the shift from negative to positive praise
Compare Baqillani's interpretation with the common understanding. How does this interpretation make the verse a praise of God's power rather than just a negation?
This connects to broader themes in kalām about God's attributes of action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن أن يكون وجه التمدح بقوله : لا تدركه الأبصار أنه تعالى يدرك الأشياء، وأنه موجود يصح أن يُدْرَك ، وأنه قادر على أن يمنعنا من إدراكه ، وأن كل من يرانا يمكن أن نراه من الخلق، وأنـه هـو تعالى قد منعنا من الإدراك له، وإن كان مدركاً لنا، وأنه ليس فيمن يدركنا ببصره من يمكنه أن يخلق فينا ما يضاد رؤيته وينفيها. فيكون متمدحاً بقدرته على خلق ما يضاد رؤيته . وكونه قادراً على خلق ضد رؤيته لازم له أبداً لا يتغيّر عنه، وكونه خالقاً لما يضاد رؤيته تمدح ببعض أفعاله
And it is possible that the aspect of praise in His saying 'Sights do not perceive Him' is that He, exalted, perceives things, and that He is existent such that He can be perceived, and that He is able to prevent us from perceiving Him, and that everyone from among creation who sees us can be seen by us, and that He, exalted, has prevented us from perceiving Him, even though He perceives us, and that there is none among those who perceive us with their sight who can create in us what opposes His vision and negates it. So He is praised for His power to create what opposes His vision. And His being able to create the opposite of His vision is necessary for Him forever, not changing from Him, and His being creator of what opposes His vision is praise by some of His acts.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage provides an alternative interpretation of the praise in the verse 'Sights do not perceive Him.' Baqillani suggests that the praise lies not in the mere negation of vision but in God's power to create impediments that prevent perception. The argument: God perceives all things, and He is existent and can be perceived. He has the power to prevent us from perceiving Him. Unlike created beings who can be seen by those who see them, no created being can create an impediment to its own vision. Thus, God's ability to create a contrary state (ḍidd) that blocks vision is an act of power that is praiseworthy. This power is eternal and unchanging. Therefore, the verse highlights God's creative power, a praise by some of His acts.

Key Terms
وجه التمدح
قادر
يمنع
يضاد رؤيته
خلق ضد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the verse purely negates vision, implying God is simply invisible.
Correction: Baqillani argues that the negation is actually a positive praise of God's power to actively prevent perception by creating a contrary state.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's alternative explanation, what is the praiseworthy aspect in the verse 'Sights do not perceive Him'?
A) That God is invisible by essence.
B) That God is able to create what opposes vision and thus prevent perception.
C) That God does not need to be seen.
D) That created beings cannot see God due to their weakness.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani reinterprets the verse as praising God's power to actively prevent vision, emphasizing His creative ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel spiritually 'blind' or distant from God, do you tend to assume He is passively absent or actively hiding Himself for a wise purpose? How would your worship change if you saw His concealment as a powerful act of His will rather than a lack of presence?
?
Is there a situation where you have resented God for not making Himself obvious to you, perhaps thinking His hiddenness is a weakness? How does Baqillani's redefinition of 'Sights do not perceive Him' challenge your complaint? What step will you take this week to praise God even in the absence of clear perception?
?
Do you treat God's invisibility as a mere fact or as a deliberate act of His sovereignty? In your prayer life, will you now consciously thank God for the very impediment that prevents you from seeing Him, trusting that it is an expression of His power and wisdom?

Refuting the Objection on Negating Sleep
Bāqillānī refutes the objection that God's praise for lacking sleep would also apply to inanimate objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Bāqillānī's response to the objection that if God is praised for lacking sleep, then inanimate objects should also be praised.

Core Idea
God's praise for lacking sleep is part of negating all defects and temporality, not just sleep.

The objectors argue from the Qur'anic verse that God is praised for not being overtaken by slumber or sleep, and they claim that inanimate objects share this trait. Bāqillānī clarifies that the praise is not merely for lacking sleep but for being alive and free from all defects. In this passage, he explains the deeper rationale and why the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objectors present their challenge: if God is praised for lacking sleep, then inanimate objects and dead things also lack sleep, so they should be praised too.
This is a classic reductio argument.
2
Examine Bāqillānī's response
Note how he distinguishes between mere negation of sleep and negation of all defects. God is praised for being alive and negating sleep as part of negating all defects.
3
Grasp the fundamental principle
Understand that praise for negating indicators of temporality applies only to living beings that are not subject to those indicators. Inanimate objects lack life and thus the praise does not apply.
This is the key logical point that defeats the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : أفليس قد تمدَّح بقوله : لا تأخذه سنة ولا نوم و ما اتخذ الله من ولد ، ولم يجب أن يكون كل من شركه في ذلك ممدوحاً من الأعراض والموات؟ قيل لهم : إنما تمدَّح جل اسمه بنفي الآفات عنه مع جوازها على غيره من الأحياء، وكل حي يمتنع ذلك عليه فإنه ممدوح بـه وممدوح أيضاً بكونه حيّاً ليس بميت. فنحن إذا قلنا إنه متمدح بكونه حياً وأن السنة والنوم لا تأخذه، فقد مدحناه بالأمرين. والمعتمد في هذا أنه سبحانه ذكر السنة والنوم تنبيها على أن جميع الأعراض ودلالات الحدوث لا تجوز عليه، ولم يرد نفي السنة والنوم فقط. وفي الأعراض والميت والجماد من دلائل الحدوث مثل ما في النائم والوسنان وإنما يُمدح الشيء بنفي ما يدل على الحدوث إذا لم يكن فيه ما ينوب في الدلالة منابه . فسقط بذلك ما اعترضوا عليه .
If they say: Did He not praise Himself by His saying: 'No slumber or sleep overtakes Him' and 'God has not taken any son'? And is it not necessary that every inanimate object and dead thing that shares in that should be praised? It is said to them: He, may His name be exalted, praised Himself only by negating defects from Him while their being possible for other living beings, and every living being for which that is impossible is praised by it and also praised by being alive and not dead. So if we say that He is praised by being alive and that slumber and sleep do not overtake Him, we have praised Him by both matters. And the relied-upon in this is that He, exalted, mentioned slumber and sleep as a notification that all accidents and indicators of temporality are not permissible for Him, and He did not intend merely to negate slumber and sleep. And among accidents, the dead, and inanimate objects are indicators of temporality similar to what is in the sleeping and drowsy. A thing is only praised by negating what indicates temporality when there is not in it something that substitutes in indication. So by that their objection falls.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objectors argue that if God is praised for lacking sleep and slumber (based on Qur'anic verses), then inanimate objects and dead things, which also lack sleep and slumber, should be praised as well. Bāqillānī answers by clarifying that God's praise is based on negating defects (آفات) from Him while those defects are possible for other living beings. Moreover, God is praised for being alive and not subject to death. So the negation of sleep is not isolated; it is part of a broader negation of all indicators of temporality (حدوث). Inanimate objects and dead things do not inherently possess the positive attribute of life that God has, so the analogy fails. The author explains that the mention of sleep and slumber serves as a reminder that all accidents and signs of temporal origination are impossible for God. The praise is for negating what indicates temporality, which applies to God because He has no other attribute that could substitute for that negation, unlike inanimate things which are not truly alive.

Key Terms
سنة
نوم
آفات
حدوث
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God is praised solely for lacking sleep, and that any entity lacking sleep deserves the same praise.
Correction: Bāqillānī explains that the negation of sleep is a symbol for negating all defects and indicators of temporality, and that such praise only applies to the living God, not to inanimate objects which lack life.

Checkpoint
According to Bāqillānī, why is God praised for not being overtaken by sleep even though inanimate objects also lack sleep?
A) Because sleep is a defect only for living beings, and God is alive while inanimate objects are not.
B) Because God's negation of sleep implies negation of all accidents and indicators of temporality, which does not apply to inanimate objects that are not truly alive.
C) Because inanimate objects also have other defects that are not negated.
D) Because the verse only applies to God, not to other things.

Key Takeaway
God's negation of sleep is a symbol for negating all defects and indicators of temporality, and only applies to the living God, not to dead objects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that God is free from sleep and all limitations, how does that contrast with your own constant need for rest and dependence? What will you do this week to consciously rely on Him more because of His self-sufficiency?
?
The objectors made a shallow comparison between God and inanimate objects. In your own life, where have you made superficial comparisons about God that diminished your awe of Him? What specific step will you take to replace that with a deeper understanding from this lesson?
?
If God's negation of sleep is a reminder that He transcends all temporal defects, how does that affect your patience when you feel weak or exhausted? Write down one practical way you will remind yourself of God's transcendence when you face your own limitations.

God's Power to Prevent Vision: First Interpretation
Bāqillānī's first interpretation based on God's power to prevent vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Bāqillānī's first interpretation of the verse, which emphasizes God's power to create what opposes vision and sees it as a praise of His ability rather than a denial of visibility.

Core Idea
God's praise lies in His power to prevent us from seeing Him, not in His invisibility.

This lesson explores the first of two interpretations offered by Imam al-Bāqillānī for the verse 'No vision perceives Him'. He argues that the verse praises God's power to prevent perception, rather than negating the possibility of seeing Him. The key is that God perceives all things and is capable of being perceived, but He actively prevents us from perceiving Him—highlighting His omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Bāqillānī's reasoning. Notice the emphasis on God's power.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'if He can be perceived, then He prevents...'
2
Identify the core claim
Bāqillānī says the aspect of praise is that God perceives things, is capable of being perceived, and has the power to prevent us from perceiving Him.
3
Analyze the comparison with creation
Notice the contrast: created beings who see each other cannot create opposition to vision in the seer. God can. This shows His unique power.
No created being can create the opposite of vision to prevent being seen.
4
Understand the eternal nature
Bāqillānī emphasizes that God's power to create the opposite of vision is eternal and unchanging. This makes the praise perpetual.
The power is not temporary; it is always true.
5
Grasp the theological point
The verse is thus about God's power, not about His invisible essence. It does not preclude the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife if He wills.
This interpretation opens the door for the beatific vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن أن يكون وجه التمدح بقوله : لا تدركه الأبصار أنه تعالى يدرك الأشياء، وأنه موجود يصح أن يُدْرَك ، وأنه قادر على أن يمنعنا من إدراكه ، وأن كل من يرانا يمكن أن نراه من الخلق، وأنه هو تعالى قد منعنا من الإدراك له، وإن كان مدركاً لنا، وأنه ليس فيمن يدركنا ببصره من يمكنه أن يخلق فينا ما يضاد رؤيته وينفيها. فيكون متمدحاً بقدرته على خلق ما يضاد رؤيته . وكونه قادراً على خلق ضد رؤيته لازم له أبداً لا يتغيّر عنه، وكونه خالقاً لما يضاد رؤيته تمدح ببعض أفعاله. ويحتمل أيضاً أن يكون وجه التمدّح في قوله لا تدركه الأبصار أنها لا تدركه جسماً مصوّراً متحيّزاً ، ولا حالا في شيء على ما يقوله النصارى، ولا مشبهاً لشيء على ما يقوله أهل التشبيه، ولا تدركه والداً ولا مولوداً على صفة من يلد أو يولد تعالى عن ذلك! ويكون القصد بذلك الردَّ على من وصفه بهذه الصفات وليس لأحد من المعتزلة التعلّق بهذه الآية، لأن الله تعالى إنما نفي عندهم من إدراك الأبصار ما أثبته لنفسه في قوله وهو يدرك الابصار . ولم يَعْنِ بذلك عند البصريين أنه يرى الأبصار، وهو تعالى عندهم لا يدرك الأبصار لأنها مما لا يصح أن يدرك ؛ ولا يرى أيضاً شيئاً بتة عند البغداديين. وإنما عني عندهم أنه يعلم الأبصار، فيجب عليهم أن يكون إنما نفى بقوله : لا تدركه الأبصار علمنا به تعالى .
And it is possible that the aspect of praise in His saying 'No vision perceives Him' is that He, exalted, perceives things, and that He exists and is such that He can be perceived, and that He is powerful to prevent us from perceiving Him, and that everyone among creation who sees us can be seen by us, and that it is He, exalted, who has prevented us from perceiving Him, though He perceives us, and that there is not among those who perceive us with their sight anyone who can create in us what opposes His vision and negates it. So He is praised by His power to create what opposes His vision. And His being powerful to create the opposite of His vision is necessary for Him forever, not changing from Him, and His being the creator of what opposes His vision is praise by some of His acts. And it is also possible that the aspect of praise in His saying 'No vision perceives Him' is that they do not perceive Him as a body, formed, located in space, nor as indwelling in something as the Christians say, nor as resembling something as the anthropomorphists say, and they do not perceive Him as a father or son according to the description of one who begets or is begotten—exalted is He above that! And the intent of that is to refute those who described Him with these attributes. And none of the Mu'tazila should cling to this verse, because God, exalted, according to them only negated from vision's perception what He affirmed for Himself in His saying 'and He perceives visions.' And according to the Basrans, He did not mean by that that He sees visions, and He, exalted, according to them does not perceive visions because they are something that cannot be perceived; and according to the Baghdadiyya, He does not see anything at all. Rather, according to them, He meant that He knows visions, so it is incumbent on them that what He negated by His saying 'No vision perceives Him' is [that we have] knowledge of Him, exalted.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage provides two possible interpretations of the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (No vision perceives Him). First, the praise lies in God's power: He perceives all things, and though He can be perceived (being existent), He has the power to prevent us from perceiving Him. Unlike created beings who see each other, no created being can create opposition to vision to prevent being seen; God can. Thus God is praised for His eternal power to create what opposes vision. Second, the praise lies in negating any physical or anthropomorphic conception: God is not a body occupying space, not indwelling in anything (refuting Christians), not resembling anything (refuting anthropomorphists), and not a parent or child (refuting those who attribute human generation). The intent is to refute those who describe God with such attributes. Then Bāqillānī argues that the Mu'tazila cannot use this verse to deny the beatific vision, because they interpret 'yudrik al-abṣār' as 'knows' the visions, not actually perceives them. According to them, God negated from Himself what He affirmed only in the sense of knowledge, so they must admit that the negation in the verse refers to our knowledge of Him—which is problematic for their position.

Key Terms
إدراك
أبصار
قدرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' proves that God cannot be seen at all, even in the afterlife.
Correction: According to this interpretation, the verse praises God's power to prevent vision, not that vision is impossible. It does not negate the possibility of seeing God if He wills.

Checkpoint
According to Bāqillānī's first interpretation, what is the main reason God is praised in the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'?
A) Because God is invisible by nature.
B) Because God has the power to create what opposes vision and prevent us from perceiving Him.
C) Because God does not need to be seen.
D) Because God only reveals Himself to prophets.

Key Takeaway
The first interpretation views the verse as a declaration of God's power to prevent vision, not as a denial of the possibility of seeing Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God’s power to prevent vision is a source of praise, how does it feel to know that your inability to see Him in this life is a direct act of His will? Does this make you long for the vision in the afterlife more intensely, or does it humble you in a different way?
?
Bāqillānī says no created being can prevent being seen, but God can. When have you felt exposed or vulnerable before others, and how does knowing that God alone controls whether you see Him affect your trust in His concealment and protection?
?
The verse 'No vision perceives Him' is not a limitation on God but a declaration of His power. How does this reframing challenge your own assumptions about God’s nature? Are you content with a God whose greatness is shown in His ability to remain veiled, or does that stir something restless in you?

Negating Anthropomorphism: Second Interpretation and Refutation
Bāqillānī's second interpretation and refutation of Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Bāqillānī's second interpretation that the verse negates any bodily or anthropomorphic attributes of God, and his argument that the Mu'tazila cannot use this verse to deny the beatific vision.

Core Idea
The verse denies that God is a body, in a place, or resembles anything, and the Mu'tazila's own interpretation undermines their denial of seeing God.

This lesson covers the second interpretation offered by Imam al-Bāqillānī, where he explains that the verse 'No vision perceives Him' refutes those who attribute physical or human-like qualities to God—such as Christians who say God indwells creation, or anthropomorphists who compare Him to creatures. It then addresses the Mu'tazila, who use this verse to deny the beatific vision. Bāqillānī turns their own interpretation against them, showing it leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the second interpretation
Bāqillānī says the verse could mean that visions do not perceive God as a body, as indwelling, as resembling anything, or as begetting/being begotten.
This refutes Christians, anthropomorphists, and pagans.
2
Identify the intended refutations
The verse targets specific false descriptions: body, location, indwelling, resemblance, and familial relations.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The Mu'tazila interpret 'yudrik' as 'knows' and deny that God sees (or perceives) visions. They use the verse to argue that God cannot be seen.
The Basrans and Baghdadiyya have slight differences.
4
Follow Bāqillānī's refutation
If 'yudrik' means 'knows', then the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' would mean 'visions do not know Him', which would negate our knowledge of God—an absurd result. So their interpretation fails.
Thus, the verse does not support their denial of beatific vision.
5
Grasp the overall lesson
The verse is about refuting false conceptions of God, not about visibility. It leaves open the possibility of seeing God in the hereafter.

Source Text
