Meaning of 'You Will Not See Me' for Moses
Bāqillānī interprets 'لن تراني' as referring to the worldly life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Bāqillānī restricts the verse 'لن تراني' to the worldly life, leaving open the possibility of seeing Allah in the hereafter.

Core Idea
God's words to Moses prohibit seeing Him in this world, not the next.

One of the key objections against the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife comes from the verse 'You will not see Me' addressed to Moses. Bāqillānī offers a succinct response that limits this statement to the worldly realm. This lesson examines his reasoning at the beginning of his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the concise format of objection and immediate response.
The passage is cut off at the page break, but the main point is clear.
2
Identify the objection
The objectors use the verse 'لن تراني' to argue that vision of God is impossible. Identify what they are assuming.
They assume the prohibition is absolute.
3
Understand Bāqillānī's reply
Bāqillānī says the verse refers only to this world (الدنيا). Note his reason: because Moses asked for vision in this world.
4
Consider the implication
If the verse is restricted to this world, then it does not negate the possibility of seeing Allah in the hereafter. Reflect on how this answer defends the Sunni position on ru'ya.
This is the key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قوله عزّ وجلّ : لن تراني قيل لهم : أراد في الدنيا، لأنه إنما سأل ربه أن يُريه نفسه في
If they say: What is the meaning of His saying, mighty and majestic: 'You will not see Me'? It is said to them: He meant in this world, because he (Moses) only asked his Lord to show Himself to him in
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a short objection-and-answer concerning the verse 'لن تراني' (You will not see Me) from the story of Moses. The objectors presumably argue that this verse proves God cannot be seen at all, even in the afterlife. Bāqillānī replies that God meant 'in this world,' because Moses specifically asked to see God in the worldly life. This answer implies that the prohibition is limited to the world, leaving open the possibility of seeing God in the hereafter. The argument is cut off at the page break, but the intended meaning is clear from the context.

Misconception: The verse 'لن تراني' means that Allah can never be seen by anyone, anywhere.
Correction: Bāqillānī clarifies that the verse is restricted to this world because Moses asked for vision in the world, so it does not negate vision in the hereafter.

Responding to the 'لن تراني' Objection
Baqillani answers that 'لن تراني' is restricted to this world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani defends the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife by restricting the verse 'لن تراني' to this world and citing the verse 'إلى ربها ناظرة'.

Core Idea
'لن تراني' is about this world, not the next.

The verse 'لن تراني' (You will not see Me) from the story of Moses is often used to argue that Allah cannot be seen at all. Baqillani shows that this interpretation is mistaken. He explains that the verse is a response to Moses' request in this world, and even if it were general, it is qualified by the verse 'إلى ربها ناظرة' which affirms vision in the hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection as presented: they ask about the meaning of 'لن تراني'. Notice how the objection assumes this verse negates all vision of Allah.
The objection is from those who deny the possibility of seeing Allah.
2
Analyze Baqillani's first answer
Baqillani responds that the verse is specifically about this world. He points out that Moses asked to see Allah in this world, so the reply is limited to that context.
This is a contextual interpretation.
3
Consider the second argument
Even if the verse were not a direct answer, it is specified by the verse 'إلى ربها ناظرة' (looking towards their Lord) from Surah Al-Qiyama, which clearly indicates the righteous will see Allah in the afterlife.
This shows that the Quran itself affirms vision of Allah.
4
Reflect on the broader doctrine
Understand that this argument supports the Sunni belief that believers will see Allah in Paradise, while also acknowledging that in this world Allah cannot be seen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة فإن قالوا : فما معنى قوله عزّ وجلّ : لن تراني ؟ قيل لهم : أراد في الدنيا، لأنه إنما سأل ربه أن يُريه نفسه في الدنيا، فقوله: لن تراني جواب هذا السؤال. ولو لم يكن جواباً، لكان أيضاً مخصوصاً بقوله : إلى ربها ناظرة .
Issue: If they say: What is the meaning of His statement, Mighty and Majestic, 'You will not see Me'? It is said to them: He meant in this world, because he (Moses) only asked his Lord to show Himself to him in this world. So His statement 'You will not see Me' is the answer to this question. And even if it were not an answer, it would also be specified by His statement 'looking towards their Lord'.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This is an objection-answer unit. The objector cites the verse 'لن تراني' (You will not see Me) from the story of Moses (Surah Al-A'raf) to argue that Allah cannot be seen at all. Baqillani replies that this verse is specific to this world because Moses asked to see Allah in this world. Therefore, 'لن تراني' is a direct response to that request and does not negate vision in the afterlife. Moreover, even if it were not an answer, it would be qualified by the verse 'إلى ربها ناظرة' (looking towards their Lord) from Surah Al-Qiyama, which affirms that the righteous will see Allah in the hereafter.

Interpreting Moses' Repentance
Baqillani offers three interpretations for Moses' statement 'تبت إليك'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's three interpretations of Moses' statement 'تبت إليك' and why they refute the claim that asking for vision was sinful.

Core Idea
Moses' repentance does not prove his request was wrong.

The objector cites Moses' words 'I turn to You in repentance' to argue that asking for vision was a sin. Baqillani responds by showing that repentance can have multiple meanings. This lesson explores his three interpretations and the linguistic flexibility of 'tawba'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the English translation. Let the structure of objection and answer become clear.
2
Identify the objector's claim
Notice that the objector uses Moses' repentance as proof that the request for vision was sinful. Baqillani first clarifies that Allah did not say Moses repented from the request itself.
3
Examine each interpretation
Baqillani presents three possible meanings for 'تبت إليك'. Identify each one and understand how it avoids implying sin.
The three interpretations are marked by 'ويحتمل أن يكون'.
4
Understand the linguistic definition
The passage ends with a general definition of 'tawba' as returning from something, which can apply to both sinful and non-sinful matters.
5
Reflect on the broader point
Consider how this defense upholds the permissibility of asking for vision and the importance of interpreting ambiguous texts in light of established principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة فإن قالوا : فما معنى قوله : تبت إليك ؟ قيل لهم : لم يقل جلّ اسمه إنه تاب من مسألته إياه الرؤية. فيمكن أن يكون ذكر ذنوباً له قد قدّم التوبة منها فجدد التوبة عند ذكرها لهول ما رأى، كما يسارع الناس إلى التوبة ويجددونها عند مشاهدة الأهوال والآيات. ويحتمل أن يكون المعنى في قوله : تبت إليك : من ترك استيذاني لك في هذه المسألة العظيمة، ومثلها ما لا يكون معه تكليف لمعرفتك والعلم بك. ويحتمل أن يكون أراد بقوله : تبت إليك أي : تبت إليك أن أسألك الرؤية لهول ما أصابني لا لأنها مستحيلة عليك، ولا لأني عاص في سؤالي كما يقول القائل : تبت من كلام فلان ومعاملته، ومن ركوب البحر، ومن الحجّ ماشياً، إذ ناله في ذلك تعب ونصب وشدّة ، وإن كان ذلك مباحاً حسناً جائزاً . والتوبة هي الرجوع عن الشيء، ذلك سُمّي الإقلاع عن الذنوب والعود إلى طاعة الله تعالى توبة منها.
Issue: If they say: What is the meaning of His statement 'I turn to You in repentance'? It is said to them: He, majestic is His name, did not say that he repented from his asking Him for vision. So it is possible that he mentioned sins for which he had already repented and renewed repentance upon mentioning them due to the terror of what he saw, just as people hasten to repentance and renew it when witnessing terrors and signs. And it is possible that the meaning in his statement 'I turn to You in repentance' is: from leaving off seeking permission from You for this great matter, and like it is something with which there is no obligation of knowing You and knowledge of You. And it is possible that he intended by his statement 'I turn to You in repentance' meaning: I turn to You in repentance from asking You for vision due to the terror that befell me, not because it is impossible for You, nor because I am disobedient in my asking, just as one says: 'I repent from speaking with so-and-so and dealing with him, and from riding the sea, and from performing Hajj on foot' when he encounters in that fatigue, hardship, and severity, even though that is permissible, good, and allowed. And repentance is returning from something; thus quitting sins and returning to obedience to Allah the Exalted is called repentance from them.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This is another objection-answer unit. The objector cites Moses' words 'تبت إليك' (I turn to You in repentance) after asking for vision to argue that the request was sinful. Baqillani replies that Allah did not say Moses repented from the request itself. He then offers three interpretations: (1) Moses may have recalled previous sins and renewed his repentance due to the terrifying experience. (2) He may have repented from seeking permission for such a great matter, which is beyond ordinary obligation. (3) He may have repented from the request itself due to the awe, but not because the request was sinful—similar to how someone might say 'I repent from' a permissible but difficult activity. Baqillani concludes by defining repentance linguistically as returning from something, which can apply to both sinful and non-sinful things.

رؤية
استيذان
تكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses' repentance shows that asking for vision was a sin.
Correction: Baqillani provides multiple interpretations where repentance does not imply sin, such as renewing repentance for past sins or repenting from a permissible but daunting request.

Checkpoint

D) He repented due to the terror he witnessed.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani defends the permissibility of asking for vision by showing that Moses' repentance can be understood in multiple ways that do not imply sin.

The Meaning of Tajalli in the Verse of Moses
Answers an objection about the meaning of tajalli in the verse of Moses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct theological interpretation of tajalli (divine self-manifestation) as removal of impediments to perception, not as God emerging from behind veils.

Core Idea
God's 'manifestation' means removing the obstacle to seeing, not appearing behind a screen.

This passage addresses a key objection about the verse where God manifests to the mountain when Moses asks to see Him. How can God 'appear' without implying physical attributes? The answer lies in the linguistic and theological understanding of tajalli as the removal of impediments to perception, consistent with divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the question-and-answer structure. The author first presents the objection about the verse, then gives the answer.
Notice the author uses a question format: 'If they ask... it is said to them'.
2
Identify the core argument
The key point is that tajalli means God removed the impediment that prevented the mountain from seeing Him, and He gave it life and perception so that it actually saw Him.
This is the literal definition given in the text.
3
Distinguish the two meanings of tajalli
The author contrasts tajalli as 'appearance from behind screens' (impossible for God) with tajalli as 'removal of impediments' (the intended meaning).
This distinction is crucial for preserving God's transcendence.
4
Connect to Arabic usage
The author supports his interpretation with examples from Arabic: colors manifesting to a blind person when sight is restored, and matters becoming clear when doubts are removed.
This shows the linguistic basis for the chosen meaning.
5
Reflect on the theological principle
Consider how this interpretation upholds that God cannot be seen in this world (except for the Prophet Muhammad in a special way) and that this world is a house of trial and faith in the unseen.
This connects to broader Ash'ari theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن سألوا عن معنى قوله : فلما تجلى ربه للجبل جعله دكا وعن تأويل ذلك وما معنى التجلّي منه . قيل لهم : معنى ذلك أنه أرى نفسه للجبل فتدكدك وصار قطعاً قطعاً لما أحبّ تعالى من إعلام موسى صلى الله عليه، أن أحداً لا يراه في الدنيا إلا لحقه ما لحق الجبل لحكمه تعالى بجعل الدنيا دار تكليف وإيمان بالغيب. ومعنى قوله : «تَجَلَّى» أي : أنه رفع عن الجبل الآفة المانعة له من رؤيته تعالى وأحياه وخلق فيه الإدراك له فرآه . وذلك وقد يكون التجلّي ظهوراً أو خروجاً من وراء السواتر والحجب . صفات الأجسام، والله يتعالى عن ذلك . وقد يكون التجلي بمعنى رفع الآفات المانعة من الإدراك . ومن قولهم : تجلت لي الألوان وتجلت للضرير المبصرات، إذا أبصر المرئيات، و «تجلى لي الأمر»، إذا زالت عوارض الشبه منه .
If they ask about the meaning of His statement: 'When his Lord manifested to the mountain, He made it flat' and about its interpretation, and what is the meaning of tajalli from Him. It is said to them: The meaning of that is that He showed Himself to the mountain, so it crumbled and became pieces, because He wanted to inform Moses, peace be upon him, that no one sees Him in this world except that what befell the mountain befalls him, due to His decree making this world a house of obligation and belief in the unseen. And the meaning of His statement: 'tajalla' is that He removed from the mountain the impediment preventing it from seeing Him, and He gave it life and created in it perception of Him, so it saw Him. And tajalli may be appearance or emergence from behind screens and veils, which are attributes of bodies, and God is exalted above that. And tajalli may be in the sense of removing impediments that prevent perception. It is from their saying: 'colors manifested to me' and 'objects manifested to the blind person' when he sees visible things, and 'the matter manifested to me' when the obscurities of doubt clear from it.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer concerning the verse about God's manifestation (tajalli) to the mountain when Moses asked to see Him. The author first states the question: what is the meaning of 'tajalla' and its interpretation? The answer explains that God showed Himself to the mountain, causing it to crumble, to teach Moses that no one can see God in this world. The key is that 'tajalla' means God removed the impediment that prevented the mountain from seeing Him, and He gave it life and perception so that it actually saw Him. The author then distinguishes between two meanings of tajalli: (1) appearing from behind screens, which is a bodily attribute and thus impossible for God, and (2) removing impediments to perception, which is the intended meaning. This is supported by Arabic usage, e.g., colors manifesting to a blind person when sight is restored, or a matter becoming clear when doubts are removed.

رؤية

إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tajalli means God emerges from behind a veil or literally appears as a physical being.
Correction: Tajalli here means God removes the impediment that prevents perception, allowing the created thing to see Him, without any physical movement or change on God's part.

Checkpoint

Tajalli in this context means God removed the obstacle that prevented the mountain from perceiving Him, not that He appeared from behind a veil.

Two Meanings of Hijab: Veil and Impediment
Defines hijab and explains its two senses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of the term 'hijab' in Islamic theology and how it applies to the vision of Allah on the Day of Judgment.

Core Idea
Hijab means prevention, not a physical barrier between Allah and His creatures.

In this lesson, we explore the term 'hijab' as used by scholars of Aqeedah. The word can refer to a physical covering or to an impediment placed in the senses. Understanding this distinction is crucial for interpreting the Quranic verse about the disbelievers being veiled from their Lord. Let's read the passage from Imam Baqillani.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find the two senses of hijab: 'satir mani' and 'afa mawdu'a fi al-basar'. Notice how each is defined.
The root meaning 'hajaba' means prevention.
3
Understand the root meaning
The root 'hajaba' means prevention. See how this applies to the chamberlain (hajib) and the verse.
This clarifies that hijab is about prevention, not a physical barrier.
4
Apply to the verse
Consider how the second meaning (impediment in sight) explains the Quranic verse about disbelievers being veiled from Allah.
This prevents misconceptions about a literal veil over Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجاب، فقد يكون بمعنى الساتر المانع ؛ وقد يكون آفة موضوعة في البصر تمنع من إدراك المرئيات. وأصل الحجب المنع ومنه سمي حاجب الأمير حاجباً لمنعه منه ودفعه عن الوصول إليه . ومنه قوله تعالى : كلا انهم عن ربهم يومئذ المحجوبون ، يعني الكفَّار، أي : إنهم ممنوعون بالآفات الموضوعة في أبصارهم من رؤيته تعالى إهانةً لهم وتفريقاً بينهم وبين المؤمنين .
As for the hijab (veil), it may be in the sense of a covering that prevents, and it may be an impediment placed in the sight that prevents perception of visible things. The root of hijb is prevention, and from this the chamberlain of the prince is called hajib because he prevents him and repels access to him. And from this is His statement, exalted: 'No! Indeed, from their Lord that day they will be veiled' (83:15), meaning the disbelievers, i.e., they are prevented by impediments placed in their sights from seeing Him, exalted, as a humiliation for them and a distinction between them and the believers.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the term 'hijab' (veil). The author explains that hijab has two meanings: (1) a physical barrier that prevents something, and (2) an impediment embedded in the eye that prevents perception of visible objects. The root meaning is 'prevention', whence the guardian of a prince is called 'hajib' because he prevents people from approaching. The author then applies this to the Quranic verse about the disbelievers being 'veiled' from their Lord on the Day of Judgment. The intended meaning is that they are prevented from seeing God by a created impediment in their eyes, as a humiliation and to distinguish them from the believers who will see Him.

ساتر

منع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hijab in the verse means a physical veil covering Allah.
Correction: The passage explains that hijab here is an impediment in the sight of the disbelievers, not a literal veil over Allah.

Checkpoint

A) A physical veil placed over God
B) An impediment in the eyes of the disbelievers
C) A barrier between God and the disbelievers
D) The disbelievers are hidden from God

Key Takeaway
The term 'hijab' in the Quranic context of disbelievers being veiled from Allah refers to an impediment in their sight, not a physical barrier over Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the disbelievers are veiled by an impediment placed in their sight, do you examine your own heart to see if any sin has created a barrier between you and perceiving Allah's greatness? What is one sin you must urgently remove today so that your vision of Him is not clouded?
?
The chamberlain prevents access to the prince. Are you actively removing the barriers you have set up that keep you from seeking Allah with sincerity? If you were to die tonight, would you be among those who are prevented or those who are allowed nearness?

Moses' Request 'Show Me' Means Vision
Baqillani refutes the semantic reinterpretation of Moses' request.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why linguistic analysis proves that Moses asked for physical sight, not metaphorical knowledge.

Core Idea
Moses asked for vision, not knowledge—Arabic grammar settles the debate.

The opponents of literal divine vision argue that Moses' request 'Rabbi arini anzur ilayka' could be a metaphor for knowledge. Imam Baqillani refutes this by turning to the Arabic language itself. This lesson examines the three linguistic arguments he provides to prove that the request was for actual sight.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the author presents the opponents' claim and then responds.
Pay attention to the phrase 'qila lahum' which marks the beginning of the refutation.
2
Identify the three linguistic arguments
The author gives three distinct reasons why the metaphorical interpretation is invalid. Find each one and note how each is based on Arabic usage.
The arguments are introduced by 'li-anna' (because) or 'wa-li-anna' (and because).
3
Reflect on the role of language in theology
Consider why linguistic precision matters in theological debates. The author uses established Arabic usage to settle the meaning of a Quranic verse.
This shows that kalam relies on sound linguistic principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم أن يكون موسى عليه السلام ، إنما أراد بقوله : رب ارني انظر إليك أي : «عرفني نفسك اضطراراً» أو أرني آية من آيات «الساعة»؟ . قيل لهم : أنكرنا ذلك لأنه غير جائز في اللغة. لأن القائل لا يجوز أن يقول لمن يسمع كلامه ويعرفه ولا يشك فيه «أرني أنظر إليك» وهو يريد عرفني نفسك أو «أرني فعلا أفعالك». هذا غير مستعمل في اللسان . ولأن النظر إذا أطلق فليس معناه إلا رؤية العين. وإن أريد به العلم، فبدليـل ولأن النظر الذي في الآية معدّى بقوله : «إِلَيْكَ»؛ والنظر المعـدى بـ «إلى» لا يجوز في كلام العرب أن يراد به إلا نظر العين . فبطل ما قالوا .
If they say: What prevents you from [accepting] that Moses, peace be upon him, only intended by his statement: 'My Lord, show me [that] I may look at You' to mean 'Make me know Yourself necessarily' or 'Show me a sign of the Hour'? It is said to them: We reject that because it is not permissible in the language. For it is not permissible for a speaker to say to one whose speech he hears and knows and does not doubt, 'Show me [so that] I may look at You,' while meaning 'Make me know Yourself' or 'Show me an act of Your acts.' This is not customary in the tongue. And because 'nazar' when used absolutely means only sight of the eye; if knowledge is intended, then [it requires] a proof. And because the 'nazar' in the verse is transitive by 'ila', and 'nazar' made transitive by 'ila' is not permissible in Arabic speech for it to mean anything except sight of the eye. So what they said is invalidated.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is another objection-and-answer concerning Moses' request 'Rabbi arini anzur ilayka' (My Lord, show me, that I may look at You). The opponents argue that Moses might have meant 'make me know Yourself' or 'show me a sign of the Hour.' The author rejects this on linguistic grounds: (1) A person does not say 'show me that I may look at you' to someone he already knows and hears, if he means 'make me know'; this usage is unattested. (2) The word 'nazar' (look) when unqualified means sight of the eye; if it means knowledge, a contextual proof is needed. (3) The verb is modified by 'ila' (to), and the construction 'nazara ila' in Arabic always means physical sight, not knowledge. Thus, Moses' request was for the actual vision of God.

Key Terms
نظر
إلى
رؤية
علم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some claim that Moses' request 'Rabbi arini anzur ilayka' was a request for knowledge, not physical sight of Allah.
Correction: The Arabic construction 'nazara ila' exclusively denotes physical sight, and the context of the verse does not provide any proof for a metaphorical interpretation.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that Moses meant 'make me know'?
A) Because Moses never asked for knowledge
B) Because the phrase 'anzur ilayka' with 'ila' can only mean sight
C) Because it is impossible for a prophet to ask for knowledge
D) Because the Quran says otherwise

Key Takeaway
The Arabic construction 'nazara ila' exclusively denotes physical sight, so Moses' request was for vision of Allah, not for knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Allah's word, do you accept its plain meaning even when it challenges your assumptions, or do you reinterpret it to fit your comfort? This lesson shows that even a prophet's words are taken literally. How will you change your approach to ambiguous verses?
?
Moses (AS) asked to see Allah with his eyes. Do you have that same longing to see your Lord? Or have you become satisfied with intellectual knowledge about Him? What will you do today to cultivate that desire?
?
Baqillani argues using Arabic grammar. Are you learning Arabic to understand the Quran directly, or are you relying on translations that might miss such nuances? What step will you take this week to learn the language of revelation?

Linguistic Proof for Visual Seeing of God
Baqillani argues that the Quranic phrase indicates visual seeing based on Arabic usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses Arabic linguistic patterns to prove that the Quranic phrase about seeing Allah indicates actual visual sight.

Core Idea
Context determines meaning; 'nazar' with 'wajh' and 'ila' means vision.

Baqillani addresses a key theological question: does 'looking at their Lord' in the Quran mean literal sight? He provides a linguistic argument based on Arabic usage. By analyzing the construction, he shows that the phrase necessarily implies visual perception. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the meanings of 'nazar'
Read the passage and note the four possible meanings of 'nazar' in Arabic: waiting, reflection, mercy, and sight. Recognize that the word alone is ambiguous.
This ambiguity makes context crucial.
2
Analyze the specific construction
Focus on the verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة'. Notice that 'nazar' is coupled with 'wajh' and made transitive with 'ila'. Baqillani argues this forces the meaning of physical sight.
The face is a body part; 'ila' indicates direction.
3
Compare with other Quranic uses
Examine the examples Baqillani gives: 'فانظر إلى طعامك' (meaning sight) versus 'فناظرة بم يرجع المرسلون' and 'ما ينظرون إلا صيحة' (meaning waiting). See how the absence of 'wajh' and 'ila' changes the meaning.
Also note the poetry example reinforcing the pattern.
4
Conclude the argument
Synthesize the evidence: the consistent linguistic pattern confirms that the verse in Surah Al-Qiyamah refers to visual seeing of Allah. This is a rational proof for the attribute of seeing.
This argument relies on expert knowledge of Arabic usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما صح أن قوله : وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة يقتضي النظر إليه بالأبصار لأن النظر في كلام العرب يحتمل وجوها : منها نظر الانتظار، ومنها الفكر والاعتبار ومنها الرحمة والتعطف ومنها الإدراك بالأبصار ؛ وإذا قرن النظر بذكر الوجه وعُدّي بحرف الجرّ ولم يُضَف الوجه إلى قبيلة وعشيرة الوجه الجارحة التي توصف بالنضارة التي تختص بالوجه الذي فيه العينان فمعناه رؤية الأبصار. ألا ترى إلى قوله عزّ وجلّ : فانظر إلى طعامك وشرابك لم يتسنه أي : انظره بعينك . ولما لم يرد بقوله تعالى : فناظرة بم يرجع المرسلون نظر العين، لم يُعَدّه بـ (إلى) ولا قرنه بالوجه. وكذلك قوله: ما ينظرون إلا صيحة واحدة، لما أراد به الانتظار دون نظر الأبصار، لم يقرنه بالوجه ولا عداه بـ «إلى». قال الشاعر: فإن يك صدر هذا اليوم ولى فإن غدا لناظره قريب فلم يقرن النظر بذكر الوجه ولا عداه بـ «إلى» لما أراد الانتظار.
And when it is established that His saying: 'Faces on that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord' necessitates seeing Him with eyes, because 'nazar' in the speech of the Arabs admits several meanings: among them the look of waiting, and among them reflection and consideration, and among them mercy and affection, and among them perception with eyes. And when 'nazar' is coupled with mention of 'face' and is made transitive by the preposition 'ila', and the face is not attributed to a tribe or clan, the face is the limb that is described as radiant, which is specific to the face that contains the two eyes, then its meaning is seeing with eyes. Do you not see His saying, Mighty and Majestic: 'Look at your food and your drink, it has not rotted' meaning: look at it with your eye. And since He did not intend by His saying: 'Then they will be watching to see what the messengers return' the seeing of the eye, He did not make it transitive with 'ila' nor couple it with 'face'. And likewise His saying: 'They do not await except one blast' when He intended waiting not ocular sight, He did not couple it with 'face' nor make it transitive with 'ila'. The poet said: 'If the front of this day has passed, then tomorrow is near for its observer.' He did not couple 'nazar' with mention of face nor make it transitive with 'ila' because he intended waiting.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic argument by Imam al-Baqillani proving that the Quranic verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة' (75:22-23) necessarily means visual seeing (ru'ya) of Allah on the Day of Resurrection. He begins by stating that the word 'nazar' in Arabic has multiple meanings: waiting, reflection, mercy, and sight. However, when 'nazar' is linked with the word 'face' (wajh) and made transitive with the preposition 'ila', it specifically means ocular vision. He supports this with the Quranic verse 'فانظر إلى طعامك وشرابك' (2:259) where the same construction means 'look with your eye'. Conversely, in verses where 'nazar' means waiting, such as 'فناظرة بم يرجع المرسلون' (27:35), it is not used with 'ila' nor coupled with 'face'. He also cites a line of poetry where 'nazar' means waiting, and there the poet avoids coupling with 'face' and the preposition 'ila'. Thus, the linguistic pattern confirms that the verse about the believers seeing their Lord refers to physical vision.

Key Terms
نظر (nazar)
الوجه (al-wajh)
إلى (ila)
الانتظار (al-intidhar)
الإدراك بالأبصار (al-idrak bil-absar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think 'nazar' always means looking with eyes, ignoring its other meanings.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that 'nazar' has multiple meanings, and the specific construction (with 'wajh' and 'ila') determines the intended sense.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what linguistic feature forces the meaning of physical sight in the phrase 'إلى ربها ناظرة'?
A) The word 'nazar' alone
B) The presence of 'wajh' and the preposition 'ila'
C) The radiance of the faces
D) The future tense

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'إلى ربها ناظرة' in Surah Al-Qiyamah, due to its inclusion of 'wajh' and the preposition 'ila', conclusively indicates visual sight of Allah on the Day of Resurrection, based on established Arabic linguistic patterns.

Response to Objection from Jamil ibn Ma'mar's Poetry
Baqillani responds to an objection from poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani interprets a poetic line that seems to use 'nazar' in a non-visual sense, and how it actually supports the rule that 'nazar ila' with eyes implies physical sight.

Core Idea
Poetic usage does not contradict the rule; even 'nazar ila' in poetry implies ocular vision when context demands.

In defending the interpretation of the verse 'towards their Lord looking' as meaning actual visual seeing, Baqillani addresses objections from Arabic poetry. The first objection cites a line by Jamil ibn Ma'mar that appears to use 'nazar' with 'ila' to mean waiting. In this lesson, we examine Baqillani's response, showing that the line actually implies physical sight accompanied by humility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response passage
Read the Arabic text of Jamil's line and Baqillani's answer. Notice how he reinterprets the poet's intention.
2
Analyze the key term 'nazar'
Identify how 'nazar' is used in the verse and in the objection. Baqillani argues that the poet meant physical sight with submission.
3
Understand the refutation
Baqillani shows that even if the word 'nazar' can mean waiting, in this context it carries the sense of actual looking due to the word 'basar' (eye) implied.
4
Connect to the main argument
Reflect on how this supports the rule that 'nazar ila' with the eye entails ocular vision.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فما معنى قول جميل بن معمر إني إليك لما وعدت لناظر نظر الدليل إلى العزيز القاهر قيل لهم : معناه نظر العين المقرون بالذل والانكسار، لأنه نظر إليه ببصره مقتضياً متنجزاً لوعده نظر الذليل إلى العزيز القاهر فإن قالوا : فما معنى قول حسان بن ثابت : وجوه ناظرات يوم بدر إلى الرحمن يأتي بالفلاح قيل لهم : أراد نظر الأبصار إلى سماء الرحمن وترقب النصر عند رميهم بالأبصار إلى الجهة التي منها يرجى النصر ؛ وقوله إلى الرحمن يعني به إلى سماء الرحمن وجهة الرغبة إليه، ولم يرد الانتظار فإن قالوا : فما أنكرتم أيضاً أن يكون الله تعالى إنما أراد بقوله : إلى ربها ناظرة ، أي : إنها إلى جنان ربّها وأفعاله وعظيم ما
Then if they say: 'What is the meaning of the saying of Jamil ibn Ma'mar: "I am towards you, since you promised, a looker, the look of a guide to the Mighty, the Dominant"?' It is said to them: 'Its meaning is the look of the eye accompanied by humility and submissiveness, because he looked at him with his eye, demanding and seeking fulfillment of his promise, the look of the lowly to the Mighty, the Dominant.' Then if they say: 'What is the meaning of the saying of Hassan ibn Thabit: "Faces looking on the day of Badr towards the Most Merciful, He brings success"?' It is said to them: 'He intended the sight of eyes towards the sky of the Most Merciful, and anticipation of victory when they cast their eyes towards the direction from which victory is hoped. And his saying "towards the Most Merciful" means towards the sky of the Most Merciful and the direction of desire towards Him, and he did not intend waiting.' Then if they say: 'What prevents you also from [the possibility] that Allah Almighty intended by His saying "towards their Lord looking" meaning that they are towards the gardens of their Lord and His actions and the greatness of what...'
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to the linguistic argument that the verse indicates visual seeing, along with Baqillani's responses. The first objection cites a line by Jamil ibn Ma'mar: 'I am towards you, since you promised, a looker...' which uses 'nazar' with 'ila' but apparently means waiting or expectation, not physical sight. Baqillani responds that here it actually means the look of the eye accompanied by humility and submissiveness, as the poet is looking at the one who promised, expecting fulfillment. The second objection quotes Hassan ibn Thabit: 'Faces looking on the day of Badr towards the Most Merciful...' which again uses 'nazar' with 'wajh' and 'ila'. Baqillani explains that here it means looking with the eyes toward the sky (the direction of Allah) while awaiting victory, not merely waiting. The third objection proposes an alternative interpretation: perhaps the verse 'إلى ربها ناظرة' means looking to His gardens, actions, and greatness. This objection is cut off on the page, but Baqillani's refutation would likely follow. These responses demonstrate that even poetic usages conform to the rule that 'nazar' with 'wajh' and 'ila' implies ocular vision, and alternative interpretations are invalid.

Key Terms
الذل (al-dhull)
الانكسار (al-inkisar)
اقتضاء (iqtida')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the poetic line uses 'nazar' solely to mean waiting without any visual element.
Correction: Baqillani shows that the line actually implies physical sight because it speaks of looking with the eye (basar) and the context demands a visual act.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what accompanies the look in Jamil's line?
A) Joy
B) Humility and submissiveness
C) Anger
D) Surprise

Key Takeaway
Even poetic uses of 'nazar ila' that seem to imply waiting actually involve physical sight when properly interpreted in context.

Baqillani shows that even a line of poetry, when properly understood, supports the theological truth. Response to Objection from Hassan ibn Thabit's Poetry
Baqillani responds to a second objection from poetry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani interprets a second poetic line, showing that 'nazar ila' can refer to looking towards the sky while awaiting mercy, still involving visual sight.

Core Idea
Looking towards the sky in anticipation of victory is still a physical act of seeing.

The second objection cites Hassan ibn Thabit's line about faces looking towards the Most Merciful on the day of Badr. Critics claim this shows 'nazar ila' can mean waiting without sight. Baqillani responds that it means looking with the eyes towards the sky (the direction of Allah) while awaiting victory, thus still entailing ocular vision.

The Self-Defeating Objection to Seeing God
Baqillani presents a conditional dilemma against the objectors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani exposes the logical flaw in the objectors' claim that God's visibility implies corporeality.

Core Idea
The objection against Seeing God is self-defeating: it forces abandoning tawhid or nullifying the question.

Baqillani presents a conditional argument that the objectors themselves must accept. If they insist that knowledge, existence, and life require corporeality, then God would have to be a body. But this leads to a dilemma: either they accept that (abandoning tawhid) or reject it (nullifying their own question). This exposes the weakness of their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if this were necessary, then...'
The passage presents a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the three conditional examples
The passage lists three analogies: knowledge, thingness, and life with attributes. Each is used to argue that God must be a body.
3
Understand the dilemma
Baqillani points out that accepting the conclusion abandons tawhid, while rejecting it nullifies the original question. The objectors cannot have both.
This shows the argument is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال لهم لو وجب هذا لوجب، إذا كان معلوماً بالقلوب وموجوداً، أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً وفي العالم أو غيره من الأماكن أو ما يُقدَّر تقدير الأماكن، لأننا لم نعقل معلوماً إلا كذلك؛ وإذا كان شيئاً أن لا يخلو من أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً، لأننا لم نعقل شيئاً إلا كذلك؛ وإذا كان عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً أن يكون جسماً مجتمعاً ذا حيّز في الوجود، لأننا لم نعقل حيّاً عالماً قادراً رائياً إلا كذلك. فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا التوحيد؛ وإن أبوه أبطلوا ما سألوا عنه. وأما قولهم: إنه لو رُئِي بالأبصار لوجب أن يكون متمثلاً متخيّلاً فإنهم إن أرادوا به أنه إذا تعدم رؤيته نتخيل له شكلاً أو جسماً ونعتقد أنا رأينا ذا هيئة وشكل، فإن ذلك باطل لقيام الدليل على أن القديم سبحانه ليس من جنس المرئيات وإن عنوا به أنا نعلم بعد رؤيته أننا رأينا شيئاً ليس كمثله شيء، فإن ذلك صحيح. وتسمية هذا العلم تخيُّلاً وتمثلاً باطل بالإجماع.
It is said to them: If this were necessary, then it would be necessary, if He is known by hearts and existent, that He be a body or a substance or an accident, and in the world or other places or what is measured by the measure of places, because we have not intellectually apprehended a known thing except thus; and if He is a thing, that He is not free from being a body or a substance or an accident, because we have not intellectually apprehended a thing except thus; and if He is knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, that He be a body, composed, possessing location in existence, because we have not intellectually apprehended a living, knowing, powerful, seeing thing except thus. If they pass over that, they abandon tawhid; and if they refuse it, they nullify what they asked about. As for their statement: 'If He were seen by eyes, He would necessarily have to be represented and imagined' – if they mean by it that when His vision occurs, we imagine for Him a form or a body and we believe that we have seen something endowed with a shape and form, then that is false due to the establishment of the proof that the Eternal (glorious is He) is not of the genus of visible things; but if they mean by it that we know after seeing Him that we have seen something unlike anything else, then that is correct. And naming this knowledge 'imagination' and 'representation' is false by consensus.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that God's visibility would entail His being a body. Baqillani first states the objectors' argument: if God is known by hearts and exists, He must be a body, substance, or accident, and be located somewhere, because we know no existent otherwise. Similarly, if He is a thing, He must be one of these categories; and if He is living, knowing, etc., He must be a composite body with location. The objectors then say that if the opponent (the Ahl al-Sunna) accepts this, they abandon tawhid (by affirming a body), and if they reject it, they nullify their own question (since they asked for proof). Baqillani then turns to the specific statement that seeing God would require Him to be represented (mutamaththil) and imagined (mutakhayyal). He distinguishes two interpretations: if they mean that sight necessitates forming a mental image of a shape or body, that is false because proof shows God is not like visible things. If they mean that after seeing we know we saw something incomparable, that is correct. Calling this knowledge 'imagination' or 'representation' is rejected by consensus.

Key Terms
توحيد
رؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that visibility requires corporeality because all visible things we know are bodies.
Correction: Baqillani shows that this analogy is false because God is not like other existents; the argument leads to a dilemma.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the objectors accept the conclusion that God must be a body?
A) They nullify their question.
B) They abandon tawhid.
C) They prove God's existence.
D) They affirm God's attributes.

Key Takeaway
The objectors' argument that God must be a body if seen is self-defeating because it leads to either abandoning tawhid or rejecting their own premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about Allah, do you ever unconsciously imagine Him with human limitations? What specific attributes of Allah do you find hardest to affirm without reducing them to your own experience?
?
The objectors refused to accept that Allah could be seen without being a body. Are there any matters of faith you have rejected because they didn't fit your logical categories? Will you submit to what the Qur'an and Sunnah affirm, even if your intellect cannot fully grasp it?
?
Baqillani's argument shows that flawed reasoning leads to contradiction or disbelief. Reflect on your own reasoning about Allah: have you ever dismissed a revealed truth because you thought it didn't make sense? How can you cultivate intellectual humility in matters of theology?

The True Meaning of Seeing God: No Representation
Baqillani distinguishes two meanings of representation in relation to seeing God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's distinction between two interpretations of 'representation' and why seeing God does not imply forming a mental image of a body.

Core Idea
Seeing God does not require imagining a body; it is simply knowing we have seen something incomparable.

The objectors claim that if God were seen, He would have to be 'represented' (mutamaththil) and 'imagined' (mutakhayyal). Baqillani clarifies that this can mean two different things. If they mean we form a mental image of a shape, that is false. But if they mean we simply know after seeing that we have witnessed something unlike anything else, that is true. However, calling that 'imagination' is a misnomer rejected by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the distinction between two possible meanings.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The first interpretation is that seeing God leads to forming a mental image of a body. The second is that we know we saw something incomparable.
Baqillani accepts the second but rejects the first.
3
Understand why the first is false
Baqillani states that proof shows God is not like other visible things, so no mental image is necessary.
4
Note the consensus
Calling the correct knowledge 'imagination' is false by consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقال لهم لو وجب هذا لوجب، إذا كان معلوماً بالقلوب وموجوداً، أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً وفي العالم أو غيره من الأماكن أو ما يُقدَّر تقدير الأماكن، لأننا لم نعقل معلوماً إلا كذلك؛ وإذا كان شيئاً أن لا يخلو من أن يكون جسماً أو جوهراً أو عرضاً، لأننا لم نعقل شيئاً إلا كذلك؛ وإذا كان عالماً قادراً سميعاً بصيراً أن يكون جسماً مجتمعاً ذا حيّز في الوجود، لأننا لم نعقل حيّاً عالماً قادراً رائياً إلا كذلك. فإن مروا على ذلك تركوا التوحيد؛ وإن أبوه أبطلوا ما سألوا عنه. وأما قولهم: إنه لو رُئِي بالأبصار لوجب أن يكون متمثلاً متخيّلاً فإنهم إن أرادوا به أنه إذا تعدم رؤيته نتخيل له شكلاً أو جسماً ونعتقد أنا رأينا ذا هيئة وشكل، فإن ذلك باطل لقيام الدليل على أن القديم سبحانه ليس من جنس المرئيات وإن عنوا به أنا نعلم بعد رؤيته أننا رأينا شيئاً ليس كمثله شيء، فإن ذلك صحيح. وتسمية هذا العلم تخيُّلاً وتمثلاً باطل بالإجماع.
It is said to them: If this were necessary, then it would be necessary, if He is known by hearts and existent, that He be a body or a substance or an accident, and in the world or other places or what is measured by the measure of places, because we have not intellectually apprehended a known thing except thus; and if He is a thing, that He is not free from being a body or a substance or an accident, because we have not intellectually apprehended a thing except thus; and if He is knowing, powerful, hearing, seeing, that He be a body, composed, possessing location in existence, because we have not intellectually apprehended a living, knowing, powerful, seeing thing except thus. If they pass over that, they abandon tawhid; and if they refuse it, they nullify what they asked about. As for their statement: 'If He were seen by eyes, He would necessarily have to be represented and imagined' – if they mean by it that when His vision occurs, we imagine for Him a form or a body and we believe that we have seen something endowed with a shape and form, then that is false due to the establishment of the proof that the Eternal (glorious is He) is not of the genus of visible things; but if they mean by it that we know after seeing Him that we have seen something unlike anything else, then that is correct. And naming this knowledge 'imagination' and 'representation' is false by consensus.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection that God's visibility would entail His being a body. Baqillani first states the objectors' argument: if God is known by hearts and exists, He must be a body, substance, or accident, and be located somewhere, because we know no existent otherwise. Similarly, if He is a thing, He must be one of these categories; and if He is living, knowing, etc., He must be a composite body with location. The objectors then say that if the opponent (the Ahl al-Sunna) accepts this, they abandon tawhid (by affirming a body), and if they reject it, they nullify their own question (since they asked for proof). Baqillani then turns to the specific statement that seeing God would require Him to be represented (mutamaththil) and imagined (mutakhayyal). He distinguishes two interpretations: if they mean that sight necessitates forming a mental image of a shape or body, that is false because proof shows God is not like visible things. If they mean that after seeing we know we saw something incomparable, that is correct. Calling this knowledge 'imagination' or 'representation' is rejected by consensus.

Key Terms
تمثيل
تخيل
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think that seeing God would mean imagining a physical form.
Correction: Baqillani clarifies that seeing God is unlike seeing any created thing; we do not form a mental image.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is correct about the claim that if God were seen He would be 'represented'?
A) It means we imagine a shape for Him.
B) It means we know after seeing that we saw something unlike anything else.
C) It is true in both interpretations.
D) It is false in all interpretations.

Key Takeaway
Seeing God does not cause representation in the mind; it is simply an experience of seeing the Incomparable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about seeing Allah in the Hereafter, do you ever catch yourself trying to imagine a shape or form? How does Baqillani's distinction challenge that habit? What concrete step will you take today to stop attempting to picture Him?
?
If seeing Allah is an experience of the Incomparable — something unlike any other sight — what does that do to your longing for it? Are you longing for a mental image, or for the real, unseen Reality? Ask yourself honestly and commit to purifying your hope.
?
Baqillani says it is false by consensus to call the knowledge after seeing 'imagination'. Have you ever described your understanding of God in words that limit Him (e.g., 'I imagine God as...')? Will you resolve from now on to avoid any phrase that suggests a likeness, and instead speak only of His incomparability?

Why God Is Not Seen Now Despite Possibility
Baqillani refutes the argument that God must be seen now if visibility is possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the argument that God must be seen now if His vision is possible, using examples from the world and analogies with angels and prophets.

Core Idea
Possibility does not guarantee actuality; vision depends on additional conditions.

The objectors argue that since no obstacle prevents seeing God, we should see Him now. Baqillani counters this by showing that the absence of obstacles does not force actual vision. He provides concrete examples where conditions determine whether something is seen, demonstrating that divine vision requires specific circumstances beyond mere possibility.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objectors' argument: if God can be seen, why do we not see Him now? They claim no obstacles (subtlety, distance, veil) apply to God.
Notice the assumption that absence of obstacles necessitates vision.
2
Examine Baqillani's refutation
Baqillani lists examples where obstacles are absent but vision does not occur: seeing fine things with increased perception, seeing distant things, seeing veiled things with strong light, the dying seeing the Angel of Death, and the Prophet seeing Gabriel while others did not.
These show that vision depends on factors beyond just the absence of obstacles.
3
Grasp the key principle
Baqillani concludes that what prevents vision cannot coexist with vision. Since the alleged obstacles (subtlety, etc.) are not present with God, they are not relevant; vision requires specific conditions that may or may not be met.
This refutes the necessity of seeing God now.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم من أنه لو جاز أن يُرى لرأيناه الساعة؟ لأن ما من رؤية ما يجوز أن يُرى منتفية عنه، وهي الرقة واللطافة والحجاب والبعد وذلك مستحيل على الله تعالى فوجب أن نراه، لو كان مما يجوز أن يُرى. يقال لهم: ليس فيما ذكرتم شيء يمنع من رؤية المرئي. لأنا نرى اللطيف مع لطافته عند زيادة الإدراك، ونرى البعيد بعده، ونرى المحجوب إذا قوي الإدراك وزاد الشعاع عندكم فأنفذ خروقه والمحتضر بالموت يرى ملك الموت، ونحن لا نراه وإن كنا بحضرته وكذلك الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يرى جبريل عليه السلام والصحابة لا تراه. وكذلك الملائكة يرى بعضهم بعضاً مع رقتهم، ونحن لا نراهم. وما منع من رؤية الشيء لا يجوز أن يقارن الرؤية له. فوجب أن لا يكون فيما ذكرتموه شيء يمنع رؤية المرئيات، كما أن الجهل المانع من العلم بالشيء لا يجوز أن يقارنه العلم بحال.
For they said: 'What prevents you from [accepting] that if it were permissible for Him to be seen, we would see Him now? Because there is no [obstacle] to seeing what is permissible to be seen that is absent from Him, and they are subtlety, fineness, veil, and distance, and that is impossible for God, may He be exalted, so it would be necessary that we see Him, if He is among what is permissible to be seen.' It is said to them: 'There is not in what you mentioned anything that prevents the vision of the visible. For we see the fine thing despite its fineness when perception increases, and we see the distant thing despite its distance, and we see the veiled thing when perception strengthens and the ray increases according to you and penetrates its openings, and the one who is dying sees the Angel of Death, but we do not see him even if we are in his presence, and likewise the Messenger, may God bless him and grant him peace, used to see Gabriel, peace be upon him, but the Companions did not see him. Likewise the angels see each other despite their subtlety, but we do not see them. And what prevents the vision of a thing is not allowed to accompany the vision of it. Therefore it is necessary that there is not in what you mentioned anything that prevents the vision of visible things, just as ignorance, which prevents knowledge of a thing, is not allowed to accompany knowledge of it at all.'
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and Baqillani's refutation regarding why God is not seen now if His vision is possible. The objectors argue that if God were visibilis (jāza an yurā), we would see Him now because there is no obstacle (māni') like subtlety, fineness, veil, or distance that applies to God. Since these obstacles are impossible for God, seeing Him would be necessary. Baqillani refutes by giving counterexamples: we see fine things when perception is strong, we see distant things, we see veiled things when light penetrates, the dying see the Angel of Death while others do not, the Prophet saw Gabriel while Companions did not, and angels see each other despite their subtlety. Thus, the absence of obstacles does not force actual vision in every case; vision depends on additional conditions. He further states that what prevents vision cannot coexist with vision, so the obstacles listed are not relevant to God's case. He analogizes with knowledge: ignorance prevents knowledge but cannot coexist with it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is possible to see, it must be seen by everyone at all times.
Correction: Vision depends on conditions; possibility does not guarantee actuality. Baqillani shows that many things are not seen despite being visible due to lack of conditions.

Checkpoint
What example does Baqillani use to show that the absence of obstacles does not necessitate actual vision?
A) Seeing a mountain from afar.
B) The dying person seeing the Angel of Death while others do not.
C) Seeing the sun in daylight.
D) Seeing one's own hand.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani refutes that God must be seen now by demonstrating that vision depends on specific conditions, not merely the absence of obstacles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely asked, 'If God exists, why don't I see Him?' Has this lesson revealed that your demand is based on an assumption that absence of obstacles guarantees vision? Where else in your faith do you demand immediate proof on your terms? What condition of your heart might need strengthening before you can perceive what is already there?
?
The dying see the Angel of Death while others remain unaware. Death will purify your perception. Are you living as if you have all the time to cultivate that sight, or are you actively training your inner vision now through obedience and reflection?
?
You might have doubted God's closeness because you do not perceive Him with your senses. But Baqillani teaches that non-perception does not mean absence. How does this lesson confront your impatience and reliance on sensory evidence? Will you now seek the conditions—humility, sincerity, remembrance—that enable the heart to see what the eye cannot?

The True Obstacle to Seeing God
Baqillani identifies the obstacle to seeing God as a deficiency in our perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the obstacle to seeing God lies in a deficiency in our own perception, not in God's nature or distance.

Core Idea
The obstacle to seeing God is in our eyes, not in God.

In this passage, Baqillani directly addresses a key objection about the possibility of seeing God. He identifies the true barrier not as something external, but as an internal limitation in our sensory faculties. This sets the stage for understanding why the vision of God is possible in the afterlife when that barrier is removed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get its rhythm. Then read the literal translation. Notice how the objectors pose the question and how Baqillani responds.
This is a concise question-and-answer format.
2
Identify the question being answered
The objectors ask: 'What is the obstacle to seeing these things?' The 'these things' refers to God and subtle beings. Identify what the obstacle is according to Baqillani.
3
Analyze Baqillani's answer
Baqillani states the obstacle is 'the existence of what opposes their perception in our sight'. Reflect on what this means: the problem is in our faculty of sight, not in the object seen.
The word 'yuḍāddu' indicates a direct opposition or contradiction.
4
Understand the implication
Baqillani adds that if God removed that opposing factor, we would perceive them. This shows the obstacle is contingent and removable, making the vision of God possible in the afterlife.
This directly counters the objection that God cannot be seen at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا: فما المانع من رؤية هذه الأمور؟ قيل لهم: هو وجود ما يضاد إدراكها في أبصارنا، ولو رفعه تعالى أدركناها. وهذا المانع هو الذي يمنع من رؤية الله تعالى في هذا الوقت.
For they said: 'What then is the obstacle to seeing these things?' It was said to them: 'It is the existence of what opposes their perception in our sight, and if the Exalted removed it, we would perceive them. And this obstacle is what prevents the vision of God, the Exalted, at this time.'
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This short passage is a direct question and answer about the specific obstacle preventing vision of subtle things like angels and of God. The objectors ask: what exactly prevents seeing these things? Baqillani answers: the obstacle is the existence of something in our eyes (abṣārinā) that opposes (yuḍāddu) the perception of such objects. If God were to remove that opposing factor, we would perceive them. He then states that this same obstacle is what prevents seeing God in this worldly life. Thus, the barrier is not in God's nature but in the limitation of human sensory faculties.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the obstacle to seeing God is a veil or distance from God Himself.
Correction: Baqillani teaches that the obstacle is a deficiency in our own perception, specifically an opposing factor in our sight, not a veil or distance.

Checkpoint

B) Their extreme subtlety.
C) The existence of something in our sight that opposes their perception.
D) The presence of a veil placed by God.

Key Takeaway
The obstacle to seeing God is a deficiency in our sensory perception, which God can remove.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The obstacle is in you, not in God. How does that change the way you seek closeness to Allah? Will you stop blaming circumstances and start asking Him to remove the barrier in your own perception?

God's Power to Create Perception: The Elephant and the Atom
Baqillani affirms God's power to create perception of small and not large things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Baqillani affirms God's absolute power to create any perception, even contradictory ones, and why this is foundational for the possibility of seeing God.

Core Idea
God can make you see an atom but not an elephant beside it—such is His absolute power.

This passage presents a clever objection against the Ash'ari view that vision depends solely on God's will. The objectors attempt a reductio ad absurdum: if God can create perception of a tiny atom while preventing perception of a large elephant, then perhaps we cannot trust our senses. Baqillani calmly affirms the principle, showing that God's power is limitless and not constrained by ordinary causation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the Arabic and translation of the objectors' challenge. Notice they propose a scenario where God creates perception of an atom but prevents perception of an elephant beside it.
This is meant to be an absurd scenario to test the Ash'ari position.
2
Understand Baqillani's Response
Baqillani replies that such a scenario is permissible under God's power. Reflect on why he does not deny it.
He affirms divine omnipotence without qualification.
3
Consider the Follow-up Objection
The objectors press further: if you allow that, then maybe right now there are elephants and rivers you don't see. This challenges the reliability of sense perception. Baqillani's implied response is that possibility does not entail actuality.
The objection does not disprove the possibility of seeing God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا فأجيزوا أن يخلق الله فيكم إدراك ذرّة ويمنعكم من إدراك فيل إلى جنبها! قيل لهم: هذا جائز عندنا في قدرة الله تعالى. فإن قالوا: فأجيزوا الساعة ذلك، وشكّوا في أن بحضرتكم فيلة وجمالاً وأنهاراً جارية، وأنتم لا ترون ذلك، وإن كنتم ترون ما هو أصغر منه.
For they said: 'Then allow that God create in you perception of an atom and prevent you from perception of an elephant next to it!' It was said to them: 'This is permissible with us according to the power of God, the Exalted.' For they said: 'Then allow that at this hour, and doubt that in your presence there are elephants, camels, and flowing rivers, and you do not see them, while you see what is smaller than them.'
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage contains two objections and Baqillani's response. The first objection: if the obstacle is subjective, then God could create perception of a tiny atom while preventing perception of a large elephant beside it. Baqillani affirms that this is permissible in God's power. The objectors then press: if so, allow that right now you might have elephants and rivers before you that you don't see, while you do see smaller things. This is a reductio ad absurdum argument challenging the claim that vision depends on God's direct will. Baqillani's response is implied: yes, it is possible in principle, but in reality we know by experience that we see large things when they are present; the objection does not disprove the possibility of seeing God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's power is limited by natural perception or that perception must always correspond to the size of objects.
Correction: Baqillani affirms that God can create perception of an atom while preventing perception of an elephant, showing that His power is absolute and not tied to physical properties.

Checkpoint

A) He denies it is possible.
B) He says it is permissible by God's power.
C) He says it would contradict God's wisdom.
D) He says it is impossible because of natural laws.

Key Takeaway
God's power is not constrained by ordinary perception; He can create any perception He wills, which underpins the possibility of seeing Him in the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God can prevent you from seeing an elephant beside you, then your perception of His presence or absence is entirely in His control. Are you relying on your senses to confirm God's reality, or on His own guarantee? This week, identify one moment where you trusted your senses over God's revelation—what would it mean to let go of that reliance?
?
The objectors feared that if God's power is absolute, we could never be certain of anything. In your life, what fears of uncertainty hold you back from fully relying on God? How would your daily decisions change if you truly believed God could create any perception He wills, even in your heart?

The Possibility of Seeing God and the Nature of the Barrier
Baqillani argues from analogies for the possibility of seeing God and identifies the barrier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument from analogies that the barrier to seeing God is not in God's essence but in our perception, and that removal of this barrier makes vision possible.

Core Idea
Seeing God is possible; the barrier is in us, not in Him.

Baqillani begins with examples of subtle beings that are seen under certain conditions—the distant, the hidden, the dying seeing the angel of death, the Prophet seeing Gabriel, angels seeing each other. He argues that what prevents vision cannot coexist with vision, so the objects mentioned are not inherently invisible. Then he answers the question about the barrier: it is something opposing perception, which God can remove.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage from the analogies to the first conclusion
Read the Arabic and translation of the opening analogies. Notice the pattern: each example shows that subtle things can be seen when the conditions allow. Identify the common point: the barrier is not intrinsic.
2
Understand the principle: what prevents vision cannot accompany vision
Focus on the statement: 'وما منع من رؤية الشيء لا يجوز أن يقارن الرؤية له'. This is the logical move that makes the argument work.
This principle is parallel to ignorance and knowledge.
3
Examine the objection and answer about the barrier
Read the objection: 'فما المانع من رؤية هذه الأمور؟' and Baqillani's answer: 'هو وجود ما يضاد إدراكها في أبصارنا'. Note that the barrier is external to the object, not part of its essence.
This barrier is what prevents seeing God now.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللطيف مع لطافته عند زيادة الإدراك، ونرى البعيد بعده، ونرى المحجوب إذا قوي الإدراك وزاد الشعاع عندكم فأنفذ خروقه والمحتضر بالموت يرى ملك الموت، ونحن لا نراه وإن كنا بحضرته وكذلك الرسول صلى الله عليه كان يرى جبريل عليه السلام والصحابة لا تراه. وكذلك الملائكة يرى بعضهم بعضاً مع رقتهم، ونحن لا نراهم. وما منع من رؤية الشيء لا يجوز أن يقارن الرؤية له. فوجب أن لا يكون فيما ذكرتموه شيء يمنع رؤية المرئيات، كما أن الجهل المانع من العلم بالشيء لا يجوز أن يقارنه العلم بحال .

فإن قالوا : فما المانع من رؤية هذه الأمور؟ قيل لهم : هو وجود ما يضاد إدراكها في أبصارنا، ولو رفعه تعالى أدركناها. وهذا المانع هو الذي يمنع من رؤية الله تعالى في هذا الوقت .

فإن قالوا فأجيزوا أن يخلق الله فيكم إدراك ذرّة ويمنعكم من إدراك فيل إلى جنبها ! قيل لهم : هذا جائز عندنا في قدرة الله تعالى .

فإن قالوا فأجيزوا الساعة ذلك، وشكّوا في أن بحضرتكم فيلة وجمالاً وأنهاراً جارية، وأنتم لا ترون ذلك، وإن كنتم ترون ما هو أصغر منه . قيل لهم : لولا أننا مضطرون إلى العلم بأن ذلك ليس بموجود، لأجزناه وليس يجب أن نشك اليوم في أن الله تعالى قد فعل كل مقدور من عنده، كما لا يجب أن نشك في أنه قد خلق اليوم إنساناً لا أبوين وفرساً لا من نتاج وناراً غير محرقة لشيء وتمراً لا من نخل ولبناً لا من ضرع، وأنه قد أحيا الأموات بسائر الأقطار وعرج بنا البارحة إلى ملكوت السموات ثم ردّنا إلى مضاجعنا، وأنه قد أمات كل من فارقناه يوماً أو ساعةً من أقاربنا وأصدقائنا ألف مرّة ثم أحياهم بعد ذلك وإن كان ذلك أجمع مقدوراً الله تعالى . فبطل بذلك ما سألتم عنه .
The subtle [being] with His subtlety when perception increases, and we see the distant after its distance, and we see the hidden if perception is strong and the ray increases according to you so it penetrates its apertures, and the one in agony of death sees the angel of death, while we do not see him even though we are in his presence, and likewise the Prophet, peace be upon him, used to see Gabriel, peace be upon him, while the Companions did not see him. And likewise the angels, some of them see others despite their subtlety, while we do not see them. And what prevents the vision of a thing is not permissible to accompany the vision of it. So it is necessary that there is nothing in what you have mentioned that prevents the vision of visible things, just as ignorance that prevents knowledge of something is not permissible to be accompanied by knowledge at all.

If they say: What is the barrier to seeing these things? It is said to them: It is the existence of something that opposes their perception in our sight, and if God removes it we would perceive them. And this barrier is what prevents seeing God at this time.

If they say: Then allow that God creates in you the perception of an atom and prevents you from perceiving an elephant next to it! It is said to them: This is permissible according to us within the power of God.

If they say: Then allow that now, and they doubt that in your presence there is an elephant, camels, and flowing rivers, and you do not see that, even though you see what is smaller than it. It is said to them: Were it not that we are compelled to know that that does not exist, we would allow it. And it is not necessary that we doubt today that God has done every possible thing from Him, just as it is not necessary that we doubt that He has created today a human being without parents, a horse without breeding, fire not burning anything, dates without a palm tree, milk without an udder, and that He has revived the dead in all regions, and ascended us last night to the kingdom of the heavens then returned us to our beds, and that He has caused to die everyone we parted from a day or an hour among our relatives and friends a thousand times then revived them after that, even though all of that is possible for God. So what you asked about is invalidated.
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is an argument for the possibility of seeing God (ru'ya). The author begins by noting examples of subtle bodies that are perceived under certain conditions: the distant is seen when perception increases, the hidden is seen when light penetrates, the dying see the angel of death, the Prophet saw Gabriel while companions did not, angels see each other despite their subtlety. The point is that the barrier to seeing a thing is not intrinsic to the thing but to the capacity of the perceiver. Therefore, what prevents vision cannot coexist with vision. Consequently, the objects of vision mentioned (God) are not inherently invisible; rather, the barrier is in our sight. Then an objection is raised: what is the barrier to seeing these subtle things? The answer: it is the existence of something opposing perception in our sight. If God removes that barrier, we would see them. This same barrier prevents seeing God now. Then a second objection: if God can remove barriers, then allow Him to make you perceive an atom but prevent you from perceiving an elephant next to it. The author agrees it is possible. Then a third objection: then allow that now, we might doubt there is an elephant and camels and rivers present that we don't see. The answer: we are compelled by necessity to know that such things are not present, so we do not doubt. Similarly, we do not doubt that God could have created extraordinary things like a human without parents, but we know He hasn't because of our immediate knowledge. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
رؤية
مانع
إدراك
لطافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Subtle beings like angels and God cannot be seen because of their subtlety, implying an intrinsic impossibility.
Correction: Baqillani shows they can be seen when the barrier is removed, so subtlety is not an absolute barrier.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the barrier to seeing the angel of death?
A) The angel's subtlety
B) Something opposing perception in our sight
C) God's will to hide him
D) The fact that we are not dying

Key Takeaway
The barrier to seeing God is not in His essence but in our perception, and it can be removed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you knew for certain that you would see God in the afterlife, how would your priorities today change? What are you clinging to that might be a barrier to that vision even now?
?
When you read that the barrier is 'something opposing perception in your sight,' what do you think that barrier is in your own heart? Pride? Distraction? Sin? Will you ask God tonight to remove it?
?
The Prophet saw Jibril while others did not. What would it take for you to become sensitive enough to perceive the unseen realities that are all around you right now? Are you content with the veil?

Refuting Objections Based on Divine Power and Necessity of Knowledge
Baqillani refutes two objections by affirming divine power and appealing to necessity of knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the objection that if God can enable perception of small things while hiding large ones, we must be able to doubt our immediate knowledge.

Core Idea
Extraordinary possibilities do not invalidate our compelled knowledge.

Baqillani now faces two more objections. The second objection suggests that God could make us perceive an atom but not an elephant next to it. He agrees this is possible. The third objection says that if such a thing is possible, we must doubt whether giants and rivers are present now. Baqillani answers by appealing to our compelled knowledge (اضطرار) that such things are not present, and by extension we do not doubt God's ordinary actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection and answer
Read 'فإن قالوا فأجيزوا أن يخلق الله فيكم إدراك ذرّة ...' and the response. Note that Baqillani agrees this is within divine power.
This shows that divine power is unlimited.
2
Read the third objection and the detailed answer
The objection asks to allow doubt about present things. Baqillani's answer lists many extraordinary events that are possible but we know they haven't happened because of compelled knowledge.
The list includes creating humans without parents, reviving dead, etc.
3
Understand the principle of compelled knowledge
Baqillani argues that we are ضروري (necessarily) aware that such things haven't occurred, so we do not doubt. Similarly, we don't doubt that God's action follows usual patterns.
This refutes the objection that possibility forces doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللطيف مع لطافته عند زيادة الإدراك، ونرى البعيد بعده، ونرى المحجوب إذا قوي الإدراك وزاد الشعاع عندكم فأنفذ خروقه والمحتضر بالموت يرى ملك الموت، ونحن لا نراه وإن كنا بحضرته وكذلك الرسول صلى الله عليه كان يرى جبريل عليه السلام والصحابة لا تراه. وكذلك الملائكة يرى بعضهم بعضاً مع رقتهم، ونحن لا نراهم. وما منع من رؤية الشيء لا يجوز أن يقارن الرؤية له. فوجب أن لا يكون فيما ذكرتموه شيء يمنع رؤية المرئيات، كما أن الجهل المانع من العلم بالشيء لا يجوز أن يقارنه العلم بحال .

فإن قالوا : فما المانع من رؤية هذه الأمور؟ قيل لهم : هو وجود ما يضاد إدراكها في أبصارنا، ولو رفعه تعالى أدركناها. وهذا المانع هو الذي يمنع من رؤية الله تعالى في هذا الوقت .

فإن قالوا فأجيزوا أن يخلق الله فيكم إدراك ذرّة ويمنعكم من إدراك فيل إلى جنبها ! قيل لهم : هذا جائز عندنا في قدرة الله تعالى .

The subtle [being] with His subtlety when perception increases, and we see the distant after its distance, and we see the hidden if perception is strong and the ray increases according to you so it penetrates its apertures, and the one in agony of death sees the angel of death, while we do not see him even though we are in his presence, and likewise the Prophet, peace be upon him, used to see Gabriel, peace be upon him, while the Companions did not see him. And likewise the angels, some of them see others despite their subtlety, while we do not see them. And what prevents the vision of a thing is not permissible to accompany the vision of it. So it is necessary that there is nothing in what you have mentioned that prevents the vision of visible things, just as ignorance that prevents knowledge of something is not permissible to be accompanied by knowledge at all.

?

?
Ponder: if you were presented with a miracle that seems to contradict your senses, would you immediately doubt your own perception? Or would you recognize that your immediate knowledge tells you something is not right? How does this lesson challenge you to hold onto what you know necessarily, even when extraordinary claims are made?
?
Baqillani argues that we are compelled to know certain things. Is there an area of your life where you pretend to be uncertain about something your heart already knows? Why do you resist that knowledge, and what would change if you surrendered to it?
