The Qur'anic Proof for God's Universal Will
Baqillani opens the chapter on God's will and gives a Qur'anic proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Qur'anic proof for God's will encompassing all events, including obedience and disobedience.

Core Idea
God's will is absolute; the Qur'an explicitly states He does whatever He wills.

Imam Baqillani begins the chapter on divine will by addressing a key question: does God will both good and evil? His answer is direct — he cites a Qur'anic verse that establishes God's unrestricted power to will. This short proof is foundational for understanding the scope of divine will in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the question-and-answer structure.
The passage is very concise; every word matters.
2
Identify the question being asked
The questioner asks: 'Why do you say God wills obedience, disobedience, and all events?' Understand the implied objection: that God might not will evil acts.
3
Identify Baqillani's answer
Baqillani responds by quoting the Qur'anic verse 'فعال لما يريد' (Doer of whatever He wills). This is his entire proof.
The verse is from Surah Hud 11:107 and Surah al-Buruj 85:16.
4
Understand the proof logic
The verse implies that whatever occurs in existence is willed by God. Since obedience and disobedience occur, they must be willed by Him.
This is a direct textual proof, not rational speculation.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider the theological significance: God's will is all-inclusive. This answers the objection and affirms His absolute sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القول فى أن الله تعالى مريد لجميع المخلوقات

إن قائل قائل : فلم قلتم إن الله تعالى مريد للطاعة والمعصية وسائر الحوادث؟ قيل له : لأن الله تعالى قال في كتابه : فعال لما يريد.
Chapter on the statement that God Almighty is willing for all creatures. A questioner says: Why do you say that God Almighty is willing for obedience and disobedience and all events? It is said to him: Because God Almighty said in His Book: 'Doer of whatever He wills.'
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a short proof for the divine attribute of will (irada). The author begins a new chapter asserting that God is willing for all creatures. Then an objection is posed: why do you claim that God wills both obedience and disobedience (i.e., good and evil)? The answer is based on the Qur'anic verse from Surah Hud and Surah al-Buruj: 'فعال لما يريد' (Doer of whatever He wills). This verse implies that whatever occurs in existence is willed by God, including acts of obedience and disobedience. The argument is that since God's will is absolute and nothing happens outside His will, He must will all events, whether they are acts of obedience or disobedience.

Key Terms
مريد
إرادة
الطاعة
المعصية
حوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God does not will evil acts because He does not command them.
Correction: The Qur'anic verse 'فعال لما يريد' shows that God's will encompasses all events, including evil acts, even though He does not approve of them. His will is not limited to what He commands.

Checkpoint
What proof does Baqillani give that God wills all creatures?
A) Rational proof
B) Consensus
C) Qur'anic verse
D) Prophetic tradition

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic verse 'Doer of whatever He wills' is the primary evidence that God's will includes all events, both good and evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills both your obedience and your disobedience, then why do you still feel shame when you sin? Does that shame come from a belief that you have escaped His will or from a recognition of His command?
?
Reflect on a moment of hardship you blamed on 'fate' but never thanked God for. If God truly does whatever He wills, can you bring yourself to thank Him even for the painful events? What holds you back?
?
You claim to believe that God wills everything. Yet when something goes against your plans, do you react with anger or resentment? What does that reaction reveal about your actual belief in His absolute will? Commit today to reciting 'فعال لما يريد' when you feel that frustration rising.

God Wills All Actions of Creatures: Proof from Revelation and Reason
Baqillani presents Qur'anic and rational arguments for God's universal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main proofs (Qur'anic and rational) that establish God's will over all actions of creatures.

Core Idea
God's will covers all events because He creates them and says He does whatever He wills.

Imam al-Baqillani begins this chapter by stating the position that Allah wills all actions of creatures, including obedience and disobedience. He then provides two types of evidence: first, clear Qur'anic verses that affirm God's absolute will, and second, a rational proof that since God creates all actions, He must also will them. This lesson unpacks those proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the two sources of proof
The passage presents two types of evidence: a direct Qur'anic verse and then two conditional verses. Find each one and notice how Baqillani uses them.
3
Understand the rational connection
Baqillani says: since God is the doer of all actions, He must will them. Follow the logical step from doing to willing.
This connects God's attribute of power (doing) to His attribute of will.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself why establishing God's universal will is important for the discussion of divine attributes and predestination.
This lays the groundwork for rejecting objections about human free will versus divine decree.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القول فى أن الله تعالى مريد لجميع المخلوقات إن قائل قائل : فلم قلتم إن الله تعالى مريد للطاعة والمعصية وسائر الحوادث؟ . قيل له : لأن الله تعالى قال في كتابه : فعال لما يريد . وقد قام الدليل على أنه فعال لسائر أفعال الخلق وجميع الحوادث، فوجب أنه مريد لذلك أجمع . و لقوله تعالى : ولو شاء الله ما فعلوه وقوله : ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعا فأخبر أنه لو لم يرد منهم العصيان والتكذيب والخلاف، لما كان ولا فعلوه ؛ وأنه لو شاء أن يؤمنوا جميعاً ، لأمنوا . فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم أن يكون إنما أراد أنه لو أراد ذلك على سبيل الإكراه والإلجاء، لكان لا محالة ؟ . قيل لهم : لو جاز أن يريد الإيمان منهم طوعاً فلا يكون ولا يلحقه عجز ولا تقصير عن بلوغ مراده، لجاز أن يريد منهم ذلك على سبيل الإلجاء والإكراه ولا يكون ولا يلحقه عجز ولا تقصير عن بلوغ مراده. فإن قالوا : لو لم يتم ما أراد منهم على سبيل الإلجاء، لدلّ ذلك على عجزه عن فعل سبب يلجئهم به إلى الإيمان، من ترهيب وإحضار نكال وغير ذلك والعجز غير جائز عليه . قيل لهم : ولو لم يتما ما أراده من إيمانهم طوعاً واختياراً، لــدل ذلك على عجزه عن فعل لطف وسبب من الأسباب يختارون عند فعله الإيمان وذلك منتف عنه .
Chapter on the statement that God Almighty is willing for all creatures. If someone says: Why do you say that God Almighty wills obedience and disobedience and all events? It is said to him: Because God Almighty said in His Book: 'Doer of whatever He wills.' And the evidence has established that He is doer of all actions of creatures and all events, so it is necessary that He wills all of that. And by His statement: 'And if God had willed, they would not have done it,' and His statement: 'And if your Lord had willed, all who are on earth would have believed all together.' So He informed that if He had not intended from them disobedience, denial, and opposition, it would not have happened nor would they have done it; and that if He had willed that they all believe, they would have believed. If they say: What prevents you from considering that He intended that if He had willed that through coercion and compulsion, it would inevitably be? It is said to them: If it were possible that He intends belief from them voluntarily and it does not happen, and no incapacity or deficiency befalls Him in achieving His intention, then it would also be possible that He intends from them that through compulsion and coercion, and it does not happen, and no incapacity or deficiency befalls Him in achieving His intention. If they say: If what He intended from them through compulsion did not occur, that would indicate His incapacity to do a cause that would compel them to belief, such as intimidation and presenting punishment and other things, and incapacity is not permissible for Him. It is said to them: And if what He intended from their belief voluntarily and by choice did not occur, that would indicate His incapacity to do a kindness and a cause from causes by which they would choose belief when He does it, and that is negated from Him.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a chapter proving that God wills all actions of creatures. It begins with an objection: why do you say God wills obedience and disobedience? The answer is twofold: first, the Qur'an says 'Doer of whatever He wills' (فعال لما يريد), and second, evidence shows God creates all actions, so He must will them. Additionally, verses like 'And if God had willed, they would not have done it' and 'If your Lord had willed, all on earth would have believed' imply that if God had not willed disobedience or had willed universal belief, it would have occurred. Then a first objection: perhaps God's will is only conditional upon coercion? Baqillani responds with a parity argument: if voluntary will can fail without incapacity, then coercive will can also fail without incapacity; no distinction is possible. The second objection: if coercive will fails, that would indicate incapacity to compel. The answer is a parallel: if voluntary will fails, that would indicate incapacity to provide incentives (لطف), which is also impermissible for God. Therefore, both objections are invalid, and God's will for all things is established.

Key Terms
إرادة
فعال لما يريد
مريد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills all actions, then humans have no free will and are not responsible.
Correction: Baqillani is only establishing that nothing occurs outside God's will; he is not negating human choice or accountability. The relationship between divine will and human choice is a deeper topic addressed elsewhere.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, which Qur'anic verse is used as direct evidence that God does whatever He wills?
A) 'And if God had willed, they would not have done it'
B) 'Doer of whatever He wills'
C) 'If your Lord had willed, all on earth would have believed'
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
God's will over all actions of creatures is proven by His self-description as 'Doer of whatever He wills' and by conditional verses that link the occurrence of actions to His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills all your actions, including your sins, does that make you more reliant on His mercy or more complacent in your disobedience? How does this truth change the way you seek forgiveness?
?
When you see someone who has not yet believed, do you recognize that God could have willed their belief but did not, and does that humble you in your own faith, knowing it is only by His will that you believe?
?
The verse 'Doer of whatever He wills' leaves no room for questioning God's decrees. Is there an event in your life that you have struggled to accept? How does this teaching challenge you to submit to His will completely?

Answering the Coercion and Incapacity Objections
Baqillani responds to two objections regarding God's will and incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's symmetrical argument against objections that try to limit God's will to coercive scenarios or that claim incapacity if His will is not fulfilled.

Core Idea
If voluntary will can fail without deficiency, then coercive will can too—so objections prove nothing.

Having established that God wills all actions, Baqillani anticipates two objections. Opponents might argue that God's will for disbelief is only conditional on coercion, or that if His coercive will failed, He would be incapable. Baqillani dismantles both by showing that the same reasoning would apply to voluntary will, thus the objections are self-defeating.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first objection
The opponent suggests that God's will for disbelief might only be through coercion (إكراه), meaning He compels them to disbelieve. Baqillani's response uses a parity argument.
2
Follow the parity argument
Baqillani says: If it is possible that God wills voluntary belief but it does not happen without deficiency, then the same could be said for coercive will. So the objection does not limit God's will.
The key is that deficiency is not attributed to God in either case.
3
Understand the second objection
The opponent then says: If coercive will fails, that shows incapacity to compel. Baqillani again gives a symmetrical answer: if voluntary will fails, that would show incapacity to provide incentives (لطف), which is also impossible for God.
Thus, the objection is invalid because it would apply equally to voluntary will.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Notice how Baqillani uses logic to show that the opponents' objections are not specific to coercive will—they would also undermine voluntary will, which the opponents accept. So the objections fail.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب القول فى أن الله تعالى مريد لجميع المخلوقات إن قائل قائل : فلم قلتم إن الله تعالى مريد للطاعة والمعصية وسائر الحوادث؟ . قيل له : لأن الله تعالى قال في كتابه : فعال لما يريد . وقد قام الدليل على أنه فعال لسائر أفعال الخلق وجميع الحوادث، فوجب أنه مريد لذلك أجمع . و لقوله تعالى : ولو شاء الله ما فعلوه وقوله : ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعا فأخبر أنه لو لم يرد منهم العصيان والتكذيب والخلاف، لما كان ولا فعلوه ؛ وأنه لو شاء أن يؤمنوا جميعاً ، لأمنوا . فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم أن يكون إنما أراد أنه لو أراد ذلك على سبيل الإكراه والإلجاء، لكان لا محالة ؟ . قيل لهم : لو جاز أن يريد الإيمان منهم طوعاً فلا يكون ولا يلحقه عجز ولا تقصير عن بلوغ مراده، لجاز أن يريد منهم ذلك على سبيل الإلجاء والإكراه ولا يكون ولا يلحقه عجز ولا تقصير عن بلوغ مراده. فإن قالوا : لو لم يتم ما أراد منهم على سبيل الإلجاء، لدلّ ذلك على عجزه عن فعل سبب يلجئهم به إلى الإيمان، من ترهيب وإحضار نكال وغير ذلك والعجز غير جائز عليه . قيل لهم : ولو لم يتما ما أراده من إيمانهم طوعاً واختياراً، لــدل ذلك على عجزه عن فعل لطف وسبب من الأسباب يختارون عند فعله الإيمان وذلك منتف عنه .
Chapter on the statement that God Almighty is willing for all creatures. If someone says: Why do you say that God Almighty wills obedience and disobedience and all events? It is said to him: Because God Almighty said in His Book: 'Doer of whatever He wills.' And the evidence has established that He is doer of all actions of creatures and all events, so it is necessary that He wills all of that. And by His statement: 'And if God had willed, they would not have done it,' and His statement: 'And if your Lord had willed, all who are on earth would have believed all together.' So He informed that if He had not intended from them disobedience, denial, and opposition, it would not have happened nor would they have done it; and that if He had willed that they all believe, they would have believed. If they say: What prevents you from considering that He intended that if He had willed that through coercion and compulsion, it would inevitably be? It is said to them: If it were possible that He intends belief from them voluntarily and it does not happen, and no incapacity or deficiency befalls Him in achieving His intention, then it would also be possible that He intends from them that through compulsion and coercion, and it does not happen, and no incapacity or deficiency befalls Him in achieving His intention. If they say: If what He intended from them through compulsion did not occur, that would indicate His incapacity to do a cause that would compel them to belief, such as intimidation and presenting punishment and other things, and incapacity is not permissible for Him. It is said to them: And if what He intended from their belief voluntarily and by choice did not occur, that would indicate His incapacity to do a kindness and a cause from causes by which they would choose belief when He does it, and that is negated from Him.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a chapter proving that God wills all actions of creatures. It begins with an objection: why do you say God wills obedience and disobedience? The answer is twofold: first, the Qur'an says 'Doer of whatever He wills' (فعال لما يريد), and second, evidence shows God creates all actions, so He must will them. Additionally, verses like 'And if God had willed, they would not have done it' and 'If your Lord had willed, all on earth would have believed' imply that if God had not willed disobedience or had willed universal belief, it would have occurred. Then a first objection: perhaps God's will is only conditional upon coercion? Baqillani responds with a parity argument: if voluntary will can fail without incapacity, then coercive will can also fail without incapacity; no distinction is possible. The second objection: if coercive will fails, that would indicate incapacity to compel. The answer is a parallel: if voluntary will fails, that would indicate incapacity to provide incentives (لطف), which is also impermissible for God. Therefore, both objections are invalid, and God's will for all things is established.

Key Terms
إكراه
إلجاء
عجز
لطف
طوعاً

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if God wills something, it must always happen immediately. Baqillani shows God can will something that does not occur without any deficiency.
Correction: God's will is not constrained by necessity of fulfillment; He may will something for a reason that does not materialize, but without any imperfection.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Baqillani's response to the first objection about coercion?
A) He says coercion is impossible for God.
B) He says voluntary will is more important.
C) He uses a parity argument: if voluntary will can fail, then coercive will can too.
D) He says the Quran proves coercion is not intended.

Key Takeaway
Objections that try to limit God's will to coercive scenarios or claim incapacity are refuted by showing they would equally apply to voluntary will, which the opponents accept.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation where God's decree seems difficult to accept, do you ever find yourself making objections similar to those raised here—demanding that God's will must always result in your expected outcome? How does Baqillani's symmetrical argument challenge that demand?
?
If you believe that God's will can fail to bring about certain events without deficiency, how should that reshape your attitude towards unanswered prayers or unfulfilled hopes? Are you willing to trust that His non-action is not due to incapacity but to a wisdom you may not see?
?
This passage shows that the same logic used to criticize God's will could be turned against your own assumptions. Where in your life do you apply inconsistent standards to God's actions versus human actions? Will you commit to examining those inconsistencies today?

Establishing God's Will Over All Human Actions
Baqillani states the proof and cites Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational and scriptural proof that God's will encompasses all human actions, including those of disbelief and obedience.

Core Idea
God's will is the determinant of every human action; nothing occurs outside His will.

Baqillani opens with a concise proof: since God is the agent of all events, He must will them. He then cites two Quranic verses showing that if God had not willed disbelief, it would not occur, and if He had willed universal belief, it would happen. This lesson unpacks this foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the first part of the passage where Baqillani states that the proof has established God as the doer of all actions and events, so He must will them.
Notice the logical sequence: agency implies will.
2
Examine the Quranic evidence
Read the two Quranic verses cited (Q. 6:137 and Q. 10:99) and understand how they demonstrate that God's will is decisive: if He had not willed disbelief, it would not occur; if He had willed universal belief, it would happen.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider the conclusion: God's will covers all human actions, both good and evil. This sets the stage for the objections that follow in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قام الدليل على أنه فعال لسائر أفعال الخلق وجميع الحوادث، فوجب أنه مريد لذلك أجمع. ولقوله تعالى: ولو شاء الله ما فعلوه وقوله: ولو شاء ربك لآمن من في الأرض كلهم جميعا فأخبر أنه لو لم يرد منهم العصيان والتكذيب والخلاف، لما كان ولا فعلوه؛ وأنه لو شاء أن يؤمنوا جميعاً، لأمنوا. فإن قالوا ما أنكرتم أن يكون إنما أراد أنه لو أراد ذلك على سبيل الإكراه والإلجاء، لكان لا محالة؟ قيل لهم: لو جاز أن يريد الإيمان منهم طوعاً فلا يكون ولا يلحقه [؟] عجز ولا تقصير عن بلوغ مراده، لجاز أن يريد الإيمان منهم على سبيل الإلجاء ولا يكون ولا يلحقه عجز ولا تقصير عن بلوغ مراده. فإن قالوا: لو لم يتم ما أراد منهم على سبيل الإلجاء، لدلّ ذلك على عجزه عن فعل سبب يلجئهم به إلى الإيمان، من ترهيب وإحضار نكال وغير ذلك والعجز غير جائز عليه. قيل لهم: ولو لم يتما ما أراده من إيمانهم طوعاً واختياراً، لــدل ذلك على عجزه عن فعل لطف وسبب من الأسباب يختارون عند فعله الإيمان وذلك منتف عنه. فإن قالوا: قد لا يكون في المعلوم شيء يؤمنون عنده، فلا يلحق العجز بفقد القدرة عليه. قيل لهم: وقد لا يكون في المعلوم شيء يلتجئون عند فعله إلى الإيمان به، وإن يقطعوا إربا إرباً وأنزل عليهم أعظم العذاب والنكال والآلام، بأن يعلم أنهم لا يختارون عند ذلك شيء من فعل الإيمان فلا يجب بنفي القدرة عليه إثبات عجز عنه. ولا جواب عن هذا.
The proof has been established that He is the doer of all actions of creation and all events, so it is necessary that He is the willer of all that. And according to His saying, 'And if Allah had willed, they would not have done it' and His saying, 'And if your Lord had willed, all on earth would have believed together,' so He informed that if He had not willed from them disobedience, denial, and opposition, they would not have occurred and they would not have done it; and that if He had willed that they all believe, they would have believed. If they say: What prevents that He only intended that if He had willed that by way of compulsion and forcing, it would inevitably be? Say to them: If it were permissible that He intends faith from them voluntarily and it does not occur, and no incapacity or shortcoming in achieving His intent attaches to Him, then it would be permissible that He intends faith from them by way of compulsion and forcing and it does not occur, and no incapacity or shortcoming in achieving His intent attaches to Him. If they say: If what He willed from them by way of compulsion were not accomplished, that would indicate His incapacity to create a cause that compels them to faith, such as terror, bringing a severe punishment, and other things, and incapacity is not permissible for Him. Say to them: And if what He willed from them voluntarily and by choice were not accomplished, that would indicate His incapacity to create a grace and a cause by which they would choose faith when it is created, and that is negated of Him. If they say: There may be no known thing at which they would believe, so incapacity is not incurred by lacking power over it. Say to them: There may be no known thing at which they would be compelled to faith by its creation, even if they were cut piece by piece and the greatest punishment and affliction and pains were sent down upon them, because it is known that they would not choose any act of faith at that time, so it is not necessary that negating power over it proves incapacity from it. There is no answer to this.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation of objections concerning God's will over human actions. The author begins by stating that evidence establishes God as the agent of all actions and events, so He must will all of them. He then cites Quranic verses (Q. 6:137? and Q. 10:99) to show that God's will is decisive: if He had not willed disbelief, it would not occur; if He had willed universal faith, it would happen. Following this, he addresses three objections:

1. First objection: The objector suggests that God's will to cause disbelief might be on the basis of compulsion (ikrah/ilja'), not voluntary. The answer: If voluntary faith can fail without implying incapacity, then forced faith can also fail without incapacity—so the objection proves nothing.
2. Second objection: If God's compulsion fails, that would show incapacity to create a compelling cause. The answer: Similarly, if God's voluntary will for faith fails, that would show incapacity to create a 'lutf' (grace) that leads to faith—but incapacity is impossible for God, so both are rejected.
3. Third objection: Perhaps there is no known condition under which they would believe, so not creating it is not incapacity. The answer: By the same token, there might be no condition that compels them, even if they are tortured, so not creating that condition does not imply incapacity.

The author concludes that the objector has no valid answer.

فعال
مريد
إرادة

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what follows from the fact that God is the doer (fāʿil) of all actions?
A) God must be all-powerful.
B) God must will all those actions.
C) God must know all actions.
D) God must create all actions.

Key Takeaway
God's will is all-encompassing; no action occurs without His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every action I take—even my sins—occurs only because Allah has willed it, how does that deepen my sense of accountability? Do I truly believe that, and how should that change the way I turn to Him in repentance?
?
When I read that Allah could have made everyone believe but chose not to, what does that tell me about the preciousness of my own faith? Does my faith make me more grateful, and do I live with the fear that it could be taken away if He wills?
?
If nothing in my life happens outside Allah's will, then every moment of disobedience I commit is a moment He willed. How does that humble me before His mercy, and what tangible change will I make in seeking His forgiveness today?

Refuting Objections to God's Will Over All Actions
Baqillani presents and refutes three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes three objections that try to limit God's will to only some actions.

Core Idea
Objections that separate God's will from evil actions collapse because they rely on the same assumptions as the rejected view.

Having established that God wills all actions, Baqillani anticipates three objections from those who might argue that God's will for evil actions would be by compulsion, that failure to compel implies incapacity, or that there is no condition for belief. This lesson walks through each objection and its powerful rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection and answer
Read the first 'If they say...' and the response. The objector suggests that God's will for disbelief might be by compulsion. Baqillani answers by showing symmetry: if voluntary will can fail without incapacity, so can compelled will.
Note the logical symmetry argument.
2
Read the second objection and answer
The second objection argues that if compelled will fails, that indicates incapacity to create a compelling cause. Baqillani counters by saying the same would apply to voluntary will failing to create a 'lutf' (grace).
3
Read the third objection and answer
The third objection says there might be no condition for belief. Baqillani replies that there might be no condition for compulsion either. Again, symmetry.
Observe the consistent pattern.
4
Understand the final conclusion
Baqillani concludes that there is no valid answer from the objector. The objections are self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Logical Proof That God Wills All Actions of Servants
Baqillani uses logical implication to show God must will all actions or else incapacity follows.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's logical proof that God must will all actions that occur in His dominion, and why any exception would imply incapacity or ignorance in God.

Core Idea
If God did not will an action in His dominion, He would lack power to achieve His will – impossible.

Imam Baqillani presents a crisp logical argument that God's will encompasses every action that occurs in His creation. He starts from the premise that God has absolute dominion over all that happens. Using a reductio ad absurdum, he shows that if any action in His dominion occurred without His will, that would imply a deficiency in God's power or knowledge. Let's trace his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Read the first sentence of the passage. What is Baqillani trying to prove?
The claim is that God wills all actions of servants.
2
Trace the conditional argument
Notice the condition: if there was an action in His dominion that He does not will, then what follows? This is a reductio ad absurdum.
The consequence is incapacity and shortcoming.
3
Observe the analogy between God's own actions and servants' actions
Baqillani says there is no difference. Why does he make this comparison?
This extends the argument to all actions, not just God's.
4
Extend the analogy to knowledge
The same logic applies to knowledge: if something occurs contrary to God's knowledge, that would imply ignorance.
This reinforces the argument that God's will and knowledge are comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على أن الله تعالى مريد لجميع أفعال العباد أنه لو كان في سلطانه منها ما ليس بمريد لكونه، للحقه العجز والتقصير عن بلوغ المراد. وكذلك لو أراد منها ما لم يكن كما أنه لو أراد من فعل نفسه ما لم يكن، أو كان منه عندنا وعندهم ما لم يرده، وهو مما يصح أن يكون مراداً لدلّ ذلك على عجزه وتقصيره وتعذر الأشياء عليه. فلا فرق في ذلك فعل نفسه وفعل خلقه الذي جعله لهم كسباً، كما أنه لا فرق بين أن يكون من فعل نفسه ما لا يعلم ولا يكون منه ما علم أنه يكون، وبين أن لا يكون خلقه ما يعلم أنه يكون، وبين أن يكون منهم ما علم أنه لا يكون، في إيجابه لتجهيله تعالى عن ذلك!
And among what indicates that Allah most high is willer of all actions of the servants is that if there were in His dominion something of them that He does not will to occur, He would be subject to incapacity and shortcoming in achieving His intent. Similarly, if He willed something of them that did not occur, just as if He willed from His own action what did not occur, or if there occurred from Him according to us and them what He did not will, while it is something that could be willed, that would indicate His incapacity, shortcoming, and the impossibility of things for Him. So there is no difference in that between His own action and the action of His creation which He made as an acquisition for them, just as there is no difference between that there be from His own action what He does not know and there not be from Him what He knows will be, and between that His creation not be what He knows will be, and between that there be from them what He knows will not be, in necessitating making Him ignorant—exalted is He above that!
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents a logical proof (qiyas) that God must will all actions of His servants. The author argues: If there were any action in God's dominion that He does not will to occur, that would imply incapacity and shortcoming—because God's will is effective. Likewise, if God wills something that does not occur, that also implies incapacity. This is the same for God's own actions and for the actions of creatures (which He creates as their acquisition, kasb). The author extends the analogy to God's knowledge: if something occurs contrary to God's knowledge, that would necessitate ignorance of God—which is impossible. Therefore, God must will all actions that occur, and no action occurs without His will. The proof is symmetrical: any disconnection between God's will and what happens would imply defect in His power or knowledge.

Key Terms
سلطان
عجز
تقصير
كسب
تجهيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will for His own actions is different from His will for creatures' actions, so the argument for His will is not universal.
Correction: Baqillani explicitly states there is no difference; the same logic applies to both. Any gap between will and occurrence implies incapacity, whether for God's own acts or creatures' acts.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani analogize to show that God must will all human actions?
A) The occurrence of accidents in the world.
B) God's own actions and knowledge.
C) The actions of angels.
D) The actions of jinn.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani proves that God must will every action in His dominion, because any exception would require incapacity or ignorance, which are impossible for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every single action you do, including your sins, occurs only because Allah wills it? How does this change the way you view your own shortcomings and your need for His mercy?
?
If you truly internalize that Allah wills all your actions, does that make you more humble and reliant on Him, or do you feel a tension with personal responsibility? How will you reconcile these in your worship today?
?
When you disobey Allah, do you think of it as something that happened outside His will? Reflect on the fact that even your disobedience is within His will—what does that demand from your repentance and your trust in His wisdom?

Objection and Answer on Incapacity in Absence of Belief
Baqillani responds to an objection about incapacity not proven by lack of power

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Baqillani argues that the absence of belief does not prove God's incapacity, and how divine foreknowledge of choice resolves the objection.

Core Idea
Lack of power to believe does not prove incapacity; it reflects God's knowledge of free choice.

The objectors claim that if God has not decreed belief for some people, their lack of faith cannot be attributed to His inability to give them power. Baqillani responds by turning the logic around: even if they were subjected to extreme torture, they still would not choose faith, because God knows they will never believe. Therefore, negating power does not establish incapacity; it simply aligns with God's foreknowledge. This short passage is a powerful defense of divine omniscience and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first sentence: the objectors argue that incapacity is not proven by lack of power because there may be nothing in God's knowledge that would make them believe.
Notice the phrase 'فقد لا يكون في المعلوم شيء' — 'there may not be in the known anything'.
2
Read Baqillani's response
Read the second part: Baqillani flips the argument by saying that even under extreme duress, they would not believe because God knows they will not choose faith.
The key is 'بأن يعلم أنهم لا يختارون' — 'because it is known that they will not choose'.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion is that negating power does not entail incapacity; it simply reflects divine foreknowledge. There is no answer to this.
The final phrase 'ولا جواب عن هذا' emphasizes the strength of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : قد لا يكون في المعلوم شيء يؤمنون عنده، فلا يلحق العجز بفقد القدرة عليه. قيل لهم : وقد لا يكون في المعلوم شيء يلتجئون عند فعله إلى الإيمان به، وإن يقطعوا إربا إربا وأنزل عليهم أعظم العذاب والنكال والآلام، بأن يعلم أنهم لا يختارون عند ذلك شيء من فعل الإيمان فلا يجب بنفي القدرة عليه إثبات عجز عنه. ولا جواب عن هذا.
If they say: There may not be in the known anything at which they would believe, so incapacity is not incurred by lack of power over it. Say to them: And there may not be in the known anything to which they would resort, upon its occurrence, to belief, even if they are cut limb from limb and the greatest torment, punishment, and pains are sent down upon them, because it is known that they will not choose at that time any act of faith. So it is not necessary, by negating power over it, to establish incapacity concerning it. And there is no answer to this.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objectors argue that if there is nothing in God's knowledge that would cause disbelievers to believe, then their lack of faith is not due to incapacity (since they are not given the power to believe). Baqillani responds that the same logic applies: even if they were subjected to extreme torture, they still would not believe because God knows they will not choose faith. Therefore, negating power over faith does not prove incapacity; it simply aligns with God's knowledge that they will not believe. The argument ends by stating there is no valid response to this.

Key Terms
قدرة (power)
عجز (incapacity)
إيمان (faith)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God does not give power to believe, then He is incapable of making them believe.
Correction: Baqillani shows that lack of power is not due to incapacity but because God knows they will not choose faith. The absence of power does not prove inability, only divine foreknowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani's response, why does the absence of belief not prove God's incapacity?
A) Because God lacks power to force belief
B) Because torturing disbelievers would make them believe
C) Because God knows they will not choose faith even under extreme duress
D) Because disbelievers have power to believe themselves

Key Takeaway
Even if God withholds power to believe, it does not imply incapacity; it reflects His knowledge that they will never choose faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that some people would not believe even under extreme torture, does it make you question the resilience of your own faith? What would it take for you to abandon your beliefs, and what does that reveal about the true foundation of your conviction? Take a moment to pray for steadfastness.
?
The passage teaches that God's withholding of power to believe does not mean He is incapable—it reflects His knowledge of human choice. How does this shift your understanding of your own faith? Do you see your belief as a result of your own effort or as a gift from God? Let this realization deepen your gratitude and reliance on Him.

Proof of God's Universal Will Over Human Actions
Baqillani proves that God wills all human actions using an argument from incapacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that God must will all human actions, based on the impossibility of incapacity or failure in Him.

Core Idea
God's will must match all actual events; otherwise, He would be incapable.

Imam Baqillani presents a logical proof that God's will encompasses every action performed by His servants. Any action that occurs in His dominion must be willed by Him, because if it were not, that would imply a deficiency in His power. This passage uses a reductio ad absurdum to demonstrate the necessity of God's universal will.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the strong conditional structure: 'if... then...'
The passage is dense; read it twice.
2
Identify the two conditional scenarios
Look for the two main hypotheticals: (1) an action in God's dominion that He does not will, and (2) an action He wills that does not occur. Each leads to an absurd consequence.
Pay attention to the parallel phrasing.
3
Notice the extension to human actions and knowledge
The author equates God's own actions with actions of His creation (which He calls 'kasb'), and then draws a parallel between will and knowledge to reinforce the argument.
This shows the argument is comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يدل على أن الله تعالى مريد لجميع أفعال العباد أنه لو كان في سلطانه منها ما ليس بمريد لكونه، للحقه العجز والتقصير عن بلوغ المراد. وكذلك لو أراد منها ما لم يكن كما أنه لو أراد من فعل نفسه ما لم يكن، أو كان منه عندنا وعندهم ما لم يرده، وهو مما يصح أن يكون مراداً لدلّ ذلك على عجزه وتقصيره وتعذر الأشياء عليه. فلا فرق في ذلك فعل نفسه وفعل خلقه الذي جعله لهم كسباً، كما أنه لا فرق بين أن يكون من فعل نفسه ما لا يعلم ولا يكون منه ما علم أنه يكون، وبين أن لا يكون خلقه ما يعلم أنه يكون، وبين أن يكون منهم ما علم أنه لا يكون، في إيجابه لتجهيله تعالى عن ذلك!
And among what indicates that God, exalted, wills all actions of servants is that if there were in His dominion something among them that He does not will to occur, then incapacity and failure would attach to Him in attaining what is willed. Likewise, if He willed something among them that did not occur, just as if He willed from His own action what did not occur, or if there occurred from Him, in our view and their view, what He did not will, while it is possible to be willed, that would indicate His incapacity, failure, and the impossibility of things for Him. So there is no difference in this between His own action and the action of His creation which He made for them as acquisition (kasb), just as there is no difference between there being from His own action what He does not know and there not occurring what He knows will occur, and between there not occurring from His creation what He knows will occur, and between there occurring from them what He knows will not occur, in necessitating regarding Him ignorance—exalted is He above that!
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) for God's universal will (irada) over all actions of servants. The argument is based on reductio ad absurdum: if there were any action in God's dominion that He does not will, that would imply a defect (incapacity or failure) in Him because He would be unable to bring about His will. Similarly, if He willed something that did not occur, that would also imply incapacity. The author equates this to the same logic for God's own actions and for human actions, noting that human actions are called 'acquisition' (kasb). He also parallels will with knowledge: just as it would be absurd for God not to know something that occurs or to know something that does not occur, so it is absurd for His will to be inconsistent with what occurs. The conclusion is that God must will all actions of servants.

Key Terms
إرادة (will)
عجز (incapacity)
كسب (acquisition)
تقصير (failure)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof implies that humans have no free will or moral responsibility.
Correction: Baqillani uses the term 'kasb' (acquisition) to maintain human responsibility: actions are created by God but acquired by humans. The argument only establishes that God's will encompasses all actions, not that humans are mere puppets.

Checkpoint
What would be implied if there were an action in God's dominion that He does not will, according to Baqillani?
A) It would show God's mercy
B) It would indicate God's incapacity and failure
C) It would prove human free will
D) It would be impossible by definition

Key Takeaway
Baqillani proves that God's will is inseparable from His sovereignty over all actions, including human actions, through a reductio argument defending divine perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills every action you take, does that change how you view your past sins? Are you still holding onto the illusion that you could have acted outside His will? What would it mean to truly accept His complete sovereignty over your choices—and yet still strive to obey Him?
?
This passage argues that any gap between God's will and reality would imply His incapacity. Are there moments in your life when you resent what God has willed for you? What hidden claim of independence are you protecting by not surrendering to His decree?
?
Baqillani uses the example of God's knowledge to prove His will must match reality. If you truly believed that God knows and wills every detail of your life, how would your anxiety about the future dissolve? What specific fear can you entrust to Him today?

Refuting the Claim of Incapacity from Uncommanded Actions
Baqillani refutes the claim that uncommanded actions imply divine incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes the objection that actions not commanded by God would imply His incapacity, using consensus and a logical argument about will and command.

Core Idea
Uncommanded actions do not imply incapacity; God's own actions prove this.

In this passage, Baqillani confronts a subtle objection: if God's servants do something He did not command, does that show God is unable to prevent it? He responds with two powerful arguments, one based on unanimous agreement (ittifaq) and another on the nature of divine will. This lesson unpacks his refutation and clarifies a key distinction between God's command and His will.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection and Response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Identify the objector's claim and then Baqillani's immediate reply.
Notice that Baqillani begins with 'Say to them'—a direct refutation.
2
Analyze the First Argument: Consensus
Baqillani says the objection is invalid by consensus (ittifaq). He gives the example of most of God's own actions never being commanded to anyone, yet no incapacity follows.
The consensus among Muslims is that God's actions are not subject to command.
3
Analyze the Second Argument: Logical Distinction
Baqillani then presents a conditional: if something commanded does not happen and something not commanded happens, while God wills the latter and does not will the former, no incapacity attaches. Incapacity only applies when a commander wills what he commands and is disobeyed.
This argument relies on the earlier proof that God wills all that occurs, so His will is always fulfilled.
4
Reflect on the Key Distinction
Consider the difference between God's command (amr) and His will (irada). The objection conflates them, but Baqillani separates them to show that uncommanded actions do not challenge God's power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فيجب إذا كان من خلقه ما لم يأمرهم به، أن يدل ذلك على عجزه . قيل لهم : هذا ساقط باتفاق، لأنه قد كانت أكثر أفعاله ولم يأمر بها أحداً خلقه، ولم يلحقه العجز فكذلك حكم فعل خلقه له. ولأنه، إذا لم يكن ما أمر بكونه وكان ما لم يأمر به، وهو تعالى مريد لكون ما لم يأمر به وغير مريد لما أمرهم به، لم يلحقه عجز ولا تقصير. لأن ذلك إنما يلحق من خولف في أمره إذا أراد ما أمر به وكره ما نهى عنه. فسقط ما سألتم عنه.
If they say: Then if there occurs from His creation what He did not command them, that would indicate His incapacity. Say to them: This is invalid by consensus, because most of His actions occurred and He did not command anyone to do them, yet no incapacity attached to Him. So likewise is the ruling of the action of His creation for Him. And because, when what He commanded to occur did not occur, and what He did not command occurred, while He, exalted, wills the occurrence of what He did not command and does not will what He commanded them, no incapacity or failure attaches to Him. For that only attaches to one who is disobeyed in his command if he wills what he commanded and dislikes what he forbade. So what you asked about falls away.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is another objection and answer. The objectors argue that if God's servants do something He did not command, that would indicate His incapacity (because He could not prevent it). Baqillani responds that this is baseless by unanimous agreement (ittifaq). He points out that many of God's own actions (e.g., creating the universe) were not commanded to anyone, yet this does not imply incapacity. Similarly, human actions are under God's dominion. Moreover, when God commands something and it does not happen, or something He did not command happens, while He wills the latter and does not will the former, no incapacity follows because incapacity only applies to one who is disobeyed when he wills what he commands and dislikes what he forbids. Since God does not will the commanded action in this case (as per the earlier proof that He wills all that occurs), there is no contradiction. Thus the objection fails.

Key Terms
أمر (command)
عجز (incapacity)
اتفاق (consensus)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector assumes that God's command and His will are identical, so uncommanded actions contradict His will and imply incapacity.
Correction: Baqillani shows that God's will and command are distinct. God wills all that occurs, but His command is a normative directive. An uncommanded action can still be willed by God, so no incapacity follows.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani say the objection about actions not commanded is invalid?
A) Because most of God's own actions were not commanded to anyone
B) Because God always commands all actions
C) Because human actions are not under God's dominion
D) Because command and will are identical

Key Takeaway
Uncommanded actions do not imply divine incapacity, as shown by consensus and the logical separation of God's command from His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the distinction between God's command and His will affect your understanding of why evil exists in the world? Do you truly believe that everything that happens is willed by God, even when it contradicts His commands? How does this challenge your heart to trust God's wisdom?
?
This lesson argues that uncommanded actions do not indicate incapacity. Think of a situation in your life where you felt God was unable to control events because He did not command them. How does this teaching correct your assumption and call you to submit to God's comprehensive will, even when you don't understand?
?
Baqillani uses consensus to refute the objection. In your own faith journey, do you rely on the consensus of the scholars or do you let doubts creep in when faced with seemingly contradictory events? What step can you take today to strengthen your reliance on the transmitted knowledge?

Command and Will: The Sacrifice of Abraham
Baqillani uses the sacrifice of Abraham to explain how God can command what He does not will

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the story of Abraham to argue that God can command what He does not will while remaining wise.

Core Idea
God commands obedience, not the act itself.

This lesson addresses a key objection against divine wisdom: how can God command something He does not will? Baqillani turns to the Quranic story of Abraham and Ishmael to show that command and will are distinct. The passage refutes alternative interpretations that would undermine the test of faith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the opening objection: 'How can He command what He does not will and be wise?' Recognize the challenge to God's wisdom.
2
Examine Baqillani's answer
Read how Baqillani cites the Quranic story of Abraham and Ishmael. Notice that God commanded the sacrifice but did not will the actual act, as He forbade it and provided a ransom.
The distinction between command and will is key.
3
Analyze the hypotheticals
Baqillani considers three alternative scenarios and shows they are incoherent. Identify each one: actual sacrifice, only laying down, or turning the neck to copper. Understand why each fails.
These strengthen the main argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قالوا : فكيف يكون أمراً بما لا يريده ويكون بذلك حكيماً؟ قيل لهم : هذا مما قد ورد به القرآن واتفق عليه سلف الأمة. لأن الله تعالى أمر إبراهيم بذبح إسماعيل، عليهما السلام، ولم يرد ذلك منه، بل نهاه عنه بعد أمره به وفداه مما أمره بفعله من ذبحه. ولو كان قد فعل الذبح، لم يكن لافتدائه معنى. ولو كان إنما أمره بالإضجاع وإمرار السكين فقط دون الذبح، لم يكن ذلك امتحاناً منه ولم يكن لقوله: إن هذا لهو البلاء المبين معنى ولا كان لافتدائه من إضجاع قد وقع معنى. وكذلك لو ذبحه ثم التحم، لم يكن للفداء معنى ولا لبلائه معنى. وكذلك لو كان قد منعه من ذبحه بقلب صفحة عنقه نحاساً، يقوله بعض جهالهم، لكان عندهم بذلك سفيهاً ومكلفاً للفعل مع العجز عنه والمنع منه وذلك عندهم باطل.
If they say: How can He command what He does not will and be wise thereby? Say to them: This is something that has been conveyed by the Quran and agreed upon by the predecessors of the community. For God, exalted, commanded Abraham to sacrifice Ishmael, peace be upon them, and He did not will that from him, but rather forbade him from it after commanding him and ransomed him from what He commanded him to do of his sacrifice. Had he actually performed the sacrifice, there would be no meaning to his ransom. And if He had only commanded him to lay down and pass the knife without actually sacrificing, that would not have been a test from Him, nor would His words 'Indeed, this is the clear trial' have any meaning, nor would His ransom from a laying down that had occurred have any meaning. Likewise, if he had sacrificed him and then he healed, there would be no meaning to the ransom nor to his trial. And likewise, if He had prevented him from sacrificing by turning the side of his neck into copper, as some ignorant among them claim, then in their view He would be foolish and one who imposes an action while being incapable of it and preventing it, and that is false in their view.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is an objection and answer. The objectors ask how God can command what He does not will and still be wise. Baqillani responds by citing the Quranic story of Abraham and Ishmael. God commanded Abraham to sacrifice his son, but did not will the actual sacrifice; instead He stopped him and provided a ransom. The author argues that if the sacrifice had actually occurred, the ransom would be meaningless. If the command was merely to lay Ishmael down and pass the knife without actually sacrificing, then it would not be a real test (since Abraham would not actually be sacrificing). If the sacrifice occurred but Ishmael was healed, again the ransom would be meaningless. If God had prevented the sacrifice by turning his neck into copper (a fanciful idea attributed to some ignorant people), then God would be considered foolish and imposing an impossible task. Thus, the only coherent interpretation is that God commanded the sacrifice, willed the intention and obedience, but did not will the actual physical act of sacrifice. This shows that command and will are distinct, and wisdom is maintained.

Key Terms
أمر (command)
إرادة (will)
حكمة (wisdom)
ذبح (sacrifice)
فداء (ransom)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God's command always entails His will, so commanding what He does not will is impossible.
Correction: Baqillani shows with the story of Abraham that God can command a test of obedience without willing the actual physical act. The command and will are distinct; wisdom is maintained.

Checkpoint
Why does Baqillani argue that God's command to Abraham to sacrifice his son does not contradict wisdom?
A) Because Abraham actually performed the sacrifice
B) Because God willed the sacrifice but changed His mind
C) Because God commanded the intention and obedience, not the actual act of sacrifice
D) Because God prevented the sacrifice by turning the neck into copper

Key Takeaway
God's command and His will are distinct; the story of Abraham demonstrates that wisdom is preserved when God commands a test of obedience without willing the physical act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When God commands you to do something difficult, do you assume that He must also will the outcome to be exactly as you imagine? How does Abraham's story challenge that assumption, and will you today surrender your need to understand the 'why' and simply obey?
?
Has there been a time you obeyed God outwardly but inwardly resented the command, questioning His wisdom? How does the distinction between command and will free you to trust that God's wisdom remains even when His will seems to contradict your expectations?
?
Are there acts of worship you perform mechanically, focusing only on the physical action, while neglecting the heart's intention? Will you, like Abraham, consciously offer your obedience as a test of your commitment, regardless of whether you see the desired result?

6 min
God's Command and Will: The Test of Abraham
Baqillani answers the objection that commanding what is not willed contradicts wisdom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani defends the coherence of God commanding what He does not will, using the example of Abraham and Ishmael.

Core Idea
God can command a test without willing its completion.

This passage addresses a common objection: if God commands something, must He will it? Baqillani argues that the story of Abraham and the near-sacrifice of Ishmael shows that God’s command can be a test, not an expression of His will for the act. By analyzing the event, he demonstrates that God’s wisdom is preserved.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Get a sense of the flow of the objection and answer.
2
Identify the objection and initial response
The objection is: How can God command what He does not will and still be wise? Baqillani's initial response grounds the answer in Quran and consensus—not pure reason alone.
Notice that Baqillani starts with revelation, not philosophy.
3
Analyze the Abraham story
Baqillani uses the story to show that the command was a test, not a willing of the act. He then refutes alternative interpretations that would undermine the test's meaning.
Pay attention to the three hypothetical scenarios he rejects.
4
Understand the refutation of alternate views
Baqillani considers and dismisses the ideas that God only commanded to lie down, that slaughter occurred then healed, or that God prevented by turning the neck to copper. Each would invalidate the test and imply foolishness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
فإن قالوا : فكيف يكون أمراً بما لا يريده ويكون بذلك حكيماً؟
قيل لهم : هذا مما قد ورد به القرآن واتفق عليه سلف الأمة. لأن الله تعالى أمر إبراهيم بذبح إسماعيل، عليهما السلام، ولم يُرد ذلك منه، بل نهاه عنه بعد أمره به وفداه مما أمره بفعله من ذبحه. ولو كان قد فعل الذبح، لم يكن لافتدائه معنى. ولو كان إنما أمره بالإضجاع وإمرار السكين فقط دون الذبح، لم يكن ذلك امتحاناً منه ولم يكن لقوله : إن هذا لهو البلاء المبين معنى ولا كان لافتدائه من إضجاع قد وقع معنى. وكذلك لو ذبحه ثم التحم، لم يكن للفداء معنى ولا لبلائه معنى. وكذلك لو كان قد منعه من ذبحه بقلب صفحة عنقه نحاساً، يقوله بعض جهالهم، لكان عندهم بذلك سفيهاً ومكلّفاً للفعل مع العجز عنه والمنع منه وذلك عندهم باطل.
Issue: If they say: 'How can He command what He does not will and yet be wise?' Say to them: This is something that has been mentioned in the Quran and upon which the predecessors of the community agreed. Because God commanded Abraham to slaughter Ishmael, peace be upon them, and He did not will that from him; rather, He forbade him from it after He commanded him and ransomed him from what He had commanded him to do, i.e., his slaughter. And if he had performed the slaughter, there would be no meaning to his ransom. And if He had only commanded him to lie down and pass the knife without the slaughter, that would not have been a test from Him, and there would be no meaning to His saying: 'Indeed, this was a manifest trial' (Quran 37:106), nor would there be a meaning to his ransom from a lying down that had occurred. And likewise, if he had slaughtered him and then the flesh healed, there would be no meaning to the ransom nor to his trial. And likewise, if He had prevented him from slaughtering him by turning the side of his neck into copper — as some of their ignoramuses say — then according to them He would be foolish and imposing an act while being incapable of it and preventing it, and that is false according to them.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objection states: How can God command something He does not will and still be considered wise? The answer begins by grounding itself in revelation and consensus: the Quran and the early community affirm this. Baqillani then provides a detailed theological analysis of the story of Abraham and the near-sacrifice of Ishmael. God commanded Abraham to slaughter his son, but He did not will that the slaughter actually occur; instead, He forbade it and provided a ransom. If the slaughter had taken place, the ransom would be meaningless. If the command was only to put the knife to the neck without slaughter, it would not be a genuine trial, and the Quranic verse calling it a 'manifest trial' would lose meaning. Similarly, if the slaughter had occurred and then the wound healed, the ransom would be pointless. Finally, Baqillani rejects the absurd suggestion that God prevented the act by turning Ishmael's neck to copper, calling it the view of ignoramuses, which would imply God is foolish and imposes impossible tasks.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God must will whatever He commands.
Correction: God may command something as a test without willing its actual occurrence, as shown in the story of Abraham.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, why did God command Abraham to slaughter Ishmael if He did not will the slaughter to occur?
A) To test Abraham's obedience while withholding His will for the act.
B) Because God changed His mind after the command.
C) To demonstrate that God can command impossible acts.
D) To show that God's will is irrelevant to His commands.

Key Takeaway
God's command is not always an expression of His will; it can serve a testing purpose, and His wisdom is not compromised.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When God commands you to do something that seems impossible or against your desires, are you willing to obey even if you do not see how it will end? What does Abraham's example demand from you right now in a specific situation where you are resisting obedience because you fear the outcome?
?
Has there been a command from God that you rationalized away because you assumed that if God really wanted it, He would make it easy or guarantee success? How does this lesson expose the flaw in that assumption, and what will you commit to changing today?
?
You believe God is wise, but do you trust His wisdom even when His commands seem pointless or contradictory to His will? Where in your life are you demanding that God explain Himself before you obey, and when will you choose obedience as Abraham did?
